Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Mahr of Khamr and Khinzir in Dhimmī Marriage with Subsequent Conversion
Ruling for mahr of khamr/khinzir in dhimmī marriage with conversion before receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr when a dhimmī couple specifies khamr or khinzir and then converts, distinguish between specified and unspecified dower, and identify the positions of Abū Ḥanīfah and the Sāḥibayn.

Core Idea
For specified dower: she takes the same item; for unspecified: value for mithlī, mahr al-mithl for qimī.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a dhimmī (non-Muslim subject) couple marries with a dower consisting of wine (khamr) or pigs (khinzir)—items not considered property in Islamic law. The question is what happens if they convert to Islam before the dower is handed over. The Ḥanafī school provides a detailed ruling that distinguishes between specified and unspecified dower, and between mithlī (fungible) and qimī (non-fungible) items. This lesson unpacks the ruling of Imām Abū Ḥanīfah and the disagreement of his two leading disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core question
We have a dhimmī couple. The husband names khamr or khinzir as mahr. Before receipt, one or both spouses convert to Islam. The question: what is the wife entitled to?
The marriage itself is valid; only the mahr is at issue.
2
Distinguish specified (ʿayn) from unspecified (ghayr ʿayn) dower
If the dower is a specific, identified item (e.g., this bottle of wine), ownership is perfected by the contract. If it is described by kind only (e.g., 'a bottle of wine'), receipt is needed to fix the specific item.
This distinction is unique to Abū Ḥanīfah.
3
Apply the mithlī / qimī classification for unspecified dower
Khamr is considered mithlī (fungible, measured by volume) so she gets its value. Khinzir is qimī (non-fungible) so she gets mahr al-mithl (the typical dower for a woman of her status).
For specified dower, this classification is irrelevant: she takes the specific item regardless.
4
Compare the positions of the three imāms
Abū Ḥanīfah: above. Abū Yūsuf: mahr al-mithl in all cases. Muḥammad: value in all cases. The text gives their reasoning based on the effect of receipt and the analogy to destruction of the subject matter.
The mufta bihi is Abū Ḥanīfah's view.

Source Text

كتاب النكاح باب المهر بخمر أو خنزير عين فأسلما أو أسلم أحدهما لها الخمر والخنزير وفي غير العين لها قيمة الخمر ومهر المثل في الخنزير. بين نفيه والسكوت عنه كما في الهداية وفي فتح القدير: إن ظاهر الرواية وجوب مهر المثل عنده إذا سكتا عنه مخالفاً لما في الهداية لأن النكاح معاوضة فما لم ينص على نفيه يكون مستحقاً لها. والواو في قوله وذا جائز للحال وقوله فلا مهر» جواب المسألة، وضبط في غاية البيان إلا من أربى أنه حرف التنبيه لا استثناء. وقيد المصنف بالمهر لأن بقية أحكام النكاح ثابتة في حقهم كالمسلمين من وجوب النفقة في النكاح ووقوع الطلاق والعدة والتوارث بالنكاح الصحيح كالنسب وثبوت خيار البلوغ وحرمة نكاح المحارم والمطلقة ثلاثاً كما في التبيين، وظاهره أنه متفق عليه وأما الكفاءة ففي الخانية أن الذمية إذا زوجت نفسها رجلاً لم يكن لوليها حق الفسخ إلا أن يكون أمراً ظاهراً بأن زوجت بنت ملكهم أو حبرهم نفسها كناساً أو دباغاً منهم أو نقصت من مهرها نقصاناً فاحشاً كان لأوليائها أن يطالبوه بالتبليغ إلى تمام مهر المثل أو يفسخ .اهـ. وفائدة عدم المهر في هذه المسائل أنهما لو أسلما أو أحدهما أو ترافعا أو أحدهما إلينا لا نحكم به، ومسألة خطاب الكفار وتفاصيلها أصولية لم تذكر عن أبي حنيفة وأصحابة وإنما هي مستنبطة وتمامه في كتابنا المسمى بلب الأصول . قوله: (ولو تزوج ذمي ذمية بخمر أو خنزير عين فأسلما أو أسلم أحدهما لها الخمر والخنزير وفي غير العين لها قيمة الخمر ومهر المثل في الخنزير) بيان لما إذا سميا ما مال هو عندهما وليس بمال عندنا وحاصله أن التسمية صحيحة ولها المسمى فإن قبضته صح، وإن لم تقبضه حتى أسلما أو أسلم أحدهما فهو على وجهين : إما أن يكون ذلك المسمى معيناً أو غير معين. فإن كان معيناً فليس لها إلا هو قيمياً كان أو متلياً، وإن كان غير معين فلها القيمة في المثلي ومهر المثل في القيمي. وهذه كله عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف : لها مهر المثل في الوجهين. وقال محمد لها القيمة في الوجهين وجه قولهما أن القبض مؤكد للملك في المقبوض فيكون له شبه بالعقد فيمتنع بسبب الإسلام كالعقد وصار كما إذا كانا بغير أعيانهما، وأما إذا التحقت حالة القبض بحالة العقد فأبو يوسف يقول لو كانا مسلمين وقت العقد يجب مهر المثل فكذا هنا ومحمد يقول صحت التسمية لكون المسمى مالاً معزياً إلى شرح الوهبانية وكذا ولي الصغيرة ولا يخفى شموله الجد وغيره قوله (سهو) قال في النهر : ليس كما قال بل أراد أنه بيان الحكم أنكحتهم ولا شك أن المهر من أحكامه، قوله (كما الهداية نبه في الهداية على أن هذا الخلاف في الميتة أيضاً فقال : وقد قيل في الميتة والسكوت روايتان والأصح أن الكل على الخلاف وجعل في الفتح ظاهر الرواية وجوب مهر المثل فيهما وقال : وجه الظاهر أن النكاح معاوضة فما لم ينص على نفي العوض يكون مستحقاً لها والميتة عندهم إلا أنه امتنع التسليم للإسلام فيجب القيمة كما إذا هلك العبد المسمى قبل القبض، ولأبي حنيفة أن الملك في الصداق المعين يتم بنفس العقد لهذا يملك التصرف فيه وبالقبض ينتقل من ضمان الزوج إلى ضمانها وذلك لا يمتنع بالإسلام كاسترداد الخمر المغصوب، وفي غير المعين القبض موجب ملك العين فيمتنع بالإسلام بخلاف المشتري لأن ملك التصرف إنما يستفاد فيه بالقبض، وإذا تعذر القبض في غير المعين لا تجب القيمة في الخنزير لأنه من ذوات القيم فيكون أخذ قيمته كأخذ عينه ولا كذلك الخمر لأنه من ذوات الأمثال ، ألا ترى أنه لو جاء بالقيمة قبل الإسلام تجبر على القبول في الخنزير دون الخمر. ولو طلقها قبل الدخول بها فمن أوجب مهر المثل أوجب المتعة، ومن أوجب القيمة أوجب نصفها. وفي الغاية ويرد على هذا ما لو اشترى ذمي داراً من ذمي بخمر أو خنزير وشفيعها مسلم يأخذ بالشفعة بقيمة الخمر والخنزير فلم تجعل قيمة الخنزير كعينه ولم يجب عنه بشيء. وأجاب عنه في التبيين أن قيمة الخنزير إنما تكون كعينه أن لو كان بدلاً . مسألة الخنزير كما في النكاح، أما لو كان بدلاً عن غيره فلا. وفي مسألة الشفعة قيمة الخنزير بدل عن الدار المشفوعة وإنما صير إليها للتقدير بها لا غير فلا يكون لها حكم عينه. وأفاد بقوله لها في المعين المسمى أنه لو كان طلقها قبل الدخول فإن لها نصفه والله تعالى أعلم . عن كالسكوت لأنها ليست مالاً عندهم فذكرها لغو وصحح المصنف أن الكل على الخلاف وهو خلاف الظاهر . قوله (وفي مسألة الشفعة (الخ قال في الحواشي السعدية : ولك أن تقول كذلك فيما نحن فيه بدل عن البضع وإنما صير إليه للتقدير بها فليتأمل فجوابه يظهر من تقرير قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير. قال في النهر : وأقول لا نسلم أنها هنا بدل عن منافع البضع إذ منافعه إنما قوبلت بالخنزير وبالإسلام تعذر أخذ القيمة لما فصير إلى مهر المثل اهـ قلت والذي قرره قاضيخان هو قوله : ولأن قيمة الخنزير لها حكم عين الخنزير ولهذا لو أتاها بقيمة الخنزير قبل الإسلام أجبرت على القبول فكان وجوب قيمة الخنزير من موجبات تلك التسمية والإسلام يقرر حكم التسمية فإنما يستوفي بعد الإسلام ما ليس من موجبات تلك التسمية وهو مهر المثل، أما قيمة الخمر ليست من موجباتها فتستوفى بعد الإسلام .اهـ والذي يظهر من هذا التقرير أن الجواب يؤخذ من قوله إن قيمة الخنزير لها حكم عينه وأنها من موجبات التسمية ففيه منع لكون المصير إليها للتقدير بها بخلاف مسألة الشفعة فإن القيمة فيها ليست من موجبات التسمية وحينئذ فمناط الفرق هذا تأمل. وعليك بالتأمل في جواب النهر، ويمكن أن يكون هذا مراده وارجع على مامر في باب العاشر آخر الزكاة عند قوله عشر الخمر لا الخنزير.
Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. If he marries with specified khamr or khinzir and both or one of them accepts Islam, she takes the khamr and khinzir. If unspecified, she takes the value of the khamr and the mahr of the like for the khinzir. The author explains that the naming is valid and she takes the named item; if she has taken it, it is valid. If she has not taken it until they become Muslim or one of them becomes Muslim, there are two scenarios: either the named item is specified (ʿayn) or unspecified. If specified, she has only that item, whether it is qimī or mithlī. If unspecified, she takes the value for mithlī and mahr al-mithl for qimī. This is Abu Hanifa's view. Abu Yusuf says mahr al-mithl in both cases. Muhammad says value in both cases. Their reasoning: receipt confirms ownership in the received item, so it resembles the contract and is prevented by Islam like the contract...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses a specific scenario in the law of mahr (dower) involving non-Muslim subjects (dhimmīs) who marry each other but specify a dower consisting of wine (khamr) or pigs (khinzir) – items that are not considered property (māl) in Islamic law. The ruling is part of the broader discussion that the marriage of dhimmīs is valid and carries the same consequences as Muslim marriage, except where specified by the law. The key issue is what happens if the spouses (or one of them) embrace Islam before the dower is handed over. The author explains that if the named dower is a specific, identified item (ʿayn), then despite conversion, she is only entitled to that same item – whether it is qimī (a thing evaluated by likeness) or mithlī (a thing measured by weight or volume). If the dower was not specified (ghayr ʿayn), meaning it was described by kind only, then the rule changes: for mithlī items (like wine, which is considered mithlī because it can be measured or weighed) she gets the market value (qīmah), but for qimī items (like pigs, which are unique and not fungible) she gets mahr al-mithl (the dower typical for women of her status). This is the position of Imām Abū Ḥanīfah. His two leading disciples differ: Abū Yūsuf holds that mahr al-mithl is due in both cases (ʿayn and ghayr ʿayn), while Muḥammad holds that value is due in both cases. The text then provides the reasoning behind each view. Abū Ḥanīfah argues that ownership of a specified dower is perfected by the contract itself (the wife can dispose of it), and receipt merely transfers liability from the husband to the wife; this transfer is not prevented by Islam, just as the recovery of a stolen wine is allowed. For unspecified dower, receipt itself gives ownership of the specific item, and that is prevented by Islam, so she cannot take the physical item; instead, she takes value for mithlī and mahr al-mithl for qimī. The passage also compares this to the case of a Muslim taking pre-emption (shufʿah) over a house bought by a dhimmī with wine or a pig, and explains why in that case the value of the pig is treated differently. The author concludes by affirming the distinction and urging careful study.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bride-gift given by the husband to the wife as a mandatory part of the marriage contract.
خمر (khamr): Wine; an intoxicating beverage made from grapes, considered impure and not property (māl) in Islamic law.
خنزير (khinzīr): Pig; an animal considered impure and not property in Islamic law, but treated as qimī (non-fungible) in this context.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower of a woman of equivalent status; the standard dower for her social standing, used when no specific amount is named or when the specified dower is invalid.
قيمي (qimī): A thing that is not measured by weight, volume, or count; a unique item that is valued by estimation.
مثلي (mithlī): A fungible item that is measured by weight, volume, or count; its like is readily available in the market.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfah, if a dhimmī marries a dhimmī with a specified (ʿayn) bottle of wine (khamr) as mahr and then the husband converts to Islam before handing it over, what is the wife entitled to?
A) The same bottle of wine
B) The market value of the wine
C) Mahr al-mithl
D) Nothing, because wine is not property

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī ruling on mahr of impure items in dhimmī marriage hinges on the distinction between specified and unspecified dower, and between mithlī and qimī items.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
8 min
Mahr of Wine or Pig Upon Wife's Conversion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on value vs. specific item in mahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the mahr of wine or pig when the wife converts to Islam, and apply the rules regarding specified versus unspecified dower.

Core Idea
For a specified dower, ownership is perfected by the contract; for an unspecified dower, possession is essential.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of a wife's conversion to Islam on a marriage contracted with a dower of wine or pig. Abu Hanifa and his two eminent disciples disagreed on whether the wife is entitled to the specific items or their monetary value. Through their arguments, we uncover the deeper principles of ownership and liability in Hanafi marriage law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The wife converts to Islam after the marriage contract. The mahr (dower) is specified as wine or pig, which are non-property for Muslims. The key question: what is she owed?
2
State the point of dispute
Abu Hanifa holds she is entitled to the specific items (though she cannot consume them, she can sell to a dhimmi). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold she is entitled to their monetary value (qimah).
The dispute hinges on whether ownership is perfected by the contract or by possession.
3
Trace Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that ownership of a specified dower (mu'ayyan) is perfected by the contract itself (bi-nafs al-'aqd). Possession merely transfers liability, which is not prevented by Islam. He analogizes to recovering usurped wine (khamr maghsoub).
For unspecified dower, possession creates ownership, so it is prevented by Islam.
4
Trace the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that delivery (taslim) of the prohibited item is impossible under Islam, so the husband must pay its value. They analogize to a named slave perishing before delivery (halaka al-'abd al-musamma qabla al-qabd).
They treat the prohibition as equivalent to physical destruction.
5
Apply the distinction between specified and unspecified dower
For specified: ownership is independent of possession, so the item is owed. For unspecified: ownership only by possession, so prevented; but value is not owed for pig (because pig is dhawat al-qiyam, value equals item) while value is owed for wine (because wine is dhawat al-amthal, value is distinct).
This distinction is crucial for understanding the practical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عندهم إلا أنه امتنع التسليم للإسلام فيجب القيمة كما إذا هلك العبد المسمى قبل القبض، ولأبي حنيفة أن الملك في الصداق المعين يتم بنفس العقد لهذا يملك التصرف فيه وبالقبض ينتقل من ضمان الزوج إلى ضمانها وذلك لا يمتنع بالإسلام كاسترداد الخمر المغصوب، وفي غير المعين القبض موجب ملك العين فيمتنع بالإسلام بخلاف المشتري لأن ملك التصرف إنما يستفاد فيه بالقبض، وإذا تعذر القبض في غير المعين لا تجب القيمة في الخنزير لأنه من ذوات القيم فيكون أخذ قيمته كأخذ عينه ولا كذلك الخمر لأنه من ذوات الأمثال ، ألا ترى أنه لو جاء بالقيمة قبل الإسلام تجبر على القبول في الخنزير دون الخمر. ولو طلقها قبل الدخول بها فمن أوجب مهر المثل أوجب المتعة، ومن أوجب القيمة أوجب نصفها. وفي الغاية ويرد على هذا ما لو اشترى ذمي داراً من ذمي بخمر أو خنزير وشفيعها مسلم يأخذ بالشفعة بقيمة الخمر والخنزير فلم تجعل قيمة الخنزير كعينه ولم يجب عنه بشيء. وأجاب عنه في التبيين أن قيمة الخنزير إنما تكون كعينه أن لو كان بدلاً . مسألة الخنزير كما في النكاح، أما لو كان بدلاً عن غيره فلا. وفي مسألة الشفعة قيمة الخنزير بدل عن الدار المشفوعة وإنما صير إليها للتقدير بها لا غير فلا يكون لها حكم عينه. وأفاد بقوله لها في المعين المسمى أنه لو كان طلقها قبل الدخول فإن لها نصفه والله تعالى أعلم .

عن
كالسكوت لأنها ليست مالاً عندهم فذكرها لغو وصحح المصنف أن الكل على الخلاف وهو خلاف الظاهر .

قوله (وفي مسألة الشفعة (الخ قال في الحواشي السعدية : ولك أن تقول كذلك فيما نحن فيه بدل عن البضع وإنما صير إليه للتقدير بها فليتأمل فجوابه يظهر من تقرير قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير. قال في النهر : وأقول لا نسلم أنها هنا بدل عن منافع البضع إذ منافعه إنما قوبلت بالخنزير وبالإسلام تعذر أخذ القيمة لما فصير إلى مهر المثل اهـ قلت والذي قرره قاضيخان هو قوله : ولأن قيمة الخنزير لها حكم عين الخنزير ولهذا لو أتاها بقيمة الخنزير قبل الإسلام أجبرت على القبول فكان وجوب قيمة الخنزير من موجبات تلك التسمية والإسلام يقرر حكم التسمية فإنما يستوفي بعد الإسلام ما ليس من موجبات تلك التسمية وهو مهر المثل، أما قيمة الخمر ليست من موجباتها فتستوفى بعد الإسلام .اهـ والذي يظهر من هذا التقرير أن الجواب يؤخذ من قوله إن قيمة الخنزير لها حكم عينه وأنها من موجبات التسمية ففيه منع لكون المصير إليها للتقدير بها بخلاف مسألة الشفعة فإن القيمة فيها ليست من موجبات التسمية وحينئذ فمناط الفرق هذا تأمل. وعليك بالتأمل في جواب النهر، ويمكن أن يكون هذا مراده وارجع على مامر في باب العاشر آخر الزكاة عند قوله عشر الخمر لا الخنزير.
According to them, however, delivery is prevented due to Islam, so the value is obligatory, just as if the named slave had perished before possession. For Abu Hanifa, ownership in the specified dower is perfected by the contract itself; therefore he has the right to dispose of it, and by possession it transfers from the husband's liability to hers, and that is not prevented by Islam, like recovering usurped wine. In the unspecified, possession causes ownership of the item, so it is prevented by Islam, unlike the buyer because the right to dispose is acquired only by possession. If possession becomes impossible for the unspecified, value is not obligatory for the pig because it belongs to items of value, so taking its value is like taking its item; but not so for wine because it belongs to items of volume. Do you not see that if he brought the value before Islam, she would be forced to accept for the pig, but not for wine. If he divorced her before consummation, then whoever obligates the mahr of the like obligates the mut'ah (gift), and whoever obligates the value obligates half of it. In Al-Ghayah, a refutation is presented: if a dhimmi buys a house from a dhimmi for wine or a pig, and the Muslim preemptor takes by preemption at the value of the wine and pig, then the value of the pig is not treated as its item, and nothing is incumbent. The response in Al-Tabyin is that the value of the pig would be like its item only if it were a substitute. The matter of the pig is as in marriage, but if it is a substitute for something else, then no. In the case of preemption, the value of the pig is a substitute for the preempted house, and it is only resorted to for estimation, not otherwise, so it does not have the ruling of its item. His statement 'to her in the specified named' indicates that if he divorced her before consummation, she gets half of it. And Allah knows best.

Regarding 'as silence', because it is not property according to them, so mentioning it is vain, and the author corrected that everything is according to the dispute, which is contrary to the apparent.

His statement (and in the matter of preemption, etc.) in Al-Hawashi Al-Sa'diyah said: you could say similarly in our case it is a substitute for the vagina and is only resorted to for estimation, so contemplate. Its answer appears from Qadikhan's elaboration in Sharh Al-Jami' Al-Saghir. In Al-Nahr: I say we do not concede that here it is a substitute for the benefits of the vagina, for its benefits were only countered by the pig, and with Islam, taking the value became impossible, so it was turned to the mahr of the like. End. I say: what Qadikhan established is his statement: because the value of the pig has the ruling of the pig's item, and therefore if he brought the value of the pig before Islam, she would be forced to accept, so the obligation of the pig's value is among the consequences of that specification, and Islam confirms the ruling of the specification; so after Islam she receives what is not among the consequences of that specification, namely the mahr of the like. As for the value of wine, it is not among its consequences, so it is received after Islam. End. What appears from this elaboration is that the answer is taken from his statement that the value of the pig has the ruling of its item and that it is among the consequences of the specification, but it is prevented from being resorted to for estimation, unlike the case of preemption, for the value there is not among the consequences of the specification. Thus the point of distinction is this, so contemplate. And you should contemplate the answer in Al-Nahr, and it is possible that this is his intent; and refer back to what passed in the chapter of 'Ashir at the end of Zakat at his statement: 'tithe of wine, not of pig'.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a wife's conversion to Islam on the mahr (dower) when it consists of items not permissible in Islam, such as wine (khamr) or a pig (khinzir). The dispute is between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The text first presents the position of the disciples: since the specific item (mu'ayyan) cannot be delivered because it is now prohibited, the husband must pay its value (qimah). They analogize this to the case of a named slave perishing before delivery. Abu Hanifa argues that ownership of the specified dower is completed by the contract itself; the husband can dispose of it, and possession merely transfers liability. This is not prevented by Islam, just as recovering usurped wine is allowed. However, if the mahr is unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyin), possession creates ownership of the item, so it is prevented by Islam, and the value of the pig is not obligatory because it is an item of value (dhawat al-qiyam), but for wine, value is not obligatory because it is an item of volume (dhawat al-amthal). The text then addresses an objection from the law of preemption (shuf'ah): if a dhimmi buys a house with wine or a pig, a Muslim preemptor pays its value, yet the value of the pig is not treated like its item. The response distinguishes that in marriage, the value is a substitute for the pig itself, while in preemption, it is only for estimation. After divorce before consummation, the ruling follows the same dispute: half the mahr or half the value. The commentary from later scholars (Qadikhan, Al-Nahr) further refines the distinction: the value of the pig is a consequence of the specification (tasmiyah), so after Islam the wife gets only the mahr of the like (mahr al-mithl), not the value. The lesson is a classic example of istihsan (juristic preference) and the application of qiyas with careful differentiation.

Key Terms
صداق معين (sadaq mu'ayyan): A dower that is specifically identified (e.g., a particular pig or wine).
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The standard dower of a woman of similar status, paid when the specified dower is invalid or not received.
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value, as a substitute for an item that cannot be delivered due to legal impediment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling for a wife who converts to Islam after the marriage contract, when the mahr is a specified pig?
A) She gets the pig but must sell it to a dhimmi.
B) She gets the value of the pig.
C) The mahr is void and she gets mahr al-mithl.
D) The marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
For a specified dower, ownership is perfected by the contract, so conversion does not change entitlement to the specific item; for unspecified dower, possession is required, so value is not due for items of value (pig) but due for items of volume (wine).

Marriage of Slaves Requires Master's Permission
Ruling statement with hadith proof and category extensions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a slave's marriage without the master's permission, identify the categories of slaves affected, and explain the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without the master's permission is invalid and suspended until permitted.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes that slaves cannot contract a valid marriage independently. The ruling is derived from a prophetic hadith and applies to all categories of slaves. Understanding this condition is essential for the correct practice of marriage contracts involving slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave (of any category) contracting marriage without the master's permission. The ruling is that it is invalid (ghayr sahih) and ineffective (la yanfudh).
In Hanafi terminology, 'invalid' here means the marriage is mawquf (suspended) like a fuduli contract.
2
Identify the categories of slaves
The ruling explicitly covers the male slave (abd), female slave (amah), mukatab, mudabbar, and umm walad. Each remains under the master's ownership regarding marriage.
The mukatab is free in earning but not in marriage; the mudabbar and umm walad are still owned.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith: 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his master is a fornicator.' (Sunan al-Tirmidhi, deemed hasan). The commentary explains that 'fornication' (uhr) here is shari'a fornication, not juristic fornication, so no hadd punishment applies.
This hadith indicates the marriage is invalid because the act of intercourse after the invalid contract is considered fornication.
4
Understand the concept of shari'a vs juristic fornication
Shari'a fornication does not incur the fixed hadd punishment; it only indicates the marriage is void. The distinction ensures that the slave is not subjected to hadd despite the invalid marriage.
This nuance is important to avoid over-penalizing the slave.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute about the partially free person
Abu Hanifa holds that a partially free person (mu'taq al-ba'd) also requires permission, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow him to marry without permission because he is free but in debt.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) may vary; this passage does not specify the preferred view.

باب نكاح الرقيق
النفاذ لا عدم
ذكره بعد نكاح الأحرار المسلمين مقدماً على نكاح الكفار لأن الإسلام فيهم غالب. والرقيق في اللغة العبد ويقال للعبيد. كذا في المغرب. والمراد به هنا المملوك من الآدمي لأنهم قالوا: إن الكافر إذا أسر في دار الحرب فهو رقيق لا مملوك، وإذا أخرج فهو مملوك أيضاً، فعلى هذا فكل مملوك من الآدمي رقيق لا عكسه قوله : (لم يجز نكاح العبد والأمة والمكاتب والمدبر وأم الولد إلا بإذن السيد أي لا ينفذ فالمراد بعدم الجواز عدم الصحة بقرينة سابقه في فصل الوكالة بالنكاح حيث صرح بأنه موقوف كعقد الفضولي لقوله عليه السلام أيما عبد تزوج بغير إذن مولاه فهو عاهر حسنه الترمذي. والعهر الزنا وهو محمول على ما إذا وطئ بمجرد العقد وهو زنا شرعي لا فقهي فلم يلزم منه وجوب الحد لأنه مترتب على الزنا الفقهي كما سيأتي ولأن في تنفيذ نكاحهما تعييبهما إذا النكاح عيب فيهما فلا يملكانه بدون إذن مولاهما وكذلك المكاتب لأن الكتابة أوجبت فك الحجر في حق الكسب فبقي في حق النكاح على حكم الرق ولهذا لا يملك المكاتب تزويج عبده ويملك تزويج أمته لأنه من باب الاكتساب وكذا المكاتبة لا تملك تزويج نفسها بدون أذن المولى وتملك تزويج أمتها لما قلنا وكذا المدبر وأم الولد لأن الملك فيهما قائم. ودخل في المكاتب معتق البعض لا يجوز نكاحه عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يجوز لأنه حر مديون. ودخل في أم الولد ابنها أي ابنها من غير مولاها كما إذا زوج أم ولده من غيره فجاءت بولد من زوجها فحكمه حكم أمه وأما ولدها من مولاها فحر ويستثنى من قولهم ابن أم الولد من غير المولى كأمه مسألة ذكرها في المبسوط من باب الاستيلاد لو اشترى ابن أم ولد له بأن استولد جارية بالنكاح ثم فارقها فزوجها المولى من غيره فولدت ثم اشترى الجارية مع الولدين فالجارية تكون أم ولد له وولده حر وولدها من غيره له بيعه اهـ. إلا أن يقال : إنها حين ولدته لم تكن أم ولد له فلا استثناء، وأطلق في نكاحه فشمل ما إذا تزوج بنفسه وما إذا زوجه غيره، وقيد بالنكاح لأن التسري للعبد والمكاتب والمدير حرام مطلقاً. كذا في شرح

It is not permissible for a slave, slave-girl, mukatab, mudabbar, or umm walad to marry except with the master's permission. So if a slave marries...
Chapter of Slave Marriage
[The permission is for] validity, not [for] nonexistence.
He mentioned it after the chapter of marriage of free Muslims, prior to the marriage of disbelievers, because Islam is prevalent among them. 'Raqiq' linguistically means slave (abd) and is also used for slaves. This is from Al-Maghrib. What is meant here is a human slave (mamluk), because they said: when an unbeliever is captured in dar al-harb, he is raqiq not mamluk, and when he is brought out, he is also mamluk. So accordingly, every human slave is raqiq, but not vice versa. His statement: (It is not permissible for a slave, slave-girl, mukatab, mudabbar, or umm walad to marry except with the master's permission) i.e., it does not become effective (la yanfudh). By 'lack of permissibility' is meant lack of validity, indicated by what preceded in the section on agency in marriage where it was stated that it is suspended (mawquf) like the contract of a stranger (fuduli), because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his master is a fornicator.' This hadith was deemed hasan by al-Tirmidhi. 'Uhr' means fornication, and it is understood to refer to one who has intercourse based on the mere contract, which is shari'a fornication, not juristic fornication; so it does not necessitate the fixed punishment (hadd) because that is contingent on juristic fornication, as will come. Also, because enforcing their marriage would be a defect against them, since marriage is a defect in them, so they do not own it without the permission of their masters. The same applies to the mukatab, because kitaba (contract of manumission) necessitates removal of interdiction (hajr) with respect to earning, so he remains under the ruling of slavery with respect to marriage. For this reason, the mukatab does not own the right to marry off his slave, but he owns the right to marry off his slave-girl because it is a matter of earning. Similarly, the mukatabah does not own marrying herself without the master's permission, but she owns marrying off her slave-girl, as we said. The same applies to the mudabbar and umm walad because ownership is established over them. Included under mukatab is the one who is partly free (mu'taq al-ba'd): his marriage is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible because he is a free man in debt. Included under umm walad is her son, i.e., her son from someone other than her master. For example, if her master marries her to another man and she bears a child from her husband, that child's ruling is the same as his mother's. As for her child from her master, it is free. An exception is made from their statement that the child of umm walad from other than the master is like his mother. There is a case mentioned in Al-Mabsut from the chapter of istilad: if [the master] buys the son of his umm walad — such that he impregnated a slave-girl in marriage then separated from her, then the master married her to another and she bore a child, then he bought the slave-girl along with the two children — then the slave-girl becomes his umm walad and his child is free, and her child from another is subject to his sale. End. Unless it is said: at the time she gave birth to him she was not his umm walad, so no exception. He (the author) used absolute terms in his marriage, so it includes whether he marries himself or is married by another. He restricted it to marriage because taking concubines (tasarrī) for a slave, mukatab, and mudabbar is absolutely forbidden. This from the commentary.

from other than him

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter on slave marriage. The main ruling is that slaves of various categories (abd, amah, mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad) cannot contract a valid marriage without the master's permission. The bar on validity (not merely permissibility) is established by a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that such a marriage renders the slave a 'fornicator' (aahir), meaning the act of intercourse after the invalid marriage is considered shari'a fornication, not juristic fornication, so the hadd punishment is not applied. The commentary explains that the requirement of permission is for the validity (nifadh) of the marriage, not for its existence. The discussion extends to various categories: a mukatab remains under slave rulings for marriage even though he is free in earning; a mudabbar and umm walad are still owned, so the same rule applies. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a partly free person (mu'taq al-ba'd): Abu Hanifa says not permissible, while the two disciples say permissible as he is free but indebted. The passage also addresses the offspring of an umm walad: from the master, the child is free; from another man, the child's ruling follows the mother's (i.e., still a slave). The commentary includes a case from Al-Mabsut about buying the child of an umm walad. The text is cut off at the end, so the full treatment is incomplete.

Key Terms
رقيق (raqiq): A human slave, including categories like abd, amah, mukatab, etc.
عهر (uhr): Fornication; in the hadith it refers to the act of intercourse based on an invalid marriage, considered shari'a fornication (not juristic) so no hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A slave's marriage without the master's permission is invalid (ghayr sahih) and suspended (mawquf), becoming valid only upon the master's consent.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Marriage of Slaves Requires Master's Permission
States the basic ruling and elaborates on its details and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a slave's marriage without master's permission, identify the proof, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions on the partially freed slave.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without master's permission is suspended until authorized.

Marriage is a contract that requires full legal capacity. For slaves, this capacity is limited because ownership restricts their autonomy. This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling that the marriage of a slave (male, female, mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad) is not effective without the master's permission, and is suspended (mawquf) like the contract of an unauthorized agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of slaves
List the types of slaves covered: 'abd (male slave), amah (female slave), mukatab (under manumission contract), mudabbar (promised freedom after death), and umm walad (mother of master's child). All require master's permission for marriage.
The text explicitly includes these five categories.
2
Understand the ruling: suspended validity
The marriage is not valid (la yanfudh) without permission, but it is suspended (mawquf) on subsequent authorization, analogous to the contract of a fuduli (unauthorized agent). If the master later permits, it becomes effective; if denied, it is void.
The proof is the hadith: 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.'
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith is cited as evidence. The text explains that the fornication mentioned is shar'i (legitimate in the sense of being against the law) not fiqhi (subject to hadd punishment), so no hadd is imposed. The warning implies the marriage is invalid without permission.
Distinction between shar'i and fiqhi zina is crucial for understanding the legal consequence.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on mu'ataq ba'd
For a partially freed slave (mu'ataq ba'd), Abu Hanifa holds that permission is still needed because the master retains rights. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold it is not needed because he is considered a free man in debt. The practical difference is that under Abu Hanifa the marriage is suspended, while under the Sahibayn it is immediately valid.
The commentator does not give a clear preference; both views are recorded.
5
Apply exceptions: who can give permission
For a male slave, only someone who has the right to manumit him can marry him off (e.g., his master). For a female slave, more authorities (father, grandfather, judge, executor, mukatab, etc.) can give permission because marriage is not a defect for her to the same extent.
This is derived from the principle: 'whoever can manumit can marry off a male slave.'

Chapter on the marriage of slaves. The marriage of a male slave, female slave, mukatab, mudabbar, and umm walad is not permissible except with the permission of the master. So if a slave marries... The chapter on the marriage of slaves: validity, not non-existence. He mentioned it after the marriage of free Muslims, before the marriage of disbelievers, because Islam is predominant among them. Linguistic definition: al-riq (slavery) refers to the slave, used for slaves. So in al-Mughrib. The intended meaning here is a human owned (mamluk), because they said: when a disbeliever is captured in Dar al-Harb, he is a slave (raqiq), not a mamluk; when he is taken out, he is also mamluk. Thus, every owned human is a slave, but not the reverse. His saying: 'The marriage of a male slave, female slave, mukatab, mudabbar, and umm walad is not permissible except with the permission of the master' means it is not effective (la yanfudh); the intended meaning of impermissibility is lack of validity, indicated by the preceding context in the chapter on agency in marriage, where he stated it is suspended like the contract of a stranger (fuduli), due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his master is a fornicator.' Al-Tirmidhi graded it hasan. Al-'uhar is fornication, and it is interpreted as when he has intercourse merely by the contract, which is a legitimate (share'i) fornication, not a juristic (fiqhi) one, so it does not necessitate hadd punishment, because hadd is based on fiqhi fornication, as will come. Also, executing their marriage would be a defect for them, because marriage is a defect for them, so they do not possess it without the permission of their master. Similarly, the mukatab, because kitabah brings about the removal of interdiction (hajr) regarding earning, but regarding marriage he remains under the ruling of slavery. Therefore, the mukatab does not possess the authority to marry off his slave, but he does possess the authority to marry off his female slave because it is a matter of earning. Similarly, the mukatabah does not possess the authority to marry herself without the master's permission, but she does possess the authority to marry off her female slave for the reason we mentioned. Similarly, the mudabbar and umm walad, because ownership (milk) subsists in them. Included in the mukatab is the partially freed slave (mu'ataq ba'd): his marriage is not permissible according to Abu Hanifah, but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible because he is a free man who is in debt. Included in the umm walad is her son, i.e., her son from other than her master, as when he marries his umm walad to someone else and she brings a child from her husband: the ruling of that child is the same as his mother. As for her child from her master, he is free. An exception from their statement 'the son of the umm walad from other than the master is like his mother' is a case mentioned in al-Mabsut from the chapter on istilad: if a man buys the son of his umm walad, meaning he made his slave-girl umm walad through marriage, then separated from her, then the master married her to another, and she gave birth, then he bought the slave-girl with the two children: the slave-girl becomes his umm walad, his child is free, and her child from another can be sold. End quote. Unless it is said: at the time she gave birth to him she was not his umm walad, so no exception. The phrase 'his marriage' is general, including when he marries himself and when someone else marries him off. It is restricted to marriage because taking a concubine (tasarrī) is absolutely forbidden for the slave, mukatab, and mudabbar. Similar in the commentary of the chapter on marriage of slaves by another. His saying: 'Because they said...' Al-Nahr says: its implication is that if a female slave married in this situation, her marriage would not be suspended but rather invalid, because at the moment of occurrence there is no one to authorize it; I have not found it explicitly in their statements. His saying: 'And by this it is known that 'master' here...' This applies to the female slave, not the male slave, as in al-Durar: Know that whoever does not possess the right to manumit a male slave does not possess the right to marry him off, unlike the female slave. So the father, grandfather, guardian (wali), judge, executor (wasi), mukatab, and the partner (sharik mufawid) possess the right to marry off the female slave, etc. But the correct thing is to omit 'wali' and restrict to others mentioned, as was done in Mukhtasar al-Zahiriyyah, because there is no guardian other than the father, grandfather, executor, and judge with authority over the property of a minor. In al-Shurnublaliyah and al-Nahr: I have not found the ruling on the marriage of the slave of the public treasury, and the slave in the spoils of war stored in our land before division, and the endowment (waqf) if with the permission of the imam and the trustee; it should be valid for the female slave but not the male slave, like the executor. Then I saw in al-Bazzaziyyah: Only he who possesses the right to manumit a male slave can marry him off. End quote. The citation from al-Bazzaziyyah and similar to what was mentioned from al-Durar only indicates his statement regarding the male slave, not the female slave. Yes, deriving permissibility for the female slave by analogy with the executor is apparent. His saying: 'If he marries the female slave of the orphan to his slave, i.e., the orphan's slave...' His saying: 'And this is excepted from their statement...' And similarly excepted from...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling that the marriage of a slave (male or female), a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission), a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death), and an umm walad (a slave-mother of her master's child) is not valid (ghayr ja'iz) without the permission of the master. The commentator begins by explaining that 'not permissible' means not valid (la yanfudh), drawing an analogy to the case of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) in the previous section. The proof is a hadith: 'Any slave who marries without the master's permission is a fornicator' (hasan by al-Tirmidhi). The commentator clarifies that the fornication here is shar'i (valid in the eyes of the Law) not fiqhi (subject to hadd), so the slave does not incur the hadd punishment. The ruling applies equally to all these categories because ownership (milk) subsists in them; the mukatab's freedom is partial (in earning only), so marriage remains like a slave. An intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Hanifa says the marriage of a mu'ataq ba'd (partially freed slave) is not permissible without permission, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is permissible because he is free but in debt. The text then addresses the status of the children of an umm walad: her child from other than her master is like her (slave), but her child from her master is free. An exception from al-Mabsut is cited about re-purchasing an umm walad with her children. The passage then discusses who qualifies as 'master' for permission: it includes anyone with authority to marry the slave off, like the father, grandfather, judge, executor for an orphan's female slave, but not for a male slave (since they lack authority to manumit). The mukatab and the shariq mufawid can marry off a female slave (as earning), but not a male slave. There is a discussion about the ruling for slaves of the public treasury or spoils: likely valid for female slaves only, by analogy with the executor. The overall rule: only he who has the right to manumit a male slave can marry him off.

Key Terms
رقيق (raqīq): A human being who is owned by another, used here to mean slave (male or female).
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has a contract of manumission with his master, paying in installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master declares he will be free upon the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and thus becomes free upon his death.
عهر ('uhar): Fornication (zina) mentioned in the hadith, interpreted here as share'i (valid in religion) not fiqhi (subject to legal punishment).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the marriage of a slave who marries without his master's permission?
A) It is void (batil) immediately.
B) It is valid but sinful (makruh).
C) It is suspended (mawquf) on the master's permission.
D) It is valid and the master's consent is not required.

Key Takeaway
The marriage of a slave without master's permission is not valid but suspended; it can become valid upon subsequent authorization.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Mahr and Nafaqah: One Sale vs. Repeated Sales for the Slave
Commentary on the ruling that a slave married with permission is sold once for mahr but repeatedly for nafaqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave married with permission is sold once for mahr and repeatedly for nafaqah, and explain the legal analogy to trade debt.

Core Idea
Mahr is a single debt; nafaqah is renewed—so the slave is sold once for mahr, repeatedly for nafaqah.

This lesson unpacks a key commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The central issue is how a slave who marries with his master's permission satisfies the debts of mahr and nafaqah. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply: the slave is sold only once for mahr, but can be sold multiple times for nafaqah because nafaqah creates new debts as time passes. The ruling is built on an analogy to trade debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave marrying with the master's permission. The ruling is that the mahr becomes a debt on the slave's neck, and the slave is sold to pay it (once).
The text specifies 'with his permission' because without permission the rules differ.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to trade debt (dayn al-tijarah). The slave with trade debt is sold to pay creditors; likewise, mahr debt arises with permission and attaches to the neck.
The author quotes 'as in trade debt' (كما في دين التجارة).
3
Trace the 'illa
The effective cause ('illa) is that both are debts that arise with the master's permission and manifest against his right. The slave's neck is the locus for satisfying such debts.
The 'illa is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted via tahqiq al-manat.
4
Distinguish mahr from nafaqah
Mahr is a single debt; if the slave is sold and the price is insufficient, the remainder is pursued after emancipation, but no second sale. Nafaqah, however, renews over time, so each accumulation constitutes a new debt allowing repeated sales.
The text quotes al-Mabsut: only nafaqah allows repeated sales.
5
Examine the sub-rulings
The passage also covers: if the slave dies, mahr and nafaqah drop (no liability on master if no earnings left). If marriage was without permission, different rules apply (no mahr until emancipation, etc.).
These sub-rulings reinforce the principle that the master's permission is the key factor.

Book of Marriage, Chapter on Marriage of Slaves. With his permission, he is sold for her mahr, and the endeavor of the mudabbar and mukatab, and he is not sold therein, and if he divorces her revocably, it is an acceptance of the marriage, unlike the first two because their suspension is not valid. This is the principle regarding what is dependent and what is limited from the suspended. His statement: (If a slave marries with his permission, he is sold for her mahr) i.e., with the master's permission because it is a debt that becomes obligatory on the slave's neck due to the existence of its cause from its people, and it has become apparent regarding the master due to the issuance of permission from him, so it attaches to his neck to repel harm from the creditors, as in trade debt, so he is sold for it unless the master ransoms him because the purpose is achieved. One thing: he is sold for maintenance. It is repelling harm from the creditor. And the author indicated by limiting to sale that it refers to one sale; if its price does not cover the mahr, he is not sold a second time, and the remainder is pursued after emancipation. And in a debt, he is sold again and again because it becomes obligatory little by little. In al-Mabsut: When maintenance accumulates on him that he is unable to pay, he is sold for it, then when maintenance accumulates again, he is sold for it as well. There is nothing among the debts of a slave that is sold again and again except maintenance because its obligation is renewed with the passage of time, and that is in the ruling of a new debt. It indicates that if, for example, two hundred accumulates on him and he is sold for one hundred, he is not sold a second time for the accumulated maintenance, but is sold for what will come later. And it is explained in Miraj al-Diraya regarding the non-repetition of sale for mahr that he is sold for the entire mahr, so it indicates that if his mahr is prompt and then the term arrives, he is sold again because he was only sold for part of it. The apparent meaning of their speech regarding the indebted authorized slave is that he is sold for a small debt, so likewise he is sold for a small mahr when his master does not redeem him. He indicated by sale that if the slave dies, the mahr and maintenance are dropped. Al-Timirtashi mentioned it. And he made it absolute, so it includes whether the slave had intercourse with her or not. And we restricted it to permission because if he marries without permission, if he did not have intercourse, there is no ruling; if he did have intercourse, then either the master separates them afterwards or he validates the marriage. If he separates them, she has no mahr against him until he is emancipated because it is a debt that did not appear regarding the master, so it becomes like a debt the slave incurred. If the master validates it afterwards, by analogy two mahr are obligatory: mahr for intercourse and mahr for validation, as in an invalid marriage if it is renewed. By istihsan, only the named mahr is required because if the dower of the like were obligatory, it would be by virtue of a valid contract. Their statement: He who does not own the emancipation of a slave does not own his marriage. His statement: (It indicates that if it accumulates...) Then here, maintenance equals mahr in that he is not sold a second time to complete what he was sold for the first time. They differ in that he is sold for what will come, i.e., what occurs of maintenance after the sale. Some virtuous person objected: If another mahr becomes incumbent upon him under the second master, as if he divorces her and then marries her, he is sold a second time, so there is no difference between mahr and maintenance except by consideration that maintenance renews under the second master necessarily, unlike mahr. The answer is that the maintenance that occurred under the second master, its cause is verified under the first master, so his sale is repeated for something that occurred, unlike sale for a second mahr that occurred under the second master, because this is caused by an independent contract until it stops upon... His statement: (It indicates that he was sold...) Apparent is that this indication is not intended. How can he be sold under the buyer and not... Book of Marriage, Chapter on Marriage of Slaves. And then, by one contract, two mahr would be obligatory, and that is impossible. So in al-Muhit and others. His speech indicates that if the master marries him himself, then he is sold a fortiori. In al-Qunyah: He sold his slave after he had married him to a woman; the mahr is in the neck of the slave, he circulates with it wherever he circulates, that is the correct view, like debt of consumption. It is said that the mahr is in the price. Both opinions are problematic because they made mahr like trade debt. They transmitted in the chapter on the authorized slave that if the master sells the indebted slave without the consent of the creditors, they revoke the sale and take him. If the buyer had hidden the slave, they have the option: if they wish, they hold the master liable for his value, or they hold the buyer liable for his value, or they validate the sale and take the price. So likewise here. And debt of consumption is not different from trade debt, for he is sold in all. In al-Qunyah also: He married his slave to a free woman then emancipated him; she has the choice to hold liable the master or the slave. Then another note: the master is liable for the lesser of his value and her mahr. In Fatawa Qadikhan: He married his slave to a woman for one thousand dirhams, then sold him to her for nine hundred dirhams after the slave had intercourse; she takes the nine hundred as her mahr, the marriage is invalidated, and the woman does not recourse for the remaining hundred against the slave, even if he is emancipated. And if the slave had a debt to another man of one thousand dirhams, and the creditor validated the sale of the slave to the woman, the nine hundred is between the creditor and the woman; the creditor claims it for one thousand dirhams and the woman for one thousand dirhams; the woman does not follow the slave after that, and the creditor follows him for what remains of his debt when he emancipates. Know that they said in the book of the authorized slave: If the master emancipates the indebted slave, the creditor chooses between holding the master liable for the value or following the slave for the entire debt. There is no difference between emancipation with or without the creditor's permission. If he makes him mudabbar, if he wishes he holds the master liable for his value, and if he wishes he makes the slave work for all his debt. If he sells him, we have written it. If he gifts him without the creditor's permission, he has the right to rescind it. With permission, there are two narrations. According to the narration of permissibility, the creditor has the right to sell him and take him from the donee because he has transferred to him with his debt. If the slave's debt is deferred, and his master sells him or gifts him, it is valid. When it comes due, the master is liable for his value. If he pawns him or rents him before the due, it is valid. When it comes due, the master is liable for his value in pawning but not in rent, and the creditor has the right to cancel it. And the judge may sell the indebted slave for payment if his master refuses, but in his presence. If the master wants to pay the amount of his value, he may do so, and the whole is not sold. From al-Muhit. And since you know that mahr is like trade debt, these rulings also apply to mahr. Al-Hakim mentioned in al-Kafi that the authorized slave indebted to a creditor prevents the master from using him, pawning him, renting him, and traveling with him if the debt is due. If it is deferred, he has... a new cause arises that necessitates his sale while he is in his hand, so that it is in the ruling of a new debt. The coming of the term is not a renewal of obligation, but the mahr is all one debt, so said in al-Mabsut: and there is nothing among the debts of the slave to the end of what preceded. His statement: (where he did not redeem him) meaning his master, and it is present tense from fada. His statement: (the mahr and maintenance are dropped) will come in the explanation of his statement: And if he marries an authorized slave, that it is interpreted regarding mahr as when the slave is restricted or authorized without leaving earnings, otherwise it is taken from what he left of earnings. Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the ruling of a slave who marries with his master's permission. The main ruling: when a slave marries with permission, the mahr (dower) becomes a debt on his neck, and he can be sold to pay it, once. This is analogous to a trade debt. The master may ransom him to avoid sale. The slave is sold only once for mahr; if the sale price does not cover the mahr, the remainder is pursued after emancipation. However, for nafaqah (maintenance), the situation differs: since maintenance obligations renew over time, the slave can be sold repeatedly as new debts accumulate. The author quotes al-Mabsut to confirm that only maintenance allows repeated sales. The commentary then addresses an objection: if the slave incurs a new mahr under a second master (e.g., after divorce and remarriage), could he be sold again? The response distinguishes that the second mahr arises from a new contract, not a renewal of the same debt, so the rule of one sale per mahr debt remains. The passage also discusses cases where the slave dies (mahr and maintenance are dropped), cases without permission (no mahr unless intercourse, then only after emancipation), and validates the marriage (by istihsan, only the named mahr is due). Later, the commentator brings rulings from al-Qunyah and Fatawa Qadikhan regarding scenarios where the master sells the slave after marriage, the priority of creditors, and options upon emancipation. The overarching principle: mahr is treated like a trade debt, and the rulings from the chapter on the authorized slave apply. The passage ends with a discussion of the master's restrictions when the slave is indebted.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower or bridal gift owed by the husband to the wife, which becomes a debt on the slave's neck when he marries with permission.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance owed to the wife, which renews over time and thus creates multiple debts.
دين التجارة (dayn al-tijarah): Trade debt incurred by an authorized slave with the master's permission in the course of business.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade, thereby incurring debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave sold only once for mahr but can be sold repeatedly for nafaqah?
A) Because mahr is a large sum while nafaqah is small.
B) Because mahr is a single debt whereas nafaqah creates new debts over time.
C) Because mahr must be paid before marriage and nafaqah after.
D) Because the master prefers to sell once for mahr.

Key Takeaway
A slave married with permission is sold once for mahr (like trade debt) but repeatedly for nafaqah because nafaqah obligations renew over time.

Mahr of an Amah When Married to Her Master's Slave: Abu Yusuf vs. the Two Shaykhs
Analysis of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mahr obligation for amah married to master's own slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation of mahr for an amah married to her master's own slave, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah) of the disagreement.

Core Idea
When master marries his amah to his own slave, the mahr has no practical effect, so its obligation is merely theoretical.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the mahr of an amah when her master marries her to his own slave. The dispute hinges on whether the mahr is obligated at all, and the discussion explores the practical consequence (thamarah) of this disagreement. The author navigates between the views of later Hanafi commentators to determine which position is more sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between the case where the master marries his amah to another man's slave (al-Muhit's case) vs. to his own slave (our case). Note the different rulings in each scenario.
The thamarah appears only in the first scenario.
2
Understand the ikhtilaf in our case
Abu Yusuf says mahr is obligatory then drops; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it is never obligatory. What is the legal rationale for each?
The majority view is non-obligation.
3
Analyze the thamarah (practical consequence)
In the first scenario (another's slave), the thamarah is whether the amah's debts are paid from the mahr. In our scenario, there is no thamarah because both sides agree the master ultimately gets the mahr.
Why does this matter for the validity of the ikhtilaf?
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The author notes that al-Nahr restricts the two opinions to the case with thamarah, implying that in the case without thamarah, the ikhtilaf is meaningless. Reflect on whether this means the majority view (non-obligation) is preferred.
The author leaves room for reflection (تأمل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقل عن المحيط : ارتدت قبل الدخول أو قبلت ابن زوجها قيل لا يسقط لأن الحق للمولى، وقيل يسقط لأنه يجب لها ثم ينتقل إلى المولى إذا فرغ من حاجتها حتى لو كان عليها دين يصرف إلى حاجتها اهـ. والأظهر ما في الرمز لأن ظاهر كلام الفتح والمحيط أن الصرف إلى حاجتها مفرع على القول بأنه يثبت لها لا على القولين، وقد يقال الأظهر ما في النهر لأن الخلاف في مسألة المحيط فيما إذا زوج أمته غير عبده والخلاف في مسألتنا فيما إذا زوجها عبده وحاصل الخلاف فيها أنه هل يجب للمولى ثم يسقط أم لا يجب أصلاً، فالثمرة إنما تظهر في الخلاف في الأولى لأن من قال الحق للمولى لا يقول بالصرف إلى حاجتها، ومن قال الحق له منتقلاً عنها يقول بالصرف، أما في مسألتنا فلا تظهر الثمرة، فقول النهر «ينبغي الخ» تقييد للقولين فيها لأنه حينئذ لا محذور في وجوبه لها لأن قال بسقوطه بعد وجوبه يدعي عدم الفائدة في بقائه، ومن من أصلاً يدعي بقائه دليل عدم وجوبه تأمل .
It is narrated from al-Muhit: If she apostatizes before intercourse or accepts her husband's son, it is said that [the mahr] does not drop because the right belongs to the master, and it is said that it drops because it is obligatory for her then transfers to the master when her need is fulfilled, such that if she has a debt, it is spent for her need. End. And the more apparent is what is in al-Ramz because the apparent wording of al-Fath and al-Muhit indicates that spending for her need is derived from the opinion that it is established for her, not on both opinions. And it may be said that the more apparent is what is in al-Nahr because the disagreement in the issue of al-Muhit is when he married his amah to someone other than his slave, while the disagreement in our issue is when he married her to his own slave. The summary of the disagreement therein is: Is it obligatory for the master then drops, or is it not obligatory at all? The practical consequence (thamar) only appears in the first disagreement because whoever says the right belongs to the master does not say spending for her need, while whoever says the right is for him but transferred from her says spending. As for our issue, no practical consequence appears. So the statement of al-Nahr 'it should be...' is a restriction of the two opinions in it because then there is no harm in its being obligatory for her, because he who says it drops after obligation claims there is no benefit in its remaining, while he who denies obligation [altogether] claims its remaining is evidence of its non-obligation. Reflect.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute analysis. The author quotes al-Muhit regarding a case: a slave woman (amah) who apostatizes before consummation or accepts her husband's son (i.e., marries a mahram?) – there are two views on whether her mahr drops: (1) It does not drop because the right to mahr is for her master, not for her; (2) It drops because the mahr is obligated to her first, then transfers to her master after her needs are met (e.g., if she has debts, the mahr goes to her debts before the master). The author then prefers the view in al-Ramz that the spending for her need is derived only from the opinion that the mahr is established for her (not from both opinions). Then he presents an alternative preference from al-Nahr, which distinguishes two issues: (a) the case in al-Muhit where the master marries his amah to someone else's slave, and (b) our case (مسألتنا) where the master marries his amah to his own slave. In (b), there is a dispute among Hanafi imams: Abu Yusuf holds that the mahr is obligatory (for the master) then drops, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it is never obligatory. The practical consequence (thamar) of this ikhtilaf only appears in case (a) (marrying to another's slave) because the two opinions lead to different results regarding spending from her mahr for her needs. In case (b), there is no practical consequence because both opinions agree that the mahr does not ultimately benefit her. Thus, al-Nahr's statement restricts the two opinions in that issue. The passage ends with a note to reflect on the reasoning.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bride-price given by the husband to the wife in marriage; for a slave woman, it belongs to her master.
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by a master.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarat al-khilaf): The practical consequence or real-world difference resulting from a legal disagreement among scholars.
مولى (mawla): Master or owner of a slave; in this context, the owner of the amah and/or the slave husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a master marrying his amah to his own slave, what is the position of Abu Yusuf?
A) The mahr is not obligatory at all.
B) The mahr is obligatory then drops.
C) The mahr belongs to the amah permanently.
D) The mahr is obligatory only if the amah is not indebted.

Key Takeaway
In the case of a master marrying his amah to his own slave, the khilaf between Abu Yusuf and the two shaykhs has no practical consequence, making the majority view (non-obligation) the more sound.

Marriage of Free and Slave Parties and Mahr of Slave Women
Ruling on marriage validity when mixing free and slave, and mahr ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of mixed free-slave marriages with the master's permission and identify who owns the mahr of a slave woman, including the exceptions.

Core Idea
In mixed marriages, only free marriages are valid with permission; the mahr of a slave woman belongs to her master except for a mukataba or partly free woman.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq presents two distinct Hanafi rulings. The first concerns the validity of marriages when a man marries both free and slave women, and how the master's permission and consummation affect the ruling. The second establishes a general rule: the mahr of a slave woman belongs to her master, with specific exceptions. Understanding these rulings is essential for correctly applying marriage and property laws in cases involving slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and note that it contains two separate parts: the first on marriage validity (from the beginning to 'من الظهيرية') and the second on mahr ownership (from 'وفي البدائع' to the end).
The transition 'وفي البدائع' signals a new source and topic.
2
Analyze the marriage scenario
Focus on the example: a man marries two free women and a slave woman. With the master's permission, only the marriages of the free women are valid. If he consummates the marriages, all become invalid. Determine the conditions (permission) and invalidators (consummation after an invalid union).
The scenario includes marrying a woman and her mother, which is a permanent impediment, but the ruling focuses on the slave element.
3
Extract the mahr rule
The second part states: every mahr that becomes obligatory for a slave woman belongs to her master, regardless of how it is set, and regardless of her status (qinn, mudabbara, umm walad). The only exceptions are a mukataba and a partly free slave, whose mahr belongs to them.
The rule covers both mahr specified in the contract and mahr al-mithl.
4
Integrate the two rulings
Note that the two rulings are independent: the validity of the marriage and the ownership of mahr are separate issues. Even if a marriage is invalid (fasid), a mahr may still be due (for consummation), and it goes to the master in general.
For example, if a slave marriage is consummated without permission, the marriage is invalid but the master still receives the mahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد الفريقين لم يجز نكاح شيء منهن، ولو تزوج حرة وأمه ثم حرة وأمة فأجاز المولى الكل جاز نكاح الحرتين، وإن دخل بهن فنكاحهن فاسد الكل من الظهيرية. ولم يبين الأمة وفي البدائع : ثم كل ما وجب من مهر الأمة فهو للمولى، سواء وجب بالعقد أو بالدخول، سواء كان المهر مسمى أو مهر المثل، سواء كانت الأمة قنة أو مدبرة أو أم ولد إلا المكاتبة والمعتق بعضها فإن المهر لها ا هـ.
If one of the two parties is not permissible for marriage, then marrying any of them is not valid. And if a free man married a free woman and her mother, then a free woman and a slave woman, and the master gave permission for all, the marriage of the two free women is valid. But if he had intercourse with them, then all marriages are invalid – from al-Zahiriyyah. He did not clarify the rule for the slave woman. In al-Bada'i: 'Then every mahr that becomes obligatory for a slave woman belongs to her master, whether it becomes obligatory by contract or by intercourse, whether the mahr is specified or the mahr of her peers, whether the slave woman is a qinn, mudabbara, or umm walad – except for a mukataba and one who is partly free, for her mahr belongs to her.'
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two rulings. First, from al-Zahiriyyah: if one of the parties in a marriage is not permissible for the other (e.g., a free person marrying a slave without permission), then the marriage is invalid. The example given is a man marrying two free women and then a slave woman; if the master gives permission for all, only the marriages of the free women are valid. If he consummated with all, all are invalid. The author notes that the source did not clarify the rule for the slave woman. Second, from al-Bada'i, the rule on the mahr of a slave woman: it belongs to her master in all cases, whether the mahr is specified or the mahr of similar women, and whether the slave woman is a qinn (full slave), mudabbara (promised freedom upon the master's death), or umm walad (mother of the master's child). The exception is a mukataba (slave with a contract of emancipation) or a slave who is partly free, for in those cases the mahr belongs to the slave woman herself.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave.
المهر (al-mahr): The dower paid to a wife upon marriage; for a slave woman, it belongs to her master.
مكاتبة (mukataba): A slave who has a written contract to earn and buy her freedom.
قنة (qinn): A full slave owned completely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Her master.

D) It is given to charity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mixing free and slave parties in marriage requires careful permission, and the mahr of a slave woman belongs to her master except for those with emancipation contracts.

Marriage of a Slave on His Person: Valid and Invalid Scenarios
Ruling on marriage with slave's person as dower

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master permits his slave to marry on his person and distinguish between valid and invalid scenarios based on the type of female spouse and the slave's own status.

Core Idea
Ownership of the spouse's person invalidates marriage; transferability of the slave's raqaba determines the ruling.

In this passage from al-Muhit, an important distinction is made regarding a master's permission for his slave to marry on his person (i.e., with his raqaba as the dower or security). The validity of the marriage hinges on whether the female spouse's master obtains ownership of the slave's person at the time of the contract. If that ownership conflicts with the nature of marriage, the contract is invalid. We explore the scenarios and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the master's instruction
The master says to his slave: 'Marry on your person.' This means the marriage is contracted with the slave's own person (his raqaba) as the consideration or basis.
2
Classify the female spouse
The slave marries one of four types: a female slave (amma), a mudabbara, an umm walad, a free woman, or a mukataba. Each has different implications for ownership transfer.
3
Apply the key principle: ownership of raqaba
If the marriage results in the female spouse (or her master) acquiring ownership of the male slave's raqaba concurrently with the contract, that corrupts the marriage because a spouse cannot own the other's person. If ownership is already with another master (like the female slave's master), it is not conflicting.
4
Derive the ruling for each female type
With master's permission, marrying an amma, mudabbara, or umm walad is valid because the male slave's raqaba becomes owned by the female slave's master, which is permissible. Marrying a free woman or mukataba is invalid because the free woman or mukataba would own the male slave's raqaba, which is incompatible with marriage.
5
Consider the exception: male slave is mukatab or mudabbar
If the male slave himself is a mukatab or mudabbar, then his raqaba is not transferable from his master's ownership, so no conflict arises. The marriage is valid even with a free woman, and the mahr is the market value of the slave.
The passage ends with 'wa yakuun al-mahr al-qeema' (and the mahr is the value).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال لعبده تزوج على رقبتك فتزوج على رقبته أمة أو مدبرة أو أم ولد أذن مولاها جاز لأن الملك في رقبته يثبت لمولاها فلا يمنع الجواز، ولو تزوج حرة أو مكاتبة فالنكاح فاسد لأنه لو صح يثبت الملك للمنكوحة في رقبته مقارناً للعقد وأنه مفسد له إذا طرأ فإذا قارن أولى أن يمنع جوازه ، فلو كان العبد مكاتباً أو مدبراً صح النكاح لأنهما لا يحتملان النقل من ملك مولاهما ويكون المهر القيمة ا هـ .
And in al-Muhit: If he said to his slave, 'Marry on your person,' and he then married on his person a female slave (amma), mudabbara, or umm walad, with the permission of her master, it is valid because the ownership of his person is established for her master, so it does not prevent validity. But if he married a free woman or a mukataba, the marriage is invalid, because if it were valid, ownership of his person would be established for the wife concurrently with the contract, and that is a corrupting factor if it occurs later; if it occurs concurrently, it is even more deserving to prevent its validity. If the slave were a mukatab or mudabbar, the marriage is valid because they do not permit the transfer from the ownership of their master, and the mahr is the value.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit discusses a situation where a master tells his male slave: 'Marry on your person (i.e., with yourself as the security or form of dower).' If the slave then marries a female slave (amma), mudabbara, or umm walad owned by another master with that master's permission, the marriage is valid. The reason given is that the ownership of the male slave's person (his 'raqaba') is transferred to the female's master upon marriage, which does not invalidate the marriage. However, if the slave marries a free woman or a mukataba, the marriage is invalid, because if it were valid, the free woman or mukataba would become the owner of the male slave's person at the moment of the contract, which is incompatible with marriage (a spouse cannot own the other's person). Such a concurrent ownership would corrupt the marriage from the outset, so it is prevented. But if the male slave himself is a mukatab or mudabbar, then the marriage is valid even with a free woman, because a mukatab or mudabbar's person is not transferable from the master's ownership (they are in the process of earning freedom), so no such conflict arises. In that case, the mahr is the value of the slave.

Key Terms
الرقبة (ar-raqaba): The person or physical entity of a slave; ownership of the raqaba means ownership of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage is valid.
B) The marriage is invalid.
C) The marriage is valid if the free woman consents.
D) The marriage is suspended until the slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a slave's marriage 'on his person' depends on whether the female spouse or her master acquires ownership of his raqaba, which must be avoided to preserve the integrity of the marriage.
