Master's Obligation to Provide Tabyi'ah for Slave Wife and Validity of Conditions
Commentary on the ruling that a master is not obliged to provide separate housing for his slave wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's obligation to provide tabyi'ah for a slave wife, and explain why a condition for tabyi'ah is void while a condition for freedom of children is valid, using the distinction between ta'liq and voluntary acts.

Core Idea
The master is not obliged to house a slave wife separately; a condition for it is void because it conflicts with his right to her service.

This lesson examines a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: when a master marries off his slave woman, he is not required to provide her with separate housing (tabyi'ah). The husband may only enjoy her when she is free from service. We will also explore why a stipulation for tabyi'ah is invalid, while a stipulation for freedom of her children is binding, revealing a fundamental distinction in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Locate the statement in the matn and commentary: the master is not obliged to provide tabyi'ah. Understand its scope: it applies to all slave women except the mukataba.
The ruling is absolute – the master retains the right to service despite marriage.
2
Understand the husband's right
The husband has milk al-hill (right to permissible enjoyment) but only when the slave is available. The commentary in al-Muhit clarifies that he may approach her when she is free from service, night or day.
The right is limited; the master's service takes priority.
3
Analyze the void condition for tabyi'ah
See why a stipulation for tabyi'ah is void: it would require a binding contract of ijarah or i'arah, both invalid (unknown duration in ijarah, non-binding in i'arah).
The condition does not affect the master's ongoing right to service.
4
Contrast with valid condition for freedom of children
Stipulating freedom of future children is valid because it is a ta'liq (suspension) – the freedom occurs automatically at birth, without a further voluntary act. The master's acceptance makes it binding.
The difference hinges on the nature of the condition: automatic legal consequence vs. dependent on a voluntary act.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical result: a condition for tabyi'ah is unenforceable; a condition for freedom of children is enforceable and the child becomes free at birth, even if the master dies or sells the slave before birth (with caveats).
This distinction avoids contradiction in the master's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن زوج أمته لا يجب عليه تبوأتها فتخدمه ويطؤها الزوج إن ظفر) لأن المولى في الاستخدام باق والتبوأة إبطال له فلما لم تلزمه يقال للزوج استوف منافع البضع إذا قدرت لأن حقه ثابت فيها. وفي المحيط : متى وجد فرصة وفراغها عن خدمة المولى ليلاً أو نهاراً يستمتع بها ا هـ. وظاهره أنه لو وجدها مشغولة بخدمة المولى في مكان خالٍ ليس له وطؤها وإنما يجوز له إذا لم تكن مشغولة بخدمة المولى ولم أره صريحاً. أطلق الأمة فشمل القنة والمدبرة وأم الولد فالكل في هذا الحكم سواء، ولا تدخل المكاتبة بقرينة قوله «فتخدمه» أي المولى لأن المكاتبة لا يملك المولى استخدامها فلذا تجب النفقة لها بدون التبوأة بخلاف غيرها فإنه إن بوأها منزلاً مع الزوج وجبت النفقة وإلا فلا لأنها جزاء الاحتباس. وأشار بإطلاق عدم وجوبها إلى أنه لو بوأها معه منزلاً ثم بداله أن يستخدمها له ذلك لأن الحق باق لبقاء الملك فلا يسقط بالتبوأة كما لا يسقط بالنكاح، وإلى أنه لو شرط تبوأتها للزوج وقت العقد كان الشرط باطلاً لا يمنعه من أن يستخدمها لأن المستحق للزوج ملك الحل لا غير لأن الشرط لو صح لا يخلو من أحد الأمرين : إما أن يكون بطريق الإجارة أو الإعارة، فلا يصح الأول لجهالة المدة، وكذا الثاني لأن الإعارة لا يتعلق بها اللزوم. فإن قلت : ما الفرق بين وبين أن يشترط الحر المتزوج بأمة رجل حرية أولاده حيث يلزم الشرط في هذه وتثبت حرية ما يأتي من الأولاد، وهذا أيضاً شرط لا يقتضيه نكاح الأمة؟ فالجواب أن قبول المولى الشرط والتزويج على اعتباره هو معنى تعليق الحرية بالولادة وتعليق ذلك صحيح، وعند وجود التعليق فيما يصح يمتنع الرجوع عن مقتضاه فتثبت الحرية عند الولادة جبراً من غير اختيار بخلاف اشتراط التبوأة فإن بتعليقها لا تقع هي عند ثبوت الشرط بل يتوقف وجودها على فعل حسي اختياري من فاعل مختار فإذا امتنع لم يوجد فالحاصل أن المعلق هنا وعد يجب الإيفاء به غير أنه إن لم يفِ به لا يثبت متعلقه أعني نفس الموعود به. كذا في فتح القدير . ومقتضاه أن السيد لو مات قبل وضع الجارية المشترط حرية أولادها لا يكون الولد حراً، وأن السيد لو باع هذه الجارية قبل الوضع يصح لأن المعلق قبل وجود شرطه عدم. وقد ذكر هذين الحكمين في المبسوط في مسألة التعليق صريحاً بقوله : كل ولد تلدينه فهو حرف فقال : لو مات المولى وهي حبلى لم يعتق ما تلده لفقد الملك لانتقالها للورثة، ولو باعها المولى وهي حبلى جاز بيعه، فإن ولدت بعده لم يعتق ذكره في باب عتق ما في البطن إلا أن يفرق بين التعليق صريحاً والتعليق معنى، ولم يظهر لي الآن. وذكره في المحيط في باب عتق ما تلده الأمة وقال . قوله (وبين أن يشترط الحر لتزوج كذا في الفتح، وظاهر أن العبد ليس كذلك مع أن ما يأتي جار فيه تأمل. ثم رأيت في شرح المقدسي ما نصه فرع : جعل محمد ولد العبد المغرور حراً بالقيمة كولد الحر المغرور لأن السبب الموجب لحريته الغرور، واشتراط الحرية عند النكاح وذا يتحقق في الرقيق كالحر وكما يحتاج الحر إلى حرية الولد فكذا المملوك بل حاجته أنه إذ ربما يتطرق به لحرية نفسه. توضيحه أنه لا عبرة بحرية الزوج ورقه في رق الولد بل المعتبر جانب الأم، وسقط اعتبار رقها في حق الولد عند اشتراط الحرية إذا كان الزوج حراً، فكذا لو كان عبداً وحكماً برقة لأنه خلق من ماء رقيقين لتفرع الولد من الأصل فيتصف بصفته فلا تثبت الحرية للولد من غير عتق . وأما إذا كان الزوج حراً فحرية الولد تثبت باتفاق الصحابة بخلاف القياس وتمامه فيه، والظاهر أن في العبارة سقطاً. والذي في الخانية والخلاصة وغيرهما التعبير برجل وهو شامل للحر والعبد. قوله (ولم يظهر لي (الآن أي الفرق المذكور ويمكن أن يفرق بأن التعليق الضمني في مسألتتنا لا يعامل معاملة التعليق الصريح لأن حرية الأولاد تعلق فيها حق الزوج وإذا تزوج المغرور أمة على أنها حرة فأولاده أحرار لأنه في المعنى شارط الحرية الأولاد والظاهر أن الأولاد أحرار وإن مات مولاها أو باعها ولا ينزل اشتراط الحرية صريحاً في مسألتنا عن اشتراطها معنى في مسألة المغرور لأن الزوج ملك بضعها بهذا الشرط فلا يفترق الحال بين بقائها على ملك المولى وانتقالها إلى غيره كالمكاتب فإنه
His saying: (And whoever marries off his slave woman, it is not obligatory upon him to provide her with separate housing (tabyi'ah), so she serves him and the husband may have intercourse with her if he finds an opportunity) because the master remains in use (of her service), and tabyi'ah is an annulment of that. Since it is not obligatory upon him, it is said to the husband: 'Take the benefits of the vulva if you are able, because your right is established in it.' In al-Muhit: 'Whenever he finds an opportunity and her being free from the master's service, night or day, he may enjoy her.' Its apparent meaning is that if he finds her occupied with the master's service in a vacant place, he does not have the right to have intercourse with her; it is only permissible for him if she is not occupied with the master's service, but I did not see it explicitly. He used the absolute term 'slave woman' so it includes the qinn, mudabbara, and umm walad; all are the same in this ruling. The mukataba is not included due to the context of his saying 'so she serves him', i.e., the master, because the master does not own the right to use the mukataba's service, thus maintenance is obligatory for her without tabyi'ah, unlike others; if he provides her a home with the husband, maintenance is obligatory, otherwise not, because it is the compensation for confinement. His absolute statement of non-obligation indicates that if he provided her a home with him (the husband) and then decided to use her for service, he has that right because the right remains due to the remaining ownership; it is not cancelled by tabyi'ah just as it is not cancelled by marriage. And it indicates that if he stipulated tabyi'ah for the husband at the time of the contract, the condition is void and does not prevent him from using her for service because what is due to the husband is only the right of permissible enjoyment (milk al-hill). Because if the condition were valid, it would be either by way of ijarah (hire) or i'arah (lend), and the first is invalid due to ignorance of the duration, and likewise the second because i'arah does not entail binding. If you say: What is the difference between this and when a free man marrying a slave woman stipulates freedom of her children, where the condition is binding and freedom of future children is established? This too is a condition not required by marriage of a slave woman. The answer is that the master's acceptance of the condition and marrying on its basis is the meaning of suspension (ta'liq) of freedom on childbirth, and such suspension is valid. When suspension exists in what is valid, retraction from its implication is prevented, so freedom is established at birth compulsorily without choice, unlike the stipulation of tabyi'ah, because its suspension does not occur upon existence of the condition; rather its existence depends on a physical voluntary act by a free agent; if he refrains, it does not exist. So the conclusion is that what is suspended here is a promise that must be fulfilled, but if he does not fulfill it, its object is not established. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Its implication is that if the master dies before the slave woman gives birth, the child is not free, and if the master sells her before birth, the sale is valid, because a suspended matter before its condition is non-existent. These two rulings are explicitly mentioned in al-Mabsut on the issue of suspension: 'Every child you give birth to is free.' He said: If the master dies while she is pregnant, the child does not become free due to loss of ownership as she transfers to heirs. And if the master sells her while pregnant, his sale is valid; if she gives birth after that, it is not freed. Mentioned in the chapter on freeing what is in the womb, unless a distinction is made between explicit suspension and implicit suspension, which is not clear to me now. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit in the chapter on freeing what the slave woman gives birth to. His saying: (And between a free man stipulating) such in al-Fath. Apparently a slave is not like that, although what comes is applicable and requires reflection. Then I saw in Sharh al-Maqdisi: A furu': Muhammad made the child of a deceived slave (al-maghrur) free with payment of its value, like the child of a deceived free man, because the cause necessitating its freedom is the deception, and stipulating freedom at marriage, and this is realized for a slave as for a free man. Just as a free man needs freedom of the child, so does a slave, rather his need is greater because he might thereby seek freedom for himself. Explanation: There is no consideration of the husband's freedom or slavery in the slavery of the child; rather the mother's side is considered. Her slavery is disregarded for the child when freedom is stipulated if the husband is free. So also if he is a slave, the child is ruled as a slave because it is created from the water of two slaves, so the child originates from the origin and takes on its attribute, thus freedom is not established for the child without manumission. But if the husband is free, the child's freedom is established by agreement of the Companions, contrary to qiyas. The full discussion is in it. Apparently there is an omission in the expression. The expression in al-Khaniyah and al-Khulasah and others is 'man' (rajul) which includes free and slave. His saying: (And it is not clear to me now) i.e., the aforementioned distinction. It can be distinguished by saying that implicit suspension in our case is not treated like explicit suspension, because the freedom of children involves the right of the husband, and when a deceived man marries a slave woman thinking she is free, his children are free because he is in effect stipulating freedom of the children. Apparently the children are free even if her master dies or sells her, and the explicit stipulation of freedom in our case is not inferior to implicit stipulation in the case of the deceived, because the husband owns her vulva by this condition, so the situation does not differ between her remaining in the master's ownership and her transfer to another, like a mukatab, for...
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the marriage of a slave woman. It addresses the master's obligation—or lack thereof—to provide separate housing (tabyi'ah) for a slave wife, and the husband's right to sexual intercourse with her despite her ongoing service to the master.

The commentator begins by affirming that the master is not obliged to provide tabyi'ah because his right to her service remains after marriage. Providing separate housing would annul that right, and since the master is not obliged to do so, the husband is told to take advantage of opportunities for intimacy when she is free from service. The commentator, citing al-Muhit, specifies that the husband may enjoy her whenever he finds an opportunity, whether at night or day, but it appears he cannot do so if she is engaged in the master's service in a vacant place—though the commentator has not seen this explicitly stated.

The ruling applies to all types of slave women: qinn (full slave), mudabbara (slave promised freedom upon master's death), and umm walad (slave mother of her master's child). The mukataba (slave who has made a contract of manumission) is excluded because the master does not own her service; hence maintenance is obligatory for her even without tabyi'ah, unlike others where maintenance depends on the master providing housing.

If the master voluntarily provides housing but later wishes to use the slave for service, he may do so because his right remains. Similarly, if a condition for tabyi'ah is stipulated at the time of marriage, that condition is void because the husband only owns the right to permissible enjoyment, not actual possession. The condition would have to be either an ijarah (hire) or i'arah (lend), both invalid: ijarah due to unknown duration, and i'arah because it is non-binding.

The commentator then contrasts this with the condition that a free man marrying a slave woman may stipulate that her children be free. That condition is valid because it is a suspension (ta'liq) of freedom on the event of childbirth, and such suspension is effective. Freedom is automatically established at birth without any further action. The difference lies in the nature of the condition: tabyi'ah requires a voluntary act (moving out), while freedom of children is a legal consequence that occurs automatically when the condition (childbirth) occurs.

The discussion then delves into the implications of such suspension: if the master dies before birth, the child is not free; if he sells the slave before birth, the sale is valid. These rulings are found in al-Mabsut. The commentator notes a potential distinction between explicit and implicit suspension but admits it is not clear to him.

The passage ends with a reference to a ruling by Imam Muhammad regarding the deceived slave (al-maghrur), where the child of a slave is freed upon payment of value, analogous to a free man. The explanation emphasizes that the husband's status matters; in stipulating freedom, the mother's side is considered, and if the husband is a slave, the child remains a slave unless explicitly freed. The commentator suggests that the expression 'rajul' in al-Khaniyah and al-Khulasah is general and includes both free and slave men.

Overall, this passage provides a detailed exposition of the rights and obligations in the marriage of slaves, particularly focusing on the validity of conditions and the distinction between explicit and implicit legal suspensions.

Key Terms
تبوأة (tabyi'ah): Providing a separate residence for the slave wife away from the master's house, which would exempt her from service.
تعليق (ta'liq): A legal suspension where a ruling or event is made conditional upon the occurrence of another event.
ملك الحل (milk al-hill): The right of the husband to permissible sexual enjoyment of his wife, as opposed to actual possession.
إجارة (ijarah): A contract of hire or lease.
إعارة (i'arah): A contract of lending for free use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master marries off his slave woman and stipulates that she must be provided with separate housing (tabyi'ah), what is the ruling on that condition?
A) The condition is valid and the master must provide housing.
B) The condition is void and does not bind the master.
C) The condition is valid only if the slave is a mukataba.
D) The condition is valid for umm walad but not for qinn.

Key Takeaway
The master is not obliged to provide tabyi'ah; a condition for it is void, while a condition for freedom of children is valid due to the difference between a legal suspension (ta'liq) and a voluntary act.

The Invalidity of the Condition of Tabwi'ah in Slave Marriage
Expansion of a matn ruling with qiyas and comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the condition of tabwi'ah and derive it using qiyas, distinguishing it from valid ta'liq conditions.

Core Idea
A condition preventing the master from using his slave woman is invalid because it cannot be classified as a valid hire or loan.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why a condition in a slave marriage that requires the master to hand over the slave woman and not use her is legally invalid. The commentator uses qiyas to show that the condition would have to be either a hire (ijarah) or a loan (i'arah), both of which fail. He then contrasts this with the valid condition that children of a free man marrying a slave woman be free, which operates through the valid mechanism of ta'liq (suspension).

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its legal problem
The condition is that the master will not use his slave woman (tabwi'ah). The Hanafi school rules this condition invalid (batil). Why? Because it conflicts with the master's ownership rights, and the husband only has the right to sexual enjoyment, not to restrict the master's use of his property.
2
Analyze the condition via qiyas: classify it as hire or loan
The condition must be analyzed legally. It could be a hire (ijarah) if the master is renting out the slave woman's services for a fee, but the duration is unknown, making it invalid. Alternatively, it could be a loan (i'arah) of services, but loans are not binding (lazim), so the condition cannot compel the master. Therefore, the condition cannot stand.
In Hanafi i'arah, the lender may revoke at any time, so a condition to abstain from use cannot be enforced.
3
Compare with the valid condition of freeing children
A free man marrying a slave woman can validly stipulate that his children will be free. This is a ta'liq (suspension) of freedom upon birth, which is a valid legal device. When the condition occurs (birth), freedom automatically follows by law, not dependent on a voluntary act.
Ta'liq is different from tabwi'ah because tabwi'ah requires a voluntary act of handing over, which the master may refuse.
4
Understand the subsidiary rulings
If the master dies or sells the slave woman before birth, the condition of freeing children does not take effect because the suspended matter is non-existent before the condition. This shows that implicit suspension (ta'liq ma'nawi) is weaker than explicit.
The passage cites al-Mabsut for these rulings.
5
Define tabwi'ah terminologically
Tabwi'ah means the master leaves the slave woman alone with her husband and refrains from using her. According to al-Khassaf, it requires actual handover and abstention. Mere permission to come and go while still using her is not tabwi'ah. The true criterion is that she be in her husband's house at night; daytime use does not harm.
The master may also travel with her, and the husband cannot prevent him.

Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling about a condition in the marriage of a slave woman. The matn likely stated that if a master marries his slave woman to a man and stipulates that he will not use her (i.e., not take her for service), the condition is invalid. The commentator explains why: the husband only has the right to sexual enjoyment (ملك الحل), not ownership of her services. A condition restricting the master's right to use his own property would be either a hire (ijarah) or a loan (i'arah), both of which are invalid here—the former due to unknown duration, the latter because a loan is not binding. The author then compares this to the case where a free man marries a slave woman with the condition that his children will be free. That condition is valid because it amounts to a suspending (ta'liq) of freedom upon birth, which is a valid legal mechanism. When the condition is fulfilled (the birth), freedom occurs automatically by operation of law, not dependent on a voluntary act. In contrast, tabwi'ah (the master giving the slave woman to her husband) requires a voluntary act of handing her over, which the master may refuse; therefore the condition cannot be enforced. The passage further discusses subsidiary rulings: if the master dies or sells the slave woman before birth, the condition of freedom for children does not take effect, showing that implicit suspension (ta'liq ma'nawi) is weaker than explicit. The text then defines tabwi'ah terminologically: according to al-Khassaf, it is the master leaving the slave woman alone with her husband and refraining from using her; mere permission to come and go while still using her is not tabwi'ah. The true criterion is that she be in her husband's house at night; daytime use does not harm. The master may also travel with her, and the husband cannot prevent him.

Key Terms
التبوأة (al-tabwi'ah): The master leaving the slave woman alone with her husband and refraining from using her, i.e., handing her over to him for marital life.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending a legal consequence (like freedom) upon the occurrence of a future event (like birth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the condition that the master will not use his slave woman invalid in Hanafi law?
A) Because the master cannot sell the slave woman after marriage.
B) Because it would require a separate contract of hire or loan, which are invalid due to unknown duration or non-binding nature.
C) Because the husband has the right to her services, not just enjoyment.
D) Because the condition contradicts the nature of marriage.

Key Takeaway
A condition in a slave marriage that prevents the master from using his slave woman is invalid because it cannot be sustained as a valid hire or loan; the master retains his property rights.

Master's Right to Compel Slaves to Marry (Ijbar)
Ruling on master's ijbar over slaves for marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's authority to compel his slaves to marry, identify the exceptions, and explain the legal basis of the ruling.

Core Idea
The master may compel both male and female slaves to marry, except the mukatab and minor slave girl.

This passage addresses the master's authority to compel his slaves into marriage. The Hanafi school permits the master to enforce a marriage contract on both male and female slaves without their consent, based on the principle that marriage protects the master's property from harm. However, there are important exceptions and a reported difference within the school regarding the male slave. We will explore the ruling, its basis, and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruled Act and Its Meaning
The act is the master's compulsion (ijbar) of his male and female slave to marry. Recognize that ijbar here means the master can conclude the marriage contract without the slave's consent, not physical coercion.
The commentator explicitly distinguishes between enforcing the contract and forcing by beating.
2
Note the Intra-Hanafi Difference
There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that the master cannot compel the male slave. The relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that he can. Understand the reasoning: marriage is a human right vs. it reforms the master's property.
The majority view uses qiyas (analogy) to the female slave.
3
Apply the Principle of Complete Ownership
The authority of ijbar depends on complete ownership, not the degree of slavery. Thus, it applies to qinn, mudabbar, and umm walad, but not to the mukatab or minor slave girl because they have free status in disposition.
The mukatab is like a free person in financial matters.
4
Identify the Exceptions
The master cannot compel the mukatab (male or female) and the minor slave girl (saghira). For the mukatab, consent is required; for the minor girl, the master's authority is limited.
The commentator mentions that the small slave girl is excluded because she is not fully owned in the sense of disposition.
5
Understand the Legal Basis (Dalil Aqli)
The ruling is based on analogical reasoning (qiyas): just as the master can give his female slave in marriage (because he owns the benefits of her sexual organ), he can also give his male slave in marriage because it reforms his property and protects from zina.
The text states: 'Our view: Giving in marriage is reforming his property... by analogy to the female slave.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وله إجبارهما على النكاح أي للسيد إجبار العبد والأمة عليه بمعنى تنفيذ النكاح عليهما وإن لم يرضيا لا أن يحملهما على النكاح بضرب أو نحوه. وعن أبي حنيفة أنه لا إجبار في العبد لأن النكاح من خصائص الآدمية والعبد داخل تحت ملك المولى من حيث إنه مال فلا يملك إنكاحه بخلاف الأمة لأنه مالك لمنافع بضعها فيملك تمليكها. ولنا أن الإنكاح إصلاح ملكه لأن فيه تحصينه عن الزنا الذي هو سبب الهلاك والنقصان فيملكه اعتباراً بالأمة. أطلقهما فشمل الصغير والكبير والصغيرة والكبيرة والقن والمدبر وأم الولد لأن الملك في الكل كامل وخرج المكاتب والمكاتبة والصغيرة فليس له إجبارهما عليه، صغيرين كانا أو كبيرين لأنهما التحقا بالأحرار تصرفاً فيشترط رضاهما. فالحاصل أن ولاية الإجبار في المملوك تعتمد كمال الملك لا كمال الرق، والملك كامل في المدير وأم الولد وإن في معنى المعلق عتقه على الأداء ولا يبطل هذا التعليق المعنوي بموت المعلق قوله (وهذا أشبه بمسألتنا أي لأن فيه تعليق حرية أولا الغير من أمة المعلق قوله (سقط مهرها) أي إن كان البيع قبل الوطء بقرينة قوله كما سيأتي الخ) قوله (وفي الاصطلاح الخ) قال في النهر : اعلم أنه لا بد في المعنى العرفي من التقييد بدفعها إليه كما ذكره بعضهم والاكتفاء بالتخلية كما ظن بعضهم غير واقع، وتسليمها إليه بعد استيفاء الصداق واجب بمقتضى العقد وذلك بالتخلية والتبوأة أمر زائد عليها وإقدام المولى على هذا لا يستلزم رضاه بها بل بمجرد إطلاق وطئه إياها متى ظفر يتوفر مقتضاه. كذا في الفتح. وهو ظاهر في أن هذا القول كافٍ في التسليم وبه صرح في الدراية حيث قال : التبوأة قدر زائد على التسليم ليتحقق بدونها بأن قيل متى ظفرت بها وطئتها. وما في البحر من بعد استيفاء الصداق يؤمر بأن يدخلها على زوجها معناه أنه يسلمها إليه اهـ. وهو أولى مما جمع به المقدسي في شرحه بين ما في الدراية وبين ما ذكره المؤلف عن المبسوط بأن المراد بالمنفي التبوية أنه المستمرة .
His saying: 'And he has the right to compel them to marry' means the master has the authority to compel his male slave and female slave to marry, i.e., to enforce the marriage upon them even if they are not satisfied, not that he forces them to marry by beating or the like. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that there is no compulsion (ijbar) regarding the male slave because marriage is a characteristic of humanity, and the male slave falls under the master's ownership as property, so he does not have the authority to give him in marriage, unlike the female slave because he owns the benefits of her sexual organ, so he has the authority to give her in marriage. Our view: Giving in marriage is reforming his property because it protects him from zina, which is a cause of destruction and loss, so he owns it by analogy to the female slave. He made it general, including young and old, male and female, the qinn, mudabbar, and umm walad because ownership in all is complete. Exception is made for the mukatab and mukatabah, and the minor female; he does not have the right to compel them, whether they are minors or adults, because they have joined the free in terms of disposition, so their consent is required. So the conclusion: The authority of compulsion (ijbar) over a slave depends on complete ownership, not complete slavery. Ownership is complete in the mudabbar and umm walad, even though they are in the meaning of one whose freedom is suspended upon payment, and this implicit suspension is not nullified by the death of the one who suspended. His saying: 'This is more similar to our issue' means because it involves suspending the freedom of the child of another from a slave woman of the one who suspended. His saying: 'Her mahr is forfeited' means if the sale was before intercourse, as indicated by his saying 'as will come'. His saying: 'And in terminology' etc. He said in al-Nahr: Know that in the customary sense, it must be restricted to handing her over to him as some mentioned, and mere takhliyah as some thought is not real. Handing her over after collecting the dower is obligatory by virtue of the contract; that is by takhliyah, and tabwi'ah is something additional. The master's undertaking this does not require his consent to it; rather, by merely allowing him to have intercourse with her whenever he finds her, its requirement is fulfilled. Thus in al-Fath. It is apparent that this statement is sufficient for delivery, and it is explicitly stated in al-Dirayah: 'Tabwi'ah is an additional amount beyond delivery; it can exist without it, as said: whenever you find her, have intercourse with her.' What is in al-Bahr: 'After collecting the dower, he is ordered to bring her into her husband,' meaning he hands her over to him. End. It is better than what al-Maqdisi combined in his commentary between what is in al-Dirayah and what the author mentioned from al-Mabsut: that the intended negation is the continuous tabwi'ah.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about the master's authority to compel his slaves to marry (ijbar). The commentator explains that the master has the power to enforce a marriage contract upon his male and female slaves even without their consent, but this does not mean physical coercion; it means he can conclude the marriage on their behalf. There is a reported view from Abu Hanifa that there is no compulsion for male slaves because they are considered property and marriage is a human right, but the preferred view (of the madhhab) is that the master can compel both because it is a reform of his property (إصلاح ملكه) as it protects the slave from zina, which would harm the master's property. The ruling applies to all types of slaves: young or old, male or female, qinn (full slave), mudabbar (one whose freedom is postponed upon the master's death), and umm walad (slave mother of the master's child). The exception is the mukatab (slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) and the minor slave girl; the master cannot compel them because they have the status of free persons in terms of disposition. The key principle: the authority of ijbar depends on complete ownership, not complete slavery. The mudabbar and umm walad, though their freedom is suspended, remain under complete ownership until the event. The passage also clarifies that the master's right to use the slave woman (even if he does not formally hand her over) is sufficient to satisfy the marriage contract; tabwi'ah (handing over) is an additional act beyond mere delivery. The death of the master does not nullify the implicit suspension for umm walad.

Key Terms
الإجبار (al-ijbar): The master's legal authority to enforce a marriage contract upon his male or female slave even without their consent.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) to purchase his freedom by paying installments; he is excluded from ijbar because he is treated as free in disposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which category of slave can the master NOT compel to marry according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) The qinn (full slave)
B) The mudabbar
C) The mukatab
D) The umm walad

Key Takeaway
The master can compel both male and female slaves to marry, except the mukatab and minor slave girl, based on complete ownership and reform of property.

Definition and Ruling of Tabyi'ah (Handing Over Slave Wife)
Defines 'tabyi'ah' and clarifies the master's duties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tabyi'ah technically according to al-Khassaf and state the master's obligations and retained rights.

Core Idea
Tabyi'ah: master hands over slave wife and stops nighttime service, enabling marital life.

In this passage, the commentator defines the technical term 'tabyi'ah' — the act of handing over a slave wife to her husband after the dower is received. It distinguishes between the master's duty to vacate her for her husband and his freedom to employ her during the day. Understanding this term is essential to know the rights of the husband and the limits of the master's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic origin
The word 'tabyi'ah' is derived from 'bo'atahu manzilan', meaning to provide a dwelling. However, in legal usage, it has a specific definition.
Linguistic meaning differs from technical meaning.
2
State the technical definition per al-Khassaf
Al-Khassaf defines tabyi'ah as the master vacating the slave woman for her husband, handing her over, and not using her for service.
This is the core definition.
3
Explain the condition for tabyi'ah
The master is ordered to hand her over after he receives the dower (the husband pays the dower to the master).
The master is not obliged to provide a separate dwelling.
4
Identify the criterion for valid tabyi'ah
The key criterion is that she stays in the husband's house at night. Daytime employment by the master does not invalidate tabyi'ah.
Nighttime residence is what matters.
5
Note the master's retained right
The master may travel with the slave wife, and the husband cannot prevent him.
This shows the master's ongoing authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالتبوأة لأن المولى إذا استوفى صداقها أمر أن يدخلها على زوجها وإن لم يلزمه أن يبوأها. كذا في المبسوط . ولذا قال في المحيط : لو باعها بحيث لا يقدر الزوج عليها مهرها كما سيأتي في مسألة ما إذا قتلها. والتبوأة مصدر بوأته منزلاً وبوأته له إذا أسكنته إياه. وفي الاصطلاح على ما ذكره الخصاف أن يخلي المولى بين الأمة وزوجها ويدفعها إليه ولا يستخدمها، أما إذا كانت تذهب وتجيء وتخدم مولاها لا تكون تبوأة، وسيأتي في النفقات إن شاء الله تعالى، وإن التحقيق أن العبرة لكونها في بيت الزوج ليلاً ولا يضر الاستخدام نهاراً وأشار المصنف إلى أن للمولى أن يسافر بها وليس للزوج منعه كما في الظهيرية .
And it is restricted by tabyi'ah because when the master has received her dower, he is ordered to make her enter to her husband, even if he is not obliged to give her a dwelling (meaning: hand her over). Thus in al-Mabsut. And therefore it is said in al-Muhit: If he sells her in a way that the husband cannot reach her, her dower falls, as will come in the issue of when he kills her. And tabyi'ah is the verbal noun from 'bo'atahu manzilan' and 'bo'atahu lahu' when you make him reside in it. And in the technical sense, according to what al-Khassaf mentioned, it is that the master vacates the slave woman for her husband and hands her over to him and does not employ her. As for when she goes and comes and serves her master, it is not tabyi'ah. And it will come in the chapter on maintenance, God willing. And the investigation is that the consideration is that she be in the husband's house at night, and daytime employment does not harm. And the author indicated that the master may travel with her and the husband has no right to prevent him, as in al-Zuhayriyya.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and ruling regarding 'tabyi'ah' — the act of handing over a slave wife to her husband. The author explains that after the master receives the dower (or the husband pays it), the master is required to make the slave woman available to her husband, though he is not obliged to provide her with a separate dwelling (tabyi'ah in the sense of housing). The technical definition from al-Khassaf is that the master vacates the slave woman for her husband, hands her over, and does not employ her in his service. If she continues to come and go and serve the master, it is not considered tabyi'ah. Later, the author notes that the actual criterion is that she be in the husband's house at night; daytime service does not prevent tabyi'ah. Additionally, the master retains the right to travel with the slave woman, and the husband cannot prevent it.

Key Terms
تبوأة (tabyi'ah): The master's act of handing over the slave wife to her husband and ceasing to use her for nighttime service, thereby enabling marital life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the technical meaning of 'tabyi'ah' according to al-Khassaf?
A) The master employs the slave woman part-time
B) The master vacates the slave woman for her husband, hands her over, and does not employ her
C) The master sells the slave woman
D) The master travels with the slave woman

Key Takeaway
Tabyi'ah is the master's obligation to hand over the slave wife and refrain from nighttime employment, but he may use her during the day and travel with her.

Master's Authority to Compel Slaves into Marriage
Ruling with proof on master's ijbar over slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's authority to compel slaves into marriage, identify the evidence and analogy used, and determine which categories of slaves are subject to compulsion.

Core Idea
The master's authority to compel marriage is based on complete ownership, not complete slavery.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi position on ijbar (compulsion) in the marriage of slaves. It defines ijbar as executing the contract on behalf of the slave without consent, not physical coercion. The passage presents the proof and analogy supporting the master's right over both male and female slaves, addresses an intra-Hanafi difference, and outlines the scope and exceptions based on the principle of complete ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Definition
Read the opening sentence: the master has the right to compel both male and female slaves to marriage. Note that compulsion means executing the contract without the slave's consent, not physical force.
This distinguishes between legal authority and coercion.
2
Locate the Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Find the report from Abu Hanifa that there is no compulsion for male slaves. Understand the reasoning: marriage is a human characteristic, and the male slave is property.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the opposite.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Proof and Qiyas
The proof: marrying off the slave improves the master's property by preventing adultery, which causes loss. This is applied to both sexes via qiyas on the female slave.
The illa (effective cause) is the master's interest in protecting his property.
4
Trace the Scope and Exceptions
The ruling applies to all slaves with complete ownership: qinn, mudabbir, and umm walad. Exceptions: mukatab (and mukataba) because their kitaba contract grants autonomy; also minors (free children) are not subject.
Exceptions are based on incomplete ownership or lack of ownership.
5
Derive the Underlying Principle
The text concludes: the authority of ijbar depends on completeness of ownership (kamil al-milk), not completeness of slavery (kamil al-riqq). This explains why mudabbir and umm walad are included (ownership complete) but mukatab is not (ownership incomplete).
This principle also governs other rulings like intercourse, expiation, and sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وله إجبارهما على النكاح أي للسيد إجبار العبد والأمة عليه بمعنى تنفيذ النكاح عليهما وإن لم يرضيا لا أن يحملهما على النكاح بضرب أو نحوه. وعن أبي حنيفة أنه لا إجبار في العبد لأن النكاح من خصائص الآدمية والعبد داخل تحت ملك المولى من حيث إنه مال فلا يملك إنكاحه بخلاف الأمة لأنه مالك لمنافع بضعها فيملك تمليكها. ولنا أن الإنكاح إصلاح ملكه لأن فيه تحصينه عن الزنا الذي هو سبب الهلاك والنقصان فيملكه اعتباراً بالأمة. أطلقهما فشمل الصغير والكبير والصغيرة والكبيرة والقن والمدبر وأم الولد لأن الملك في الكل كامل وخرج المكاتب والمكاتبة والصغيرة فليس له إجبارهما عليه، صغيرين كانا أو كبيرين لأنهما التحقا بالأحرار تصرفاً فيشترط رضاهما. فالحاصل أن ولاية الإجبار في المملوك تعتمد كمال الملك لا كمال الرق، والملك كامل في المدير وأم الولد وإن كان الرق ناقصاً، والمكاتب على عكسهما ولذا دخلا تحت قوله كل مملوك أملكه فهو حردونه . وحل وطء أم الولد دون المكاتبة لأنه يعتمد كمال الملك فقط ولم يجز عتقهما عن الكفارة لأنها تبتني على كمال الرق، وأما البيع فإنه يعتمد كمالهما فلم يجز بيع الكل.
His statement: (He has the right to compel both of them to marriage) means the master has the right to compel the male slave and female slave to marriage in the sense of executing the marriage contract upon them even if they are not pleased, not that he forces them into marriage by beating or the like. And it is reported from Abu Hanifa that there is no compulsion regarding the male slave because marriage is a characteristic of humanity and the male slave falls under the master's ownership as property, so he does not have the authority to marry him off, unlike the female slave because he owns the usufruct of her private part, so he owns the right to give her in marriage. Our proof is that marrying off is an improvement of his property because it protects him from adultery which is a cause of destruction and deficiency, so he has the authority by analogy to the female slave. He made it absolute so it includes young and old, male and female, the outright slave, the mudabbir, and the umm walad because ownership is complete in all. Exception is made for the mukatab and mukataba and the minor, so he has no right to compel them – whether they are young or old – because they have joined the free in disposition, so their consent is required. The conclusion is that the authority of compulsion over a slave depends on completeness of ownership, not completeness of slavery, and ownership is complete in the mudabbir and umm walad even though their slavery is incomplete, and the mukatab is the opposite of them, so they are included under his statement: 'Every slave I own is free.' And the permissibility of intercourse with the umm walad but not with the mukataba because it depends only on complete ownership, and it is not permissible to emancipate them as expiation because expiation is based on complete slavery, and as for sale, it depends on completeness of both, so it is not permissible to sell any of them.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on the master's right to compel his slaves into marriage (ijbar). The author clarifies that the master's authority is to execute the marriage contract on behalf of the slave even without the slave's consent, not to physically force them. He then presents an opposing view attributed to Abu Hanifa that there is no compulsion for male slaves because they are property and not owners of their own persons, unlike female slaves where the master owns their private parts. The author provides the Hanafi proof that marrying off the slave is an improvement (istislah) of the master's property by preventing adultery, which would diminish the slave's value. By analogy (qiyas) to the female slave, the male slave is included. The ruling applies to all types of slaves—young, old, qinn, mudabbir, umm walad—because the master has complete ownership in all these cases. Exceptions are the mukatab (slave contracted for manumission) and the minor (who is not a slave? Actually the text says 'al-saghira' meaning the minor girl who is free? Context: the minor girl who is a slave? The passage says 'kharaja al-mukatab wa al-mukataba wa al-saghira' meaning the mukatab, mukataba, and the minor (girl) are excluded. This is because they are like free persons in disposition, so their consent is required. The key principle is that the authority of compulsion depends on complete ownership (kamil al-milk), not complete slavery (kamil al-riqq). Hence the mudabbir and umm walad, though their slavery is incomplete, are still subject to compulsion because ownership is complete. Conversely, the mukatab has incomplete ownership (the master's ownership is incomplete due to the kitaba), so compulsion does not apply. The passage then contrasts rulings on intercourse, expiation, and sale to illustrate the principle.

Key Terms
إجبار (ijbar): The master's legal authority to compel his slave to marry without the slave's consent.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with the master to earn and pay for his freedom.
تبوأة (tabyi'ah): The act of the master handing over the slave wife to her husband and vacating her for marital life, typically by providing a separate dwelling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following slaves is the master NOT allowed to compel into marriage?
A) A male outright slave (qinn)
B) A female mudabbira
C) A male mukatab
D) An umm walad

Key Takeaway
The master's authority to compel marriage (ijbar) applies to slaves with complete ownership, regardless of the completeness of their slavery, but is unavailable for mukatab slaves whose ownership is incomplete.

Marriage of Minor Mukataba: A Curious Hanafi Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute over the effect of manumission on a minor mukataba's marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the marriage of a minor mukataba after manumission and evaluate Ibn al-Humam's critique.

Core Idea
A minor mukataba's marriage remains suspended on the master's acceptance even after she becomes free — a paradoxical strengthening of the master's control.

This lesson examines a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute about a minor mukataba (a slave under contract of manumission) who is married off by her master. The ruling, reported in al-Muhit, produces a paradoxical outcome: the more she distances herself from the master by becoming free, the closer he becomes to having power over her marriage. The 'verifier' Ibn al-Humam contested this ruling, leading to a debate that tests the boundaries of qiyas and the authority of transmitted texts.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling from Al-Muhit
Identify the core ruling: If a master marries off his minor mukataba, the marriage is suspended on her acceptance. If she does not accept and later becomes free (by paying the kitaba), the marriage becomes suspended on the master's acceptance, because she is still a minor and cannot accept.
The suspension shifts from her acceptance to the master's acceptance after manumission.
2
Analyze the Paradox
Note the paradoxical consequences: (a) the master can force the marriage after manumission but not before, and (b) if the mukataba is later returned to slavery, the marriage becomes void even if the master accepts it.
This 'wonder' arises from the intersection of kitaba, minority, and guardianship.
3
Present Ibn al-Humam's Critique
Understand Ibn al-Humam's objection: by analogy to a slave who marries without permission and is then emancipated (the marriage becomes effective), the mukataba's marriage should become effective immediately upon manumission, without requiring the master's acceptance.
He argues that the master's guardianship is permanent and the kitaba-based suspension has ended.
4
Examine the Hanafi Response
The author defends Imam Muhammad's position: the case is explicitly stated in al-Jami' al-Kabir, so it cannot be dismissed. Furthermore, the master's compulsion (ijbar) is distinct from the slave's own action; the minor's incapacity persists.
The response rejects the qiyas as invalid due to a difference in the effective cause.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Imam Muhammad: the marriage remains suspended on the master's acceptance after manumission. This is the view adopted by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and is considered the operative ruling for fatwa.
Intra-Hanafi disputes are resolved by reference to the primary sources (Zahir al-Riwayah) and the later tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط وغيره : المولى إذا زوج مكاتبته الصغيرة توقف النكاح على إجازتها لأنها ملحقة بالبالغة فيما يبتنى على الكتابة، ثم إنها لو لم ترد حتى أدت فعتقت بقي النكاح موقوفاً على إجازة المولى لا إجازتها لأنها بعد العتق لم تبق مكاتبة وهي صغيرة والصغيرة ليست من أهل الإجازة فاعتبر التوقف على إجازتها حال رقها ولم يعتبر بعد العتق . قالوا : وهذه المسألة من أعجب المسائل فإنها مهما زادت من المولى بعداً ازدادت إليه قرباً في النكاح فإنه يملك إلزام النكاح عليها بعد العتق لا قبله . وأعجب منه أنها لو ردت إلى الرق يبطل النكاح الذي باشره المولى وإن أجازه المولى لأنه طرأ حل بات على موقوف فأبطله إلا أن هذا كله ثبت بالدليل وهو يعمل العجائب. وقد بحث المحقق في فتح القدير بأن الذي يقتضيه النظر عدم التوقف على إجازة المولى بعد العتق بل بمجرد عتقها ينفذ النكاح لما صرحوا به من أنه إذا تزوج العبد بغير إذن سيده فأعتقه نفذ لأنه لو توقف فإما على إجازة المولى وهو ممتنع لانتفاء ولايته، وإما على العبد فلا وجه له لأنه صدر من جهته فكيف يتوقف عليه؟ ولأنه كان نافذاً من جهته. وإنما توقف على السيد. فكذا السيد هنا فإنه ولي مجبر، وإنما التوقف على إذنها لعقد الكتابة وقد زال فبقي النفاذ من جهة السيد. وهذا هو الوجه وكثيراً ما يقلد الساهون الساهين وهذا بخلاف الصبي إذا زوج نفسه بغير إذن وليه فإنه موقوف على إجازة وليه ، فلو بلغ قبل أن يرده لا ينفذ حتى يجيزه الصبي لأن العقد حين صدر منه لم يكن نافذاً من جهته إذ لا نفاذ حالة الصبا، أو عدم أهلية الرأي بخلاف العبد ومولى المكاتبة الصغيرة والحاصل أن الصغير والصغيرة ليسا من أهل العبارة بخلاف البالغ اهـ . وجوابه أنه سوء أدب وغلط ; أما الأول فلأن المسألة صرح بها الإمام محمد في الجامع الكبير فكيف ينسب السهو إليه وإلى مقلديه؟ وأما الثاني فلأن محمداً علل لتوقفه على إجازة المولى بأنه وسوء أدب وغلط
And in al-Muhit and others: If the master marries off his minor mukataba, the marriage is suspended on her acceptance because she is treated like a mature woman in matters based on kitaba. Then if she does not respond until she pays and becomes free, the marriage remains suspended on the acceptance of the master, not her acceptance, because after manumission she is no longer a mukataba and she is a minor, and a minor is not qualified to give acceptance. So suspension was considered on her acceptance while she was a slave, and it was not considered after manumission. They said: This is one of the most astonishing cases: the more she distances herself from the master, the closer he comes to her in marriage, for he can force the marriage on her after manumission but not before. Even more astonishing: if she is returned to slavery, the marriage that the master himself performed is invalidated, even if the master accepts it, because a valid condition (hilla) intervened upon a suspended contract and invalidated it. But all this is established by evidence, and evidence works wonders. And the verifier (Ibn al-Humam) in Fath al-Qadir investigated that what is required by consideration is that there should be no suspension on the master's acceptance after manumission; rather, upon her manumission, the marriage becomes effective immediately, because they have stated that if a slave marries without his master's permission and then the master emancipates him, the marriage takes effect. Because if it were suspended, it would either be on the master's acceptance – which is impossible due to the absence of his guardianship, or on the slave – which has no basis because the marriage originated from him, so how could it be suspended on him? And because it was effective from his side; it was only suspended on the master. Similarly, here the master is a compelling guardian (wali mujbir), and the suspension was only on her permission due to the kitaba contract, which has ceased, so the effect from the master's side remains. This is the correct view, and many follow the negligent. This is unlike a minor boy who marries himself without his guardian's permission, for it is suspended on his guardian's acceptance. If he reaches puberty before the guardian rejects it, it does not take effect until the minor himself accepts it because the contract when issued from him was not effective from his side, since there is no effectiveness during minority, or lack of capacity of opinion, unlike the slave and the master of the minor mukataba. The conclusion is that minors are not qualified to express valid consent, unlike adults. Then the answer: it is rudeness and error. As for the first, Imam Muhammad explicitly states this case in al-Jami' al-Kabir, so how can negligence be attributed to him and his followers? As for the second, because Muhammad provided the rationale for its suspension on the master's acceptance, that it is ... rudeness and error.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the marriage of a minor mukataba (a slave girl under contract of manumission) given in marriage by her master. According to the position reported in al-Muhit, if the master marries off his minor mukataba, the marriage is suspended (mawquf) on her acceptance while she is still a slave, because in matters related to the kitaba contract she is treated like an adult. However, if she does not accept and then pays off her kitaba and becomes free, the marriage becomes suspended on the master's acceptance, not hers, because after manumission she is no longer a mukataba and she is still a minor, and a minor cannot validly accept. This leads to a paradoxical result: the more she distances herself from the master (by becoming free), the closer he becomes to having power over her marriage. Even more paradoxically, if she is later returned to slavery (e.g., by option of redemption), the marriage performed by the master becomes void, even if the master accepts it. The 'verifier' (Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir) critiques this ruling, arguing by analogy (qiyas) that upon manumission, the marriage should take effect immediately without needing the master's acceptance, just as when a slave marries without permission and is then emancipated, the marriage becomes effective. He argues that the master's guardianship remains, and the suspension on her permission due to kitaba has ceased. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq responds, defending Imam Muhammad's position and accusing Ibn al-Humam of rudeness and error. The defense notes that Imam Muhammad explicitly stated this in al-Jami' al-Kabir, and thus it cannot be dismissed.

Key Terms
مكاتبة صغيرة (mukataba saghira): A female slave who has made a kitaba (contract of manumission) and is still a minor (below puberty).
موقوف (mawquf): A contract that is suspended, i.e., its validity depends on future acceptance by a party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in al-Muhit, if a master marries off his minor mukataba and she becomes free before accepting, whose acceptance is required for the marriage to be valid?
A) The mukataba's acceptance
B) The master's acceptance
C) Both the mukataba and the master
D) No acceptance is needed

Key Takeaway
The marriage of a minor mukataba after manumission requires the master's acceptance because she remains a minor and the kitaba contract has ended.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
12 min
Master's Consent After Minor Mukataba's Freedom
Critical refinement of a ruling on master's consent after mukataba's freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires the master's consent after a minor mukataba's freedom and evaluate the counterargument based on qiyas.

Core Idea
A change in guardianship (milk to wala) requires renewed consent for a suspended marriage.

When a master marries his minor female mukataba (a slave under a manumission contract), the marriage is suspended on her consent. But if she becomes free while still a minor, a new question arises: does the master's consent become necessary again? The classical Hanafi position says yes, because her freedom creates a new guardianship based on patronage (wala). A later scholar, Ibn Hammam (the muhaqqiq), argued that the marriage should become effective automatically, analogizing to a slave who marries without permission and then is freed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind both views and explains why the classical position remains the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Identify the facts: master marries his minor female mukataba. The marriage is valid but suspended (mawquf) on her consent because she is like an adult regarding kitaba matters. She then pays and becomes free while still a minor.
The suspension initially is on her consent, but after freedom she cannot consent (minor), so it shifts to the master.
2
Understand the classical ruling
The classical ruling (from Imam Muhammad) says the marriage remains suspended on the master's consent after freedom. Reason: a new guardianship based on wala arose, requiring fresh ijaza.
Contrast with the slave case: a slave's marriage is effective from his side, suspended only on master's right; upon freedom, no new guardianship arises.
3
Analyze the muhaqqiq's objection
Ibn Hammam argued that by analogy with a slave who marries without permission and is freed, the marriage should become effective without further consent. He also compared to a minor who marries without guardian: that marriage requires the minor's own consent after puberty.
He claims the master's consent given at the time of marriage should be sufficient, as he was already the guardian.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The author refutes the analogy: the slave's marriage was already effective from his side; only the master's right suspended it. Upon freedom, that right ends, so the marriage becomes effective. For the mukataba, her marriage was never effective from her side (she is a minor and unable to consent), so after freedom, the master's new wala guardianship requires his consent.
The change from milk to wala is key; the mukataba is not like the adult slave.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the classical view, the master can still reject the marriage after her freedom; under the muhaqqiq's view, the marriage becomes binding immediately upon freedom.
The author defends the classical view as the mufta bihi position.

وفي المحيط وغيره : المولى إذا زوج مكاتبته الصغيرة توقف النكاح على إجازتها لأنها ملحقة بالبالغة فيما يبتني على الكتابة، ثم إنها لو لم ترد حتى أدت فعتقت بقي النكاح موقوفاً على إجازة المولى لا إجازتها لأنها بعد العتق لم تبق مكاتبة وهي صغيرة والصغيرة ليست من أهل الإجازة فاعتبر التوقف على إجازتها حال رقها ولم يعتبر بعد العتق . قالوا : وهذه المسألة من أعجب المسائل فإنها مهما زادت من المولى بعداً ازدادت إليه قرباً في النكاح فإنه يملك إلزام النكاح عليها بعد العتق لا قبله . وأعجب منه أنها لو ردت إلى الرق يبطل النكاح الذي باشره المولى وإن أجازه المولى لأنه طرأ حل بات على موقوف فأبطله إلا أن هذا كله ثبت بالدليل وهو يعمل العجائب. وقد بحث المحقق في فتح القدير بأن الذي يقتضيه النظر عدم التوقف على إجازة المولى بعد العتق بل بمجرد عتقها ينفذ النكاح لما صرحوا به من أنه إذا تزوج العبد بغير إذن سيده فأعتقه نفذ لأنه لو توقف فإما على إجازة المولى وهو ممتنع لانتفاء ولايته، وإما على العبد فلا وجه له لأنه صدر من جهته فكيف يتوقف عليه؟ ولأنه كان نافذاً من جهته. وإنما توقف على السيد. فكذا السيد هنا فإنه ولي مجبر، وإنما التوقف على إذنها لعقد الكتابة وقد زال فبقي النفاذ من جهة السيد. وهذا هو الوجه وكثيراً ما يقلد الساهون الساهين وهذا بخلاف الصبي إذا زوج نفسه بغير إذن وليه فإنه موقوف على إجازة وليه ، فلو بلغ قبل أن يرده لا ينفذ حتى يجيزه الصبي لأن العقد حين صدر منه لم يكن نافذاً من جهته إذ لا نفاذ حالة الصبا، أو عدم أهلية الرأي بخلاف العبد ومولى المكاتبة الصغيرة والحاصل أن الصغير والصغيرة ليسا من أهل العبارة بخلاف البالغ اهـ . وجوابه أنه سوء أدب وغلط أقره عليه في النهر واستحسنه وكذا في الشرنبلالية وشرح الباقاني وغيرهم. وقال العلامة المقدسي في الرمز قلت: هذا الذي بحثه هو القياس كما صرح به الإمام الحصيري في شرح الجامع الكبير، وإذا كان هو القياس فلا يقال في شأنه إنه غلط أدب على أن الشخص الذي بلغ رتبة الاجتهاد إذا قال مقتضى النظر كذا الشيء هو القياس لا يرد عليه بأن هذا منقول لأنه إنما اتبع الدليل المقبول وإن كان البحث لا يقضي على المذهب اهـ. ولا يخفى أن ما ذكره لا ينفي كون تعبير المحقق سوء أدب في حق الإمام محمد محرر المذهب وأتباعه إلا أن يقال إنه لم يطلع على نسبة الفرع المذكور إليه إذ ذاك بل ظنه تخريجاً من بعض المشايخ وتبع بعضهم بعضاً كما يشعر به كلامه حيث قال : هذا استظرفت مسألة نقلت . أن المولى إلى أن قال : عن المحيط هكذا تواردها الشارحون على أنا لم نعهد منه في مخالفاته للمذهب صريحاً مثل هذا الكلام فالأنسب . ثم اعلم أن السيد لو زوج المكاتبة بغير رضاها ثم عجزت بطل النكاح لما ذكرناه، وإن كان مكاتباً لم يبطل لكن لا بد من إجازة المولى وإن كان قد رضي أولاً لأنه إنما رضي بتعلق مؤن النكاح كالمهر والنفقة بكسب المكاتب لا يملك نفسه وكسب المكاتب بعد عجزه ملك المولى . كذا في التلخيص. فهو نظير ما إذا زوجها الأبعد مع وجود الأقرب ثم زالت ولاية الأقرب فإنه لا بد من أن يجيزه الأبعد، وسيأتي إيضاحه بعد ذلك أيضاً. واعلم أن الفضولي إذا باشر ثم صار وكيلاً فإنه ينفذ بإجازته بيعاً كان أو نكاحاً، وكذا لو صار ولياً . ولو صار مالكاً فإن طرأ عليه حل بات أبطله وإلا فلا. وينفذ بإجازته. والعبد المحجور إذا باشر عقداً ثم أذن له به فإن كان نكاحاً نفذ بإجازته، ولو كان بيع مال مولاه فإنه لا ينفذ بإجازته والصبي المحجور إذا باشر عقداً ثم أذن له وليه فيه فأجازه جاز نكاحاً أو بيعاً، ولو بلغ فأجازه بعد بلوغه جاز. والعبد المحجور إذا تصرف بلا إذن ثم أعتق فإن كان نكاحاً أو إقراراً بدين نفذ بلا إجازة، وإن كان بيعاً لا يجوز بإجازته بعد إعتاقه والمكاتب لو زوج قنه ثم عتق فأجاز لم يجز، والقاضي لو زوج اليتيم ولم يكن في منشوره ثم أذن له فأجاز جاز، وكذا الولي الأبعد مع الأقرب. وتمامه في جامع الفصولين من الفصل الرابع والعشرين .
And in Al-Muhit and others: If the master marries his minor female mukataba, the marriage is suspended upon her consent because she is treated as an adult in matters built on the contract of kitaba. Then if she does not respond until she pays and becomes free, the marriage remains suspended upon the master's consent, not hers, because after freedom she is no longer a mukataba and she is a minor, and a minor is not qualified to give consent. So the suspension was considered upon her consent while she was a slave, and it is not considered after freedom. They said: This issue is one of the most amazing issues: the further she is from the master, the closer she gets to him in marriage, for he can compel the marriage upon her after freedom but not before. And even more amazing: if she is returned to slavery, the marriage that the master performed becomes void, even if he gave consent, because a definitive dissolution occurred upon a suspended contract, nullifying it. All this is proven by evidence, and evidence works wonders. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir investigated: what logic requires is that there is no suspension upon the master's consent after freedom; rather, upon her freedom the marriage is executed, based on their explicit statement that if a slave marries without his master's permission and then the master frees him, the marriage is executed. For if it were suspended, it would be either upon the master's consent, which is impossible due to lack of guardianship, or upon the slave, which has no basis because the act originated from him—how can it be suspended upon him? And it was already effective from his side; its suspension was upon the master. So here, the master is a compulsive guardian; the suspension was upon her consent due to the kitaba, which has ceased, so the effectiveness from the master's side remains. This is the correct view, and many of the negligent follow the negligent. This is unlike a minor boy who marries himself without his guardian's permission: it is suspended upon the guardian's consent. If he reaches puberty before the guardian rejects it, it does not become effective until the boy himself gives consent, because the contract when issued from him was not effective from his side, as there is no effectiveness in childhood or lack of capacity of opinion, unlike the slave and the master of a minor female mukataba. The conclusion: the minor male and female are not qualified to express consent, unlike the adult. End. And the response is that this is bad manners and a mistake, as affirmed in Al-Nahr and approved, and likewise in Al-Sharnabaliyya and the commentary of Al-Baqani and others. Al-Maqdisi in Al-Ramz said: I say: what he investigated is the qiyas, as stated by Imam Al-Husayri in his commentary on Al-Jami' al-Kabir. If it is the qiyas, then it should not be called a mistake of bad manners, for a person who has reached the level of ijtihad and says 'the requirement of investigation is such' is following accepted evidence, even if his investigation does not override the madhhab. End. It is not hidden that what he said does not negate that the muhaqqiq's expression is bad manners towards Imam Muhammad, the author of the madhhab, and his followers, unless it is said that he was not aware of the attribution of the mentioned branch to him, but thought it was an extraction by some teachers, and some followed others, as his words suggest when he said: 'I found an interesting issue transmitted: that the master...' until he said: 'from Al-Muhit, thus the commentators have transmitted it, and we have not known him in his disagreements with the madhhab to say such words explicitly, so it is more appropriate.' Then know that if the master marries the mukataba without her consent and then she becomes unable to pay (tajjuz), the marriage becomes void for what we mentioned. If he were a male mukatab, it does not become void, but the master's consent is necessary, even if he initially agreed, because he only agreed to the attachment of marriage expenses like mahr and maintenance to the mukatab's earnings; he [the mukatab] does not own himself, and his earnings after incapacity become the property of the master. Thus in Al-Talkhis. So it is analogous to when a distant guardian marries her in the presence of a closer one, then the closer one's guardianship ends: the distant guardian must give consent. Its clarification will come later also. And know that if a fuduli (unauthorized party) undertakes an act and then becomes an agent, it becomes effective by his consent, whether sale or marriage; likewise if he becomes a guardian. If he becomes an owner, then if a definitive dissolution arises, it nullifies it; otherwise no, and it becomes effective by his consent. A slave under interdiction who undertakes a contract and then is given permission: if it is marriage, it becomes effective by his consent; if it is sale of his master's property, it does not become effective by his consent. A minor under interdiction who undertakes a contract and then his guardian gives permission and consents: it is valid, whether marriage or sale; if he reaches puberty and then consents, it is valid. A slave under interdiction who acts without permission and then is freed: if it is marriage or acknowledgment of debt, it becomes effective without consent; if it is sale, it is not valid by his consent after his freedom. If a mukatab marries his slave and then becomes free and gives consent: it is not valid. If a judge marries an orphan without it being in his charter and then permission is given and he consents: it is valid. Likewise, a distant guardian in the presence of a close one. Its completion is in Jami' al-Fusulayn from the twenty-fourth chapter.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a Hanafi ruling concerning the marriage of a minor female mukataba (a slave under a contract of manumission) performed by her master. The original ruling from Al-Muhit states that such a marriage is suspended upon her consent if she becomes an adult before freedom. If she gains freedom while still a minor, the marriage remains suspended upon the master's consent because she is now free but still a minor, and a minor cannot give valid consent. The commentator, al-Marghinani's text is being explained here, and the author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defends this position against a critique by the muhaqqiq (Ibn Hammam) in Fath al-Qadir. The muhaqqiq argued that once she is freed, the marriage should become immediately effective without needing the master's renewal of consent, drawing an analogy with a slave who marries without permission and is then freed: the marriage becomes effective. The muhaqqiq also compares to a minor who marries without guardian permission — that marriage remains suspended even after puberty until the now-adult person consents. The response in this text rebukes the muhaqqiq's expression as 'bad manners and a mistake,' citing other scholars who affirm the original ruling. The text then provides a detailed reasoning: the master's guardianship over the mukataba is based on ownership (milk), and upon her freedom, a new guardianship based on patronage (wala) arises, so his consent must be renewed. If she is returned to slavery (via 'ajz), the marriage becomes void because a definitive dissolution (hull bat) occurs. The passage then extends the discussion to other scenarios: if the master marries the mukataba without her consent and she later becomes unable to pay, the marriage is void; if the husband is a male mukatab, it does not void but needs the master's permission because his earnings become the master's property after 'ajz. Finally, the text provides general rules for contracts by fuduli (unauthorized persons), slaves, minors, and others, showing when subsequent consent or change of status makes the contract effective. This is a dense legal passage typical of a sharh, expanding and refining the matn.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A female slave who has entered into a contract of kitaba with her master to purchase her freedom by paying installments.
إجازة (ijāzah): Consent or ratification that validates a suspended contract.
توقف (tawaqquf): Suspension of the effect of a contract pending a condition, such as consent.
ولاء (walā'): The right of patronage or clientage that arises when a slave is freed, giving the former master certain rights, including guardianship in marriage if the freed slave is a minor.
فضولي (fuḍūlī): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the original ruling in Al-Muhit, if a master marries his minor female mukataba and she later earns her freedom while still a minor, whose consent is required for the marriage to be effective?
A) Only the mukataba's consent
B) Only the master's consent
C) Both the mukataba's and the master's consent
D) No consent is needed after freedom

Key Takeaway
When a minor mukataba gains freedom, the master's new wala guardianship requires his explicit consent for the previously suspended marriage to become effective.

Nikah — Marriage

Dropping of Mahr by Master's Killing: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr when master kills slave before intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr when a master kills his female slave before intercourse, and explain the reasoning behind both Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions.

Core Idea
A master who kills his female slave before consummation loses the right to the mahr due to preventing the exchange.

This passage addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: when a master kills his own female slave before the marriage is consummated, does the husband still owe the mahr? The matn states that it drops, following Abu Hanifa, but the two associates (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the mahr remains due. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school uses analogy (qiyas) and considers the effect of one's own actions on contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling in the matn
Identify the explicit ruling: the mahr drops when the master kills his female slave before intercourse. This is Abu Hanifa's position.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that the full mahr is due to the master, analogizing to natural death.
Their proof is based on the principle that the death ends the marriage, regardless of cause.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine Abu Hanifa's qiyas: he compares the master to a seller who kills the sold item before delivery, and to a free woman who apostatizes before consummation. In both cases, the exchange (badal) is dropped because the party prevented delivery.
Killing is considered destruction (itlaf) in worldly rulings, so the master is treated as having destroyed the subject matter.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the husband pays no mahr (or gets it back); under the Sahibayn, the husband must pay the mahr to the master.
This affects whether the master or the husband bears the loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويسقط المهر بقتل السيد أمته قبل الوطء وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : عليه المهر لمولاها اعتباراً بموتها حتف أنفها، وهذا لأن المقتول ميت بأجله. وله أنه منع المبدل قبل التسليم فيجازي بمنع البدل كما إذا ارتدت الحرة، وكما إذا قتل البائع المبيع قبل التسليم والقتل في حق أحكام الدنيا جعل إتلافاً حتى وجب القصاص والدية، فكذا في حق المهر. أفاد بسقوطه أنه إذا لم يكن مقبوضاً سقط عن ذمة الزوج، وإن كان مقبوضاً لزمه رد جميعه على الزوج. كذا في المبسوط . وقيد بالسيد لأنه لو قتلها أجنبي لا يسقط اتفاقاً، وأطلق السيد حسن الظن بمثل هذا الإمام قوله (أو زوج نافلته كذا في بعض النسخ وهو الموافق لما في التلخيص، وفي بعضها أو نافلته بدون زوج قوله لأن النفاذ بالولاية الأصلية) وهي ولاية الملك وإنما امتنع في الحال لما فيه من الإضرار بالمرة من والغرماء فإذا سقط الدين فات الضرر فنفذ العقد بالولاية الأصلية قوله (وقالا عليه المهر لمولاها قال في النهر : ينبغي أن يقيد الخلاف بما إذا لم تكن مأذونة
His saying: (And the mahr drops by the master killing his female slave before intercourse) and this according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Upon him is the mahr for her master, considering it as her dying a natural death; this is because the killed person dies by his term. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that he (the master) prevented the exchanged item (the slave) before delivery, so he is recompensed by preventing the exchange, just as if a free woman apostatizes, and just as if the seller kills the sold item before delivery. And killing, in the rulings of this world, is considered destruction, so retaliation and blood-money are obligatory; likewise regarding the mahr. It benefits from its dropping that if it was not received, it drops from the husband's liability; if it was received, he must return all of it to the husband. Thus in Al-Mabsut. And it is restricted to the master because if a stranger kills her, it does not drop by agreement. And he (the author) released 'the master' — good opinion of such an Imam. His saying: (or the marriage of his slave) — thus in some copies, and it agrees with what is in Al-Talkhis; in some copies it is 'or his slave' without 'married'. His saying: (because effectiveness is with the original guardianship) which is guardianship of ownership; it was only prevented in the current situation because of the harm to the mother and the creditors; so when the debt falls, the harm is removed, and the contract becomes effective by the original guardianship. His saying: (And they said: upon him is the mahr for her master) — he said in Al-Nahr: it is appropriate to restrict the disagreement to when she was not authorized.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, presenting an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling when a master kills his female slave before consummating the marriage. The matn states: 'The mahr drops by the master killing his female slave before intercourse.' This is Abu Hanifa's position. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two associates, al-Sahibayn) hold that the full mahr is still due to the master, analogizing it to her dying a natural death — since the slave's death by any cause ends the marriage, the mahr remains. Abu Hanifa's proof is based on the principle that the master has prevented the delivery of the bride (al-mubdal) before delivery, so he is punished by losing the mahr (the exchange). This is analogous to the case of a free woman who apostatizes (which prevents consummation) — the mahr drops. Also analogous to a seller who kills the sold item before delivery: the price is not due. Killing in worldly rulings is considered destruction, so the master is treated as having destroyed the object of the contract. The passage explains the practical implications: if the mahr was not yet paid, it drops from the husband's liability; if it was already paid, the master must refund it to the husband. This ruling is specific to the master; if a third party kills the slave, the mahr does not drop by agreement. The commentary then continues with other subsidiary matters: 'او زوج نافلته' refers to the guardian marrying his slave, and the effectiveness of guardianship; and the restriction of the dispute to when the slave was not given permission (ma'dhunah). This is a clear example of Abu Hanifa's use of qiyas and istihsan against the literal analogy of the two associates.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bridal gift that is obligatory upon the husband in a valid marriage.
قتل (qatl): Homicide; in this context, the killing by the master of his female slave.
بدل (badal): Exchange; the mahr is considered the compensation (badal) for the marital right (the bride's availability for intercourse).
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery or handover; here, the delivery of the bride to the husband for consummation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the mahr drop when the master kills his female slave before intercourse?
A) Because the marriage becomes void automatically upon the slave's death
B) Because the master prevented delivery of the bride, analogous to a seller killing the sold item
C) Because the slave's death is considered a natural death and the mahr is dropped in all deaths
D) Because the husband is not required to pay mahr to a killer

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, if a master kills his female slave before consummation, the mahr drops because he prevented the delivery of the bride; this is the relied-upon position of Abu Hanifa.
