New Guardianship Requires New Consent in Marriage
Ruling with proof via qiyas and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a subsequent change in the basis of guardianship (e.g., from ownership to wala) requires renewed ratification for a marriage contract made by the guardian.

Core Idea
A new basis of guardianship demands a new consent – the guardian's original approval does not carry over.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key Hanafi principle: when a guardian's authority over a person changes in nature after a marriage contract, the contract's validity depends on a fresh expression of consent. For example, a master who owns a slave-girl and marries her off as guardian later acquires a different guardianship (wala) through emancipation. The contract does not automatically remain valid; the master must ratify it anew. Understanding this rule helps you navigate real-world cases where guardianship shifts, such as when a slave is freed or a mukatab becomes unable to pay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Guardianship
Recognize that the Hanafi school distinguishes between guardianship by ownership (wilāyat al-milk) and guardianship by clientage (wilāyat al-walāʾ). These are separate legal bases.
Ownership gives full authority; wala arises from emancipation and is a weaker form.
2
State the General Rule
When a new basis of guardianship arises after the contract, the guardian must give a new consent (ijāzah) for the contract to be valid. The original consent tied to the old basis is insufficient.
The passage illustrates this with the case of a master who marries his slave-girl, then frees her.
3
Apply the Rule to the Example
The master initially had guardianship by ownership. After emancipation, he acquires wala. Since wala is a different legal basis, the master must ratify the marriage for it to be effective.
The text says: 'Therefore he may only ratify if there is no relative closer than him.'
4
Compare with Cases Where Ratification is Not Needed
Distinguish from cases where the original basis persists, such as a pledgor who sells a pledged item and later the debt falls – the sale is effective without new consent because the original guardianship (by ownership) remains unchanged.
The key is whether the basis of authority changed, not just the person's status.
5
Extend to Analogous Scenarios
Apply the same reasoning to other situations: a fuduli who later becomes an agent/guardian, a slave who becomes free, a mukatab who becomes unable to pay, etc. In each, a new basis of authority requires fresh ratification.
The passage provides several examples to work through.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب نكاح الرقيق قبل الوطء لا بقتل الحرة نفسها قبله والإذن في العزل لسيد لأمة ولو عتقت أمة كذا تجدد له ولاية لم تكن وقت العقد وهي الولاء بالعتق، ولذا إنما يكون له الإجازة إذا لم يكن لها ولي أقرب منه كالأخ والعم قال : فصار كالشريك زوج العبد ثم ملك الباقي، وكمن أذن لعبد ابنه أو زوج نافلته ثم مات الابن بخلاف الراهن ومولى المأذون باعا ثم سقط الدين حيث لا يفتقر إلى الإجازة لأن النفاذ بالولاية الأصلية. وحاصله أن الولاية التي قارنها رضاه بتزويجها ولاية بحكم الملك وبعد العقد تجدد له ولاية بحكم الولاء فيشترط تجدد رضاه لتجدد الولاية. في شرح تلخيص الجامع الكبير. وكثيراً ما يعترض المخطيء على المصيبين. ثم اعلم أن السيد لو زوج المكاتبة بغير رضاها ثم عجزت بطل النكاح لما ذكرناه، وإن كان مكاتباً لم يبطل لكن لا بد من إجازة المولى وإن كان قد رضي أولاً لأنه إنما رضي بتعلق مؤن النكاح كالمهر والنفقة بكسب المكاتب لا يملك نفسه وكسب المكاتب بعد عجزه ملك المولى . كذا في التلخيص. فهو نظير ما إذا زوجها الأبعد مع وجود الأقرب ثم زالت ولاية الأقرب فإنه لا بد من أن يجيزه الأبعد، وسيأتي إيضاحه بعد ذلك أيضاً. واعلم أن الفضولي إذا باشر ثم صار وكيلاً فإنه ينفذ بإجازته بيعاً كان أو نكاحاً، وكذا لو صار ولياً . ولو صار مالكاً فإن طرأ عليه حل بات أبطله وإلا فلا. وينفذ بإجازته. والعبد المحجور إذا باشر عقداً ثم أذن له به فإن كان نكاحاً نفذ بإجازته، ولو كان بيع مال مولاه فإنه لا ينفذ بإجازته والصبي المحجور إذا باشر عقداً ثم أذن له وليه فيه فأجازه جاز نكاحاً أو بيعاً، ولو بلغ فأجازه بعد بلوغه جاز. والعبد المحجور إذا تصرف بلا إذن ثم أعتق فإن كان نكاحاً أو إقراراً بدين نفذ بلا إجازة، وإن كان بيعاً لا يجوز بإجازته بعد إعتاقه والمكاتب لو زوج قنه ثم عتق فأجاز لم يجز، والقاضي لو زوج اليتيم ولم يكن في منشوره ثم أذن له فأجاز جاز، وكذا الولي الأبعد مع الأقرب. وتمامه في جامع الفصولين من الفصل الرابع والعشرين .
Chapter on marriage of slaves. Before intercourse, it is not [waived] by the free woman killing herself before it, and permission for withdrawal is for the master of a slave-girl. And if a slave-girl is emancipated, a new guardianship arises for him that was not present at the time of the contract, which is the clientage of emancipation. Therefore, he may only ratify if there is no relative closer than him, like a brother or paternal uncle. He said: So it is like the partner who married a slave then later acquired the remaining share, and like one who permitted his son's slave or married his nafil slave then his son died, unlike the pledgor and the guardian of a permitted slave who sold and then the debt fell, where ratification is not needed because effectiveness is through the original guardianship. The gist is that the guardianship that accompanied his consent to her marriage is a guardianship by virtue of ownership, and after the contract a new guardianship arises by virtue of clientage, so his new consent is required for the new guardianship. This is from Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir. Then know that if the master married off a mukataba without her consent and then she became unable [to pay], the marriage is void for what we mentioned; but if it was a mukatab, it is not void, but his master's ratification is necessary even if he consented initially, because he only consented to the expenses of marriage like dower and maintenance being from the mukatab's earnings; he does not own himself, and after his inability, his earnings belong to the master. This is in al-Talkhis. It is analogous to when a more distant relative marries her off in the presence of a closer one, then the closer's guardianship ceases; then the more distant must ratify. And its clarification will come later. Know that if an unauthorized person (fuduli) undertakes an act and then becomes an agent, it is effective by his ratification, whether sale or marriage; likewise if he becomes a guardian. If he becomes an owner, then if an impediment arises that invalidates it, then otherwise it is not, but it is effective by his ratification. The interdicted slave who undertakes a contract and then is permitted for it: if it is a marriage, it is effective by his ratification; if it is a sale of his master's property, it is not effective by his ratification. The interdicted minor who undertakes a contract and then his guardian permits it and ratifies it, it is valid whether marriage or sale; if he reaches puberty and then ratifies it after puberty, it is valid. The interdicted slave who acts without permission and then is emancipated: if it was a marriage or acknowledgment of debt, it is effective without ratification; if it was a sale, it is not valid by his ratification after emancipation. If a mukatab marries his slave and then is emancipated and then ratifies, it is not valid. If a judge marries an orphan and it is not in his patent, then he is permitted and ratifies, it is valid; likewise the more distant guardian with the closer one. Its completion is in Jami' al-Fusulayn from the twenty-fourth chapter.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling regarding the effect of subsequent changes in guardianship on the validity of a marriage contract made by a person with limited authority. The commentator discusses various scenarios where a person who initially had a certain type of guardianship (e.g., by ownership) later acquires a different type (e.g., by clientage after emancipation). The key principle is that a new type of guardianship requires a new consent (ijazah) for the contract to be valid. The text contrasts cases where the original guardianship remains effective (e.g., the pledgor or the guardian of a permitted slave when the debt falls) with cases where a new guardianship arises (e.g., a master who owns a slave-girl and later becomes her patron after freeing her). The ruling also covers the situation of a mukataba (slave with a contract of manumission) whose master marries her off without her consent: if she becomes unable to pay, the marriage is void. For a mukatab (male slave), the marriage does not become void but requires the master's ratification. The discussion includes the unauthorized agent (fuduli) who later becomes an agent or guardian, and the interdicted slave or minor who acts and then gains permission or capacity. In all cases, the effectiveness of the contract depends on the nature of the new status and whether the original act is considered sound.

Key Terms
ولاية (wilāyah): Guardianship or authority over a person for the purpose of contracting marriage.
ولاء (walāʾ): Clientage relationship established by emancipation, giving the patron a form of guardianship.
فضولي (fuḍūlī): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a master marries his slave-girl and then frees her, what is the status of the marriage?
A) The marriage becomes void automatically.
B) The marriage remains valid without any further action.
C) The marriage is suspended pending the master's ratification.
D) The marriage is valid only if the slave-girl consents after emancipation.

Key Takeaway
A change in the legal basis of guardianship (e.g., from ownership to wala) requires a new ratification for the prior marriage contract to be valid in the Hanafi school.

Waiver of Mahr When Master Kills Slave-Girl Before Consummation
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with qiyas reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the waiver of mahr when the master kills his slave-girl before consummation, explain the proofs of each side, and apply the ruling to related scenarios.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: mahr waived for master's prevention; Sahibayn: mahr due by analogy to natural death.

The mahr is a financial obligation that can be affected by events before consummation. A unique case arises when the master himself kills his slave-girl before the marriage is consummated. The Hanafi school is divided on whether the mahr is waived or remains due. This lesson unpacks the arguments and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core issue
The master marries his own slave-girl, then kills her before consummation. The question: is the mahr waived or still due?
The situation involves a master who is also the husband.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa rules that the mahr is waived. His analogy: like a seller who kills the sold item before delivery loses the price, the master prevented delivery of the wife, so he loses the mahr.
Also analogized to a free woman's apostasy before consummation.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the mahr remains due. Their analogy: the slave-girl died at her appointed time like natural death; killing does not change the fact that death occurred.
They treat the killing as not altering the obligation.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
Abu Hanifa responds that in worldly rulings, killing is treated as destruction (itlaf), not as natural death. This is evidenced by the obligation of qisas and diyah.
This rebuttal distinguishes the case from natural death.
5
Apply to related scenarios
The passage extends the ruling to other forms of tafwit: selling her away, emancipating her with option of separation, hiding her. In each, the mahr is waived (subject to her return).
Distinguish from stranger killing, after consummation, or free woman suicide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الرقيق ٣٤٧ العاقد فشمل الصغير والكبير. وذكر في المصفى فيه قولان وفي فتح القدير : ولو لم يكن من أهل المجازاة بأن كان صبياً زوج أمته وصيه مثلاً :قالوا يجب أن لا يسقط في قول أبي لغيره اقتل عبدي فقتله لا يلزمه شيء. وإنما قيد بالحرة للاختلاف في قتل الأمة نفسها والصحيح عدم السقوط كما في الخانية، لأن المهر لمولاها ولم يوجد منه منع المبدل فلو قال المصنف لا بقتل المرأة نفسها لكان أولى. وقيد بالقتل لأن الأمة لو أبقت فلا صداق لها ما لم تحضر في قياس قول أبي حنيفة وهو قول أبي يوسف. كذا في الخانية. ولو ارتدت المرأة عن الإسلام قبل الدخول، فإن كانت حرة سقط المهر اتفاقاً، وإن كانت أمة ففي التبيين أن في السقوط روايتين، وفي غاية البيان : وإذا ارتدت الأمة أو الحرة قبل الدخول يسقط المهر اتفاقاً فكأنه لضعف رواية عدمه لم يعتبرها، وحكم تقبيل ابن الزوج منهما كالردة. وفي المحيط : لو قبلت الأمة ابن زوجها قبل الدخول بها لحقها به دين فإن كانت لا يسقط اتفاقاً لما مر من أن المهر في هذه الحالة لها يوفى منه ديونها. غاية الأمر أنه إذا لم يف بدينها كان على المولى قيمتها للغرماء فيضم إلى المهر ويقسم بينهم، وسيأتي أنه لو أعتق المديون كان عليه قيمته فالقتل أولى.
His saying: (The mahr is waived by the master killing his slave-girl before consummation) and this is according to Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The mahr is due to her master, analogizing to her dying a natural death, because the killed person dies at his appointed time. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof: He prevented the exchange before delivery, so he is punished by preventing the compensation, just as when a free woman apostatizes, and just as when a seller kills the sold item before delivery. Killing in the context of worldly rulings is considered destruction, so retaliation and blood-money are obligatory; similarly regarding the mahr. The implication of its waiver is that if it had not been received, it is dropped from the husband's liability; if it had been received, he must return the entire amount to the husband. This is in al-Mabsut. It is restricted to the master because if a stranger killed her, it is not waived by agreement. He said: (or married his nafil slave) as in some copies, and this is in accordance with al-Talkhis; in some copies it is just 'or his nafil' without 'married'. His saying: because effectiveness is through the original guardianship, which is the guardianship of ownership; it was only prevented in the present case due to harm to the deceased and the creditors; when the debt falls, the harm disappears and the contract becomes effective through the original guardianship. His saying: (and they said: the mahr is due to her master) He said in al-Nahr: the dispute should be restricted to when she is not permitted. The contracting party includes the minor and major. It is mentioned in al-Musaffa that there are two opinions. In Fath al-Qadir: If he is not among those deserving punishment, such as a minor who marries his slave-girl through his guardian, they said: it should not be waived according to Abu Hanifa, unlike the free minor woman who apostatizes, because the minor sane woman is among those deserving punishment for apostasy, unlike other acts because she does not contemplate them and apostasy is forbidden to her. So by this, non-waiver is preponderant. It is restricted to the slave-girl because if the master killed the husband of his slave-girl, the mahr is not waived by agreement, because he acted on the husband, not on the object of the contract. It is restricted to before consummation because if he killed her after it, it is not waived by agreement. He referred by 'killing' to every spoilation caused by the master's act; thus the mahr is waived if he sold her and the buyer took her away from the city, or if he emancipated her before consummation and she chose separation, or if he hid her in a place unreachable by the husband. This is in al-Tabyin and others. The meaning of waiver in the first and third cases is waiver of the right to demand it, as stated in al-Muhit and al-Zahiriya, not absolute waiver, because if he brings her afterwards, he has the mahr, as is not hidden. The author intended by slave-girl the qinn, the mudabbara, and the umm walad, because it is known that the mahr of a mukataba belongs to her, not to the master, so it is not waived by the master killing her. The summary: When a woman dies, it is either that she is free, a mukataba, or a slave-girl; each of the three either dies a natural death, by her own killing, or by another's killing; each of the nine is either before or after consummation; thus there are eighteen [cases]. The correct view is that her mahr is not waived in all except when she is a slave-girl and her master kills her before consummation. His saying: (not) by the free woman killing herself (before it) i.e., the mahr is not waived by the free woman killing herself before consummation, because a person's injury to himself is not considered in worldly rulings, so it resembles her dying a natural death; and because she does not have the right to drop their right; so it is as if he said 'kill me' and he killed him, then the blood-money is obligatory, unlike 'cut my hand' and he cut it, nothing is obligatory; unlike the master's killing, because it is considered in worldly rulings to the extent that expiation is obligatory on him; likewise if the master said to another 'kill my slave' and he killed him, nothing is due on him. It is restricted to the free woman because of the difference regarding the slave-girl killing herself: the correct view is non-waiver, as in al-Khaniyya, because the mahr belongs to her master and he did not prevent the exchange; so if the author had said 'not by the woman killing herself', it would have been better. It is restricted to killing because if the slave-girl runs away, she has no dower unless she returns, analogously to Abu Hanifa's view, and this is the view of Abu Yusuf. This is in al-Khaniyya. If a woman apostatizes from Islam before consummation, if she is free, the mahr is waived by agreement; if she is a slave-girl, in al-Tabyin there are two narrations; in Ghayat al-Bayan: If the slave-girl or free woman apostatizes before consummation, the mahr is waived by agreement; it is as if the weak narration of non-waiver was not considered. The ruling of kissing the husband's son is like that of apostasy. In al-Muhit: If the slave-girl kisses her husband's son before consummation, a debt is incurred upon her; then if she is [a slave], the mahr is not waived by agreement, because in this situation the mahr belongs to her and her debts are paid from it. The upshot: if it does not suffice for her debt, then the master owes her value to the creditors; it is added to the mahr and divided among them. It will come later that if he emancipates the debtor, he owes its value, so killing is more so.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of the master killing his female slave before consummation on the mahr. Abu Hanifa rules that the mahr is waived entirely, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the mahr is still due to the master, analogizing to the case of natural death. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the master prevented the delivery of the object of exchange (the wife) before the husband could receive her, so he is penalized by losing the countervalue (the mahr). This is analogous to a free woman who apostatizes before consummation (the mahr is waived) and to a seller who kills the sold item before delivery (the price is waived). The commentators clarify that this applies only when the master kills her; if a stranger kills her, the mahr is not waived by consensus. The restriction to before consummation is crucial: if the master kills her after consummation, the mahr remains due. The passage also discusses that 'killing' includes any act that prevents the husband from reaching her, such as selling her to a buyer who takes her far away, or emancipating her so that she chooses separation, or hiding her. The waiver mentioned is not absolute but rather a suspension of the right to demand the mahr: if the master later brings her, the husband has the right to the mahr. The text then discusses the case of the free woman who kills herself: her mahr is not waived because a person's self-inflicted harm is not legally significant in worldly matters. For the slave-girl who kills herself, the correct view is also non-waiver. Finally, the passage discusses apostasy: for a free woman, the mahr is waived by agreement; for a slave-girl, there is a dispute, but the preponderant view is that it is also waived.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bride-price given by the husband to the wife upon marriage.
قبل الوطء (qabl al-waṭʾ): Before sexual consummation of the marriage.
تفويت (tafwīt): Spoilation or prevention of the husband from accessing his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the mahr waived when the master kills his slave-girl?
A) Always, regardless of timing.
B) Only if the killing occurs before consummation.
C) Only if the killing occurs after consummation.
D) Never; the mahr is always due.

Key Takeaway
The mahr is waived only when the master kills his slave-girl before consummation; the waiver is not absolute but subject to her return.

Nikah — Marriage

Fall of Mahr: The Sole Exception and Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fall of mahr due to master's actions before consummation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the single condition under which the mahr of a slave woman falls before consummation and evaluate the intra-Hanafi disputes regarding suicide, apostasy, and other actions.

Core Idea
The mahr falls only when the master kills his slave woman before consummation—all other actions leave it intact.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the intricate ruling on when the mahr (dower) of a slave woman falls due to her master's actions before consummation. While the general rule is that the mahr does not fall, there is one clear exception. The text also explores intra-Hanafi disputes on related issues like suicide and apostasy, guiding us to the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exception
State the default: mahr does not fall. Then identify the only case where it does: master kills slave woman before consummation.
The exception is limited to killing; selling or hiding only suspends the claim.
2
Examine the rationale behind the exception
Understand why the master's killing causes the mahr to fall: his action is legally recognized (e.g., expiation is due), unlike self-injury.
Contrast with a free woman's suicide, which is not recognized, so mahr does not fall.
3
Analyse the disputed cases
Review the intra-Hanafi differences: slave woman's suicide, apostasy, kissing husband's son. Two narrations exist; the correct view is non-fall.
Al-Zayla'i and Qadi Khan affirmed non-fall in these cases.
4
Distinguish permanent fall from suspension
In cases of selling or hiding, the mahr is not permanently lost; the claim is suspended until she returns.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding that mahr is completely forfeited.
5
Trace the tarjih mechanism
Identify why later Hanafi authorities preferred non-fall in all cases except the master's killing. Note the critique of Fath al-Qadir's reasoning.
The mahr ultimately belongs to the master, so his right is not lost by the slave's actions.

يسقط مهرها لأن الصغيرة العاقلة من أهل المجازاة على الردة بخلاف غيرها من الأفعال لأنها لم تخطر عليها والردة محظورة عليها اهـ. فترجح به عدم السقوط. وقيد بالأمة لأن السيد لو قتل زوج أمته لم يسقط المهر اتفاقاً لأنه تصرف في لا في المعقود عليه. وقيد بكونه قبل الوطء لأنه لو قتلها بعده لا يسقط اتفاقاً، وأشار بالقتل إلى كل تفويت حصل بفعل المولى فلهذا سقط المهر لو باعها وذهب بها المشتري من المصر أو أعتقها بعده فله المهر كما لا يخفى. وأراد المصنف بالأمة القنة والمدبرة وأم الولد لما عرف من أن مهر المكاتبة لها لا للمولى فلا يسقط بقتل المولى إياها. والحاصل أن المرأة إذا ماتت فلا تخلو إما أن تكون حرة أو مكاتبة أو أمة، وكل من الثلاثة إما أن تكون حتف أنفها أو بقتلها نفسها أو بقتل غيرها وكل من التسعة إما قبل الدخول أو بعده فهي ثمانية عشر، ولا يسقط مهرها على الصحيح في الكل إلا إذا كانت أمة وقتلها سيدها قبل الدخول قوله : (لا) بقتل الحرة نفسها (قبله أي لا يسقط المهر بقتل الحرة نفسها قبل الوطء لأن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة في حق أحكام الدنيا فشابه موتها حتف أنفها، ولأنها لا تملك إسقاط حقهم فصار كما إذا قال اقتلني فقتله فإنه تجب الدية بخلاف اقطع يدي فقطعها لا يجب شيء بخلاف قتل المولى لأنه معتبر في حق أحكام الدنيا حتى تجب الكفارة عليه ولذا لو قال المولى لغيره اقتل عبدي فقتله لا يلزمه شيء. وإنما قيد بالحرة للاختلاف في قتل الأمة نفسها والصحيح عدم السقوط كما في الخانية، لأن المهر لمولاها ولم يوجد منه منع المبدل فلو قال المصنف لا بقتل المرأة نفسها لكان أولى. وقيد بالقتل لأن الأمة لو أبقت فلا صداق لها ما لم تحضر في قياس قول أبي حنيفة وهو قول أبي يوسف. كذا في الخانية. ولو ارتدت المرأة عن الإسلام قبل الدخول، فإن كانت حرة سقط المهر اتفاقاً، وإن كانت أمة ففي التبيين أن في السقوط روايتين، وفي غاية البيان : وإذا ارتدت الأمة أو الحرة قبل الدخول يسقط المهر اتفاقاً فكأنه لضعف رواية عدمه لم يعتبرها، وحكم تقبيل ابن الزوج منهما كالردة. وفي المحيط : لو قبلت الأمة ابن زوجها قبل الدخول بها لحقها المديون كان عليه قيمته فالقتل أولى. فادعى الزوج أنها قبلته بشهوة وكذبه سيدها تبين الأمة منه بإقراره ويلزمه نصف المهر لتكذيب المولى أنه كان بشهوة .اهـ. وينبغي ترجيح عدم سقوطه في ردة الأمة وتقبيلها ابن الزوج قياساً على ما إذا قتلت نفسها فإن الزيلعي جعل الروايتين في الكل. وقد صحح قاضيخان عدمه في القتل فليكن تصحيحاً في الأخريين أيضاً وهو الظاهر، لأن مستحقه لم يفعل شيئاً وهو المولى . وما في فتح القدير من بناء الخلاف على الخلاف في أن المهر هل يجب للمولى ابتداء أو يجب لها ثم ينتقل للمولى عند الفراغ من حاجتها ضعيف لأنه ولو وجب لها ابتداء يستقر للمولى بعده فلا يسقط بفعلها على القولين كما لا يخفى. وأما القائل بالسقوط بقتلها نفسها علل بأن فعلها يضاف إلى المولى بدليل أنها لو قتلت إنساناً خوطب مولاها بالدفع أو الفداء. بقتل المرأة نفسها ليس احترازياً لأن وارثها لو قتلها قبل الدخول فإنه لا يسقط المهر أيضاً لأنه بالقتل لم يبق وارثاً مستحقاً للمهر لحرمانه به فصار كالأجنبي إذا قتلها . وهذا هو
The contracting party includes the minor and the adult. It is mentioned in al-Musaffa that there are two opinions, and in Fath al-Qadir: 'If he is not among those capable of retribution, such as a minor who marries off his female slave by his guardian, they said: it should not fall (the mahr) according to Abu Hanifa's opinion, unlike the case of a free minor woman who apostatizes, her mahr falls, because the sane minor is capable of retribution for apostasy, unlike other actions, because she is not aware of them, and apostasy is prohibited to her.' So the preference is for non-fall. He qualified it with 'slave woman' because if the master kills the husband of his slave woman, the mahr does not fall by agreement, because it is a disposition in, not in the object of contract. He qualified it with being before intercourse, because if he kills her after it, it does not fall by agreement. He indicated by 'killing' every loss caused by the master's action, so the mahr falls if he sells her and the buyer takes her out of the city, or frees her before consummation and she chooses separation, or hides her in a place where the husband cannot reach her. This is in al-Tabyeen and others. The meaning of its falling in the first and third is the fall of the right to claim, as stated in al-Muhit and al-Zahiriya, not its fall absolutely because if he brings her back after that, he has the mahr, as is clear. The author meant by 'slave woman' the proper slave, mudabbara, and umm walad, because it is known that the mahr of a mukataba is hers, not the master's, so it does not fall by the master's killing her. The conclusion: When a woman dies, she is either free, mukataba, or slave; each of the three is either by natural death, by killing herself, or by being killed by another; and each of the nine is either before or after consummation — that is eighteen cases. The mahr does not fall according to the correct view in all except when she is a slave and her master kills her before consummation. His statement: 'Not by the free woman killing herself before it' i.e., the mahr does not fall by the free woman killing herself before intercourse, because a person's injury to themselves is not considered in worldly rulings, so it is like her dying naturally; and because she does not have the right to drop their right, so it is like if he said 'kill me' and he killed him, the blood-money is obligatory, unlike 'cut my hand' and he cut it, nothing is obligatory. Contrary to the master's killing, because it is considered in worldly rulings so that expiation is obligatory upon him; hence if the master said to another 'kill my slave' and he killed him, nothing is obligatory upon him. He qualified with 'free woman' because of the difference regarding the slave woman killing herself; the correct view is non-fall as in al-Khaniya, because the mahr belongs to her master and he did not prevent the alternative. So had the author said 'not by the woman killing herself' it would have been better. He qualified with 'killing' because if the slave woman absconds, she has no dower until she returns, according to the analogy of Abu Hanifa's statement, which is the statement of Abu Yusuf. This is in al-Khaniya. If the woman apostatizes from Islam before consummation, if she is free, the mahr falls by agreement; if she is a slave, in al-Tabyeen there are two narrations, and in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'If the slave or free woman apostatizes before consummation, the mahr falls by agreement' as if he did not consider the weak narration of non-fall. The ruling of kissing the husband's son is like apostasy. In al-Muhit: 'If the slave woman kisses the husband's son before consummation, a debt attaches to her... it does not fall by agreement for the reason that the mahr in this case is hers to discharge her debts.' The upshot is that if it is not sufficient for her debt, the master must pay her value to the creditors, so it is added to the mahr and divided among them... It will come that if the debtor is freed, his value is due, so killing is more apt. If the husband claims she kissed him with desire and her master denies it, the slave woman is separated from the husband by his admission and half the mahr is obligatory because the master's denial that it was with desire. It is preferable to prefer non-fall in the apostasy of the slave woman and her kissing the husband's son, by analogy to her killing herself, since al-Zayla'i made two narrations in all. Qadi Khan affirmed non-fall in killing, so let it be a correction in the other two as well, and it is apparent, because the one entitled (the master) did nothing. What is in Fath al-Qadir about building the difference on the difference of whether the mahr is obligatory for the master initially or for her and then transfers to the master is weak, because even if it is obligatory for her initially, it becomes established for the master afterwards, so it does not fall by her action on both opinions. As for the one who says it falls by her killing herself, he reasoned that her action is attributed to the master, evidenced by if she kills a person, her master is addressed to pay or ransom. The killing of the woman herself is not exclusionary because if her heir kills her before consummation, the mahr also does not fall, because by killing he does not remain an heir entitled to the mahr due to his deprivation, so he becomes like a stranger who killed her. And this is the [end].
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of the ruling on when the mahr (dower) of a slave woman falls due to actions of her master before consummation. The author begins by noting that the term 'al-aqid' (contracting party) includes minors and adults, and discusses the case of a minor master who marries off his slave woman—here, according to Abu Hanifa, the mahr does not fall if the minor later apostatizes, because the minor is not considered capable of retribution (mujaza) for apostasy. The author then qualifies the discussion: the master's killing of the slave woman's husband does not cause the mahr to fall; rather, it is the master's own act of causing loss to the slave woman herself (like killing, selling, or hiding her) that leads to the fall of the mahr, but only when done before consummation. The fall of the mahr in the cases of selling or hiding means the right to claim it is suspended, not that it is permanently lost—if the slave woman is returned, the mahr can be claimed. The author then enumerates eighteen possible scenarios: the woman may be free, mukataba, or slave; she may die naturally, by suicide, or by being killed; and this may occur before or after consummation. According to the correct view, the mahr falls only in one case: when a slave woman is killed by her master before consummation. The passage then explains why a free woman's suicide does not cause the mahr to fall: a person's self-injury is not legally recognized in worldly matters, unlike the master's killing which incurs expiation. For a slave woman's suicide, there is a difference of opinion; the correct view is non-fall because the mahr belongs to the master and he did not cause the loss. Similarly, apostasy of a slave woman is debated—some say it causes fall, but the author prefers non-fall by analogy to suicide, citing al-Zayla'i and Qadi Khan. Kissing the husband's son is like apostasy. The passage ends with a criticism of Fath al-Qadir's attempt to base the difference on whether the mahr is initially for the master or the slave, arguing that the mahr ultimately belongs to the master regardless. The overall ruling is that the master's action causing loss before consummation is the only ground for the mahr to fall, and even then only temporarily.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower or marriage gift that becomes obligatory upon the husband by virtue of the marriage contract.
السقوط (al-suqut): The legal fall or cancellation of a right, here the right to claim the mahr.
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave owned by a master.
المكاتبة (al-mukataba): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitaba) with her master; she is partially free.
المدبرة (al-mudabbara): A slave woman who has been promised freedom upon her master's death (tadbir).
أم الولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, entitled to freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In how many of the eighteen scenarios does the mahr fall according to the correct view in this passage?
A) Eighteen
B) One
C) Nine
D) None

Key Takeaway
The mahr of a slave woman falls only when her master kills her before consummation; intra-Hanafi disputes exist but the relied-upon view is non-fall for all other causes.

Permission for 'Azl with Another's Slave Woman
Ruling and evidence for permission of 'azl with another's slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'azl with another's slave woman and identify the required permission, citing the textual evidence.

Core Idea
'Azl is permissible, but with another's slave woman, the master's permission is required to protect his right to offspring.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a specific case of 'azl (coitus interruptus): when performed with a slave woman belonging to another man. The Hanafi school requires the master's permission because his interest lies in the offspring the slave may produce. The lesson will walk through the ruling, its proof, a weak alternative opinion, and a related issue of paternity denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
'Azl is essentially permissible (mubah) as established by hadith, but specific conditions apply depending on the woman's status.
2
Locate the condition for another's slave woman
The master's permission is required because 'azl thwarts his interest in offspring. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the apparent narration.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A weak narration suggests the slave woman herself may give permission, but the relied-upon position is that only the master's consent suffices.
4
Examine the dalil
The hadith from Jabir (Bukhari) shows the Companions practiced 'azl while the Qur'an was being revealed, and the Prophet refuted the claim that it is minor infanticide.
5
Consider a practical consequence
If pregnancy occurs after 'azl, paternity denial is allowed only if the man did not return to her or returned after urinating, as remnants of semen may have entered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والإذن في العزل لسيد الأمة) لأنه يخل بمقصود المولى وهو الولد فيعتبر رضاه، قول أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه في ظاهر الرواية. وعنهما في غيرها أن الإذن لها وهو ضعيف. قيد بالأمة أي أمة الغير لأن العزل جائز عن أمة نفسه بغير إذنها والإذن في العزل عن الحرة لها ولا يباح بغيره لأنه حقها. وفي الخانية ذكر في الكتاب أنه لا يباح بغير إذنها، وقالوا في زماننا يباح لسوء الزمان. قال في فتح القدير بعده: فليعتبر مثله الأعذار من مسقطاً لإذنها . وأفاد وضع المسألة أن العزل جائز بالإذن وهذا هو الصحيح عند عامة العلماء في البخارى عن جابر : كنا نعزل والقرآن ينزل والحديث السنن أن رجلاً قال : يا رسول الله إن لي جارية وأنا أعزل عنها وأنا أكره أن تحمل وأنا أريد ما يريد الرجال وأن اليهود تحدث أن العزل الموعدة الصغرى . قال : كذبت اليهود لو أراد الله أن يخلقه ما استطعت أن تصرفه. وفي فتح القدير ثم في بعض أجوبة المشايخ الكراهة وفي بعضها عدمها. وفي المعراج : العزل أن يجامع فإذا جاء وقت الإنزال نزع فأنزل خارج الفرج اهـ. ثم إذا عزل بإذن أو بغير إذن ثم ظهر بها حبل هل يحل نفيه ؟ قالوا : إن لم يعد إليها أو عاد ولكن بال قبل العود حل نفيه، وإن لم يبل لا يحل كذا روي عن علي رضي الله عنه، لأن بقية المني في ذكره يسقط فيها ولذا قال أبو حنيفة فيما إذا اغتسل من الجنابة قبل البول ثم بال
His statement: (And permission for 'azl is for the master of the slave woman) because it thwarts the master's objective, which is offspring, so his consent is considered. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa and his two companions in the apparent narration. From them in other than it that permission is for her, and it is weak. He qualified it with 'slave woman' meaning the slave of another, because 'azl from one's own slave woman is permissible without her permission, and permission for 'azl from a free woman is for her, and it is not permissible without it because it is her right. In al-Khaniya, it is mentioned in the book that it is not permissible without her permission, and they said in our time it is permissible due to the badness of the times. He said in Fath al-Qadir after it: 'So let similar excuses be considered as dropping her permission.' And he benefited the formulation of the issue that 'azl is permissible with permission, and this is the correct view according to the generality of scholars. In al-Bukhari from Jabir: 'We used to practice 'azl while the Qur'an was being revealed.' And the hadith: A man said: 'O Messenger of Allah, I have a slave woman and I practice 'azl with her, and I dislike her becoming pregnant, and I desire what men desire, and the Jews say that 'azl is minor infanticide.' He said: 'The Jews lie; if Allah intended to create it, you would not be able to turn it away.' In Fath al-Qadir then: 'In some answers of the mashayikh, it is disliked, and in some it is not.' In al-Mi'raj: 'Al-'azl is that he copulates and when the time of ejaculation comes, he withdraws and ejaculates outside the vagina.' End. Then, if he practices 'azl with or without permission and then pregnancy appears, is it permissible for him to deny it? They said: If he did not return to her, or returned but urinated before returning, his denial is permissible; if he did not urinate, it is not permissible. This is narrated from 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, because the remainder of semen in his penis falls into her. Hence Abu Hanifa said regarding when he performs ghusl from janabah before urinating and then urinates...
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on 'azl (coitus interruptus) with a slave woman belonging to another person. The matn states that permission for 'azl is for the master of the slave woman. The commentator explains that this is because 'azl thwarts the master's interest in obtaining offspring from his slave, so his consent is necessary. Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold this in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). In another narration, it is said that the slave woman herself can give permission, but this is weak. The ruling is qualified: 'azl with one's own slave woman is permissible without her permission; with a free woman, her permission is required because it is her right. The commentator then cites a ruling from al-Khaniya that in their time, due to deteriorating conditions, 'azl without permission is allowed. Fath al-Qadir suggests that excuses similar to 'bad times' can drop the requirement of permission. The passage then presents a hadith from Jabir in Sahih al-Bukhari that the Companions practiced 'azl while the Qur'an was being revealed, and a specific hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) denied that 'azl is minor infanticide. There is a difference among later scholars as to whether 'azl is disliked or not. Finally, the passage discusses the issue of denying paternity if pregnancy occurs after 'azl: if the man did not return to her or returned but urinated before returning, he can deny the child; otherwise not, because remnants of semen may have entered. This is based on a report from 'Ali. The overall ruling is that 'azl is permissible with the master's permission when the woman is another's slave, and the general principle is that 'azl is allowed in Islamic law, though some later scholars considered it disliked in certain circumstances.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Coitus interruptus; withdrawing before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy.
سيد الأمة (sayyid al-amah): The owner of the slave woman, whose permission is required for 'azl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented here, from whom must permission be obtained to perform 'azl with a slave woman belonging to another person?
A) The slave woman herself
B) The husband's wife
C) The master of the slave woman
D) No permission is needed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the master's permission for 'azl with another's slave woman, a rule derived from the master's right to offspring, supported by prophetic hadith.

Mahr Liability on Husband's Claim of Kiss from Slave Wife
Clarifying the ruling on mahr when husband's claim of kiss is denied by master, with extension to apostasy and suicide of slave wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mahr liability when a husband claims a kiss from a slave wife and explain why the mahr does not drop when the slave wife commits apostasy or suicide, applying qiyas to the case of the heir killing her.

Core Idea
Mahr does not drop by the slave wife's actions because the master, the rightful recipient, did nothing to cause its loss.

In this passage, al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a ruling from the matn concerning the mahr of a slave wife when the husband claims a kiss with desire. The initial ruling establishes that half the mahr is due if the master denies the kiss. The commentary then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the mahr drops if the slave wife apostatizes, kisses her stepson, or kills herself. The author prefers the view that the mahr does not drop, using qiyas to the case of the heir killing the wife before consummation.

Guided Path
1
State the initial ruling
Identify the scenario and the default ruling from the matn: husband claims kiss, master denies, half mahr is due.
This sets the baseline for the extended dispute.
2
Identify the extended issue
Recognise the dispute: does the mahr drop if the slave wife apostatizes, kisses the husband's son, or kills herself?
The author groups these together.
3
Contrast the two Hanafi views
Al-Zaylai transmitted two narrations (dropping and non-dropping). Qadikhan affirmed non-dropping in the case of suicide. The author sides with Qadikhan.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute among later scholars.
4
Analyze the author's qiyas
The author analogizes to the case of the heir killing the wife: the mahr does not drop because the heir is disqualified. Similarly, the master is the rightful recipient and did nothing wrong.
The analogy refutes the attribution argument.
5
Critique the opposing reasoning
The opposing view argues that the slave's act is attributed to the master. The author shows inconsistency by the heir case, where attribution does not lead to mahr dropping.
The master's liability in tort does not extend to mahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فادعى الزوج أنها قبلته بشهوة وكذبه سيدها تبين الأمة منه بإقراره ويلزمه نصف المهر لتكذيب المولى أنه كان بشهوة .اهـ. وينبغي ترجيح عدم سقوطه في ردة الأمة وتقبيلها ابن الزوج قياساً على ما إذا قتلت نفسها فإن الزيلعي جعل الروايتين في الكل. وقد صحح قاضيخان عدمه في القتل فليكن تصحيحاً في الأخريين أيضاً وهو الظاهر، لأن مستحقه لم يفعل شيئاً وهو المولى . وما في فتح القدير من بناء الخلاف على الخلاف في أن المهر هل يجب للمولى ابتداء أو يجب لها ثم ينتقل للمولى عند الفراغ من حاجتها ضعيف لأنه ولو وجب لها ابتداء يستقر للمولى بعده فلا يسقط بفعلها على القولين كما لا يخفى. وأما القائل بالسقوط بقتلها نفسها علل بأن فعلها يضاف إلى المولى بدليل أنها لو قتلت إنساناً خوطب مولاها بالدفع أو الفداء. بقتل المرأة نفسها ليس احترازياً لأن وارثها لو قتلها قبل الدخول فإنه لا يسقط المهر أيضاً لأنه بالقتل لم يبق وارثاً مستحقاً للمهر لحرمانه به فصار كالأجنبي إذا قتلها . وهذا هو والتقييد
If the husband claims that she (the slave wife) kissed him with desire and her master denies him, the slave woman becomes separated from him by his confession, and half the mahr is incumbent upon him due to the master's denial that it was with desire. End. And it is preferable to incline towards its non-shedding in the apostasy of the slave woman and her kissing the son of the husband, by analogy to the case where she kills herself. For al-Zaylai considered two narrations in all cases, and Qadikhan affirmed non-shedding in killing, so let it be affirmation in the other two as well, and it is apparent, because the one entitled to it did nothing—he being the master. What is in Fath al-Qadir, that the dispute is built upon a dispute over whether the mahr is obligatory for the master initially or for her and then transfers to the master after fulfilling her need, is weak because even if it is obligatory for her initially, it becomes settled for the master after that, so it does not drop by her act on both views, as is not hidden. As for the one who says it drops by her killing herself, he argues that her act is attributed to the master, evidenced by if she kills a human, her master is addressed to pay blood money or ransom. The killing of a woman by herself is not a legal impediment because if her heir kills her before consummation, the mahr does not drop either, because by killing he no longer remains an heir entitled to the mahr due to his exclusion by it, so he becomes like a stranger if he kills her. This is the point and the restriction.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn. It discusses a scenario: a husband has a slave wife (owned by her master), and he claims she kissed him with desire, but the master denies it. The ruling is that the slave woman becomes separated from the husband (i.e., the marriage is annulled) by the husband's own admission, and he owes half the mahr (prompt payment) because the master denies that the kiss was with desire, which would have precluded mahr. The comment then addresses an extension: whether the mahr drops if the slave wife commits apostasy or kisses her husband's son (i.e., stepson). The author suggests that the preferred view is that the mahr does not drop in these cases, by analogy to the case where the slave kills herself, which is disputed. Al-Zaylai reported two narrations on all these matters (dropping or not dropping of mahr). Qadikhan affirmed that the mahr does not drop when she kills herself, so the author argues this should be the same for apostasy and kissing. The reasoning is that the rightful recipient of the mahr (the master) did nothing to cause its loss. The author then critiques a view in Fath al-Qadir that the entire dispute is based on whether the mahr is initially owed to the master or to the slave, dismissing it because even if owed to the slave initially, it settles for the master afterwards, so her actions cannot nullify it. The opposing view (mahr drops when she kills herself) argues by attribution: her act is attributed to the master, like if she kills someone, the master is liable. But the author refutes this by analogy: if her heir kills her before consummation, the mahr does not drop, because the heir is disqualified from inheritance by killing, so the mahr goes to other heirs, and the killer is like a stranger. Thus, the mahr remains due. The passage ends with a transition: 'This is the point and the restriction.'

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift (dower) that becomes obligatory upon marriage; here half is due upon separation.
ردة (riddah): Apostasy from Islam by the slave wife; it dissolves the marriage.
تقبيل (taqbil): Kissing with desire; if the husband claims she kissed him, it may imply she consented to intimacy.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave wife commits apostasy, what is the preferred Hanafi ruling regarding the mahr according to this passage?
A) The mahr drops completely because she caused the marriage to end.
B) The mahr does not drop because the master is the rightful recipient and did nothing.
C) The husband pays half the mahr only if the master denies the kiss.
D) The mahr is determined by the master's consent.

Key Takeaway
The mahr of a slave wife does not drop due to her apostasy, suicide, or kissing a stepson because the master's right to the mahr is unaffected by her acts.

Conditions for Denying Paternity in the Hanafi School
Ruling on nafi al-walad and reconciliation of sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a master to deny paternity and apply them to a scenario involving a female slave.

Core Idea
Denying paternity is permissible only when three conditions are met: non-muhsanah, withdrawal, and predominant assumption.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the delicate issue of a master denying paternity of a child born to his female slave. The Hanafi school sets strict conditions to protect lineage and prevent false denials. Understanding these conditions is crucial for applying fiqh in family and slave-related matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
The text states that a master has latitude to deny paternity only when three conditions are met: the slave is non-muhsanah, the master practiced 'azl (withdrawal), and he has predominant assumption that the child is not from him.
Condition 1: slave not previously married or in a state that firmly establishes lineage. Condition 2: withdrawal occurred. Condition 3: strong doubt.
2
Understand the case of muhsanah
If the slave is muhsanah, denial is not allowed because even with withdrawal, some semen might have entered. The master cannot rely on withdrawal alone.
Muhsanah here means the slave is in a state where lineage is likely established (e.g., she is married or has a known sexual history with the master).
3
Reconcile sources
The text notes disagreement between Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Mi'raj. The reconciliation is that the condition of non-repetition (not having intercourse after withdrawal) is part of establishing predominant assumption.
This shows how later Hanafi scholars harmonize conflicting fatwas.
4
Apply the analogy to womb blockage
The author analogizes a woman blocking her womb to prevent pregnancy to a husband's withdrawal without permission—both are prohibited without the spouse's consent.
This extends the principle to married women: blocking conception without husband's permission is haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الرقيق من حيث كانت فخرج المني وجب إعادة الغسل. كذا في المعراج وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : رجل له جارية غير محصنة تخرج وتدخل ويعزل عنها المولى فجاءت بولد وأكبر ظنه أنه ليس منه كان في سعة نفيه، وإن كانت محصنة لا يسعه نفيه لأنه ربما يعزل فيقع الماء في الفرج الخارج ثم يدخل فلا مطلقاً يعتمد على العزل اهـ. وهذا يفيد ضعف التفصيل المتقدم وأنه لا يحل النفي محصنة وأن جوازه مشروط بثلاثة : عدم تحصينها ووجود العزل منه وغلبة الظن بأنه ليس منه. وقد يقال : إن ما في المعراج بيان لمحل غلبة الظن بأنه ليس منه فإذا كان قد عزل ولم يعد غلب على ظنه أنه ليس منه بشرط أن لا تكون محصنة وبه يحصل التوفيق. وينبغي أن يكون سد المرأة فم رحمها كما تفعله النساء لمنع الولد حراماً بغير إذن الزوج قياساً على عزله بغير إذنها. وفي فتح القدير : وهل يباح الإسقاط بعد الحبل ؟ يباح ما لم يتخلق شيء منه . ثم في غير موضع : ولا يكون ذلك إلا بعد مائة وعشرين يوماً. وهذا يقتضي أنهم أرادوا بالتخليق نفخ الروح وإلا فهو غلط لأن التخليق يتحقق بالمشاهدة قبل هذه المدة اهـ. وفي الخانية من كتاب الكراهية ولا أقول بأنه يباح الإسقاط مطلقاً فإن المحرم إذا كسر بيض الصيد يكون ضامناً لأنه أصل الصيد فلما كان يؤاخذ بالجزاء ثم فلا أقل من أن يلحقها إثم ههنا إذا أسقطت بغير عذر ا هـ . وينبغي الاعتماد عليه لأن له أصلاً صحيحاً يقاس عليه، والظاهر أن هذه المسألة لم تنقل عن أبي حنيفة صريحة ولذا يعبرون عنها بصيغة «قالوا». والظاهر أن المراد من الأمة في المختصر القنة والمدبرة وأم الولد وأما المكاتبة فينبغي أن يكون الإذن إليها لأن الولد لم يكن للمولى ولم أره صريحاً.
Book of Nikah, Chapter on Marriage of Slaves. From where it was, when semen came out, repeating ghusl became obligatory. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: A man has a non-muhsanah slave girl who goes out and comes in and the master practices withdrawal (azl) from her, and she gives birth to a child and his predominant assumption is that it is not from him, he has latitude to deny him. But if she is muhsanah, he does not have latitude to deny him, because he might have withdrawn and water fell into the outer vagina then entered, so he cannot rely on withdrawal absolutely. This indicates the weakness of the preceding detail, and that denying is not permissible for a muhsanah, and that its permissibility is conditioned by three: that she is not muhsanah, existence of withdrawal from him, and predominant assumption that it is not from him. And it may be said: That which is in al-Mi'raj is an explanation of the place of predominant assumption that it is not from him. So if he withdrew and did not repeat, his predominant assumption is that it is not from him, on condition that she is not muhsanah, and thus reconciliation is achieved. And it ought to be that a woman closing the mouth of her womb, as women do to prevent pregnancy, is prohibited without the husband's permission, analogously to his withdrawal without her permission. And in Fath al-Qadir: Is abortion permissible after conception? It is permissible as long as nothing has been created from it. Then in another place: That is only after one hundred and twenty days. This requires that by 'creation' they meant the breathing of the soul; otherwise it is a mistake, because creation is observed before this period. In al-Khaniyah, from Kitab al-Karahiyah: I do not say that abortion is absolutely permissible. For the muhrim, if he breaks the egg of game, he is liable because it is the origin of the game. Since he is taken for the ransom, then at the very least she incurs sin here if she aborts without excuse. So it is appropriate to rely on this, because it has a sound basis for analogy. Apparently this issue is not explicitly transmitted from Abu Hanifa, so they express it with the formula 'they said'. And apparently the meaning of 'ummah' in al-Mukhtasar includes the full slave, the mudabbara, and the umm walad. As for the mukataba, it seems that the permission is to her because the child is not for the master, and I have not seen it explicitly.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from various sources on issues related to reproductive rights in the context of slave ownership. The first ruling, from Fatawa Qadikhan, concerns a master denying paternity of a child born to his female slave. The permissibility is conditional: the slave must not be muhsanah (i.e., not previously married or in a state that establishes lineage firmly), the master must have practiced 'azl (coitus interruptus), and his predominant assumption must be that the child is not from him. If the slave is muhsanah, denial is not allowed because there is a strong possibility that despite withdrawal, some semen entered. The author notes a reconciliation between the earlier and later texts: the prevailing view is that withdrawal without subsequent bath (ghusl) after urination ensures presumption. Next, the author extends the ruling by analogy: a woman blocking her womb to prevent pregnancy is prohibited without her husband's permission, just as withdrawal without her permission is disliked. Then, the permissibility of abortion (isqat) is discussed. According to Fath al-Qadir, abortion is permissible before any creation (takhalluq), which they interpret as the soul being breathed at 120 days. However, al-Khaniyah cautions that it is not absolutely permissible, drawing an analogy to breaking the egg of a game animal in ihram, which incurs liability. The author concludes that the relied-upon view is that abortion without excuse incurs sin, as it is analogous to destroying the origin of life. This issue, not explicitly from Abu Hanifa, is expressed with 'they said'. Finally, the term 'ummah' in the matn is clarified to include full slave, mudabbara, and umm walad, but for a mukataba, permission regarding her child rests with her.

Key Terms
عزل ('azl): Coitus interruptus: withdrawing the penis before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy.
محصن (muhsan): Literally 'fortified'; in this context, a slave who is married or in a state that establishes lineage firmly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What three conditions must be met for a master to be in latitude (s'a) to deny paternity of his slave's child?
A) The slave is muhsanah, the master performed 'azl, and he has certainty.
B) The slave is non-muhsanah, the master performed 'azl, and he has predominant assumption.
C) The slave is muhsanah, the master did not perform 'azl, and he has doubt.
D) The slave is non-muhsanah, the master did not perform 'azl, and he has certainty.

Key Takeaway
Denying paternity is a serious legal act subject to strict conditions; without all three, it is not permissible.

The Hanafi Ruling on Abortion Before Ensoulment
Ruling on abortion with qiyas analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on abortion before 120 days and apply the qiyas analogy to determine when it incurs sin.

Core Idea
Abortion is permissible before 120 days but incurs sin without an excuse; after 120 days, it is prohibited.

This section from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the permissibility of abortion (isqat) after conception. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the stages of fetal development, using the concept of 'takhalluq' (creation/form) tied to the breathing of the soul at 120 days. The author presents a qiyas analogy from the rules of ihram to highlight the moral gravity of abortion without excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time limit for permissibility
Fath al-Qadir states that abortion is permissible as long as nothing has been 'created' (takhalluq) from the fetus. The author clarifies that takhalluq here means the breathing of the soul, which occurs after 120 days.
Before 120 days, the fetus is considered a mere clot/embryo without a soul.
2
Understand the analogy from ihram
Al-Khaniyah draws an analogy between aborting a fetus and breaking the egg of a game animal by a muhrim. The egg is the origin of the bird, so breaking it incurs liability. Similarly, the fetus is the origin of a human life, so aborting without excuse incurs sin.
This analogy strengthens the view that abortion is not absolutely permissible.
3
Determine the relied-upon view
The author states that al-Khaniyah's view (that abortion without excuse incurs sin) should be relied upon because it has a sound basis for analogy. This becomes the mufta bihi position.
Thus the ruling: permissible before 120 days but makruh tanzih (disliked) without a valid reason; haram after 120 days.
4
Note the absence of explicit transmission from Abu Hanifa
The text remarks that this issue is not explicitly narrated from Abu Hanifa, hence later jurists use the expression 'they said' (said by the mashayikh).
This indicates the ruling is derived by later scholars (mutakhkhirin) through ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الرقيق من حيث كانت فخرج المني وجب إعادة الغسل. كذا في المعراج وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : رجل له جارية غير محصنة تخرج وتدخل ويعزل عنها المولى فجاءت بولد وأكبر ظنه أنه ليس منه كان في سعة نفيه، وإن كانت محصنة لا يسعه نفيه لأنه ربما يعزل فيقع الماء في الفرج الخارج ثم يدخل فلا مطلقاً يعتمد على العزل اهـ. وهذا يفيد ضعف التفصيل المتقدم وأنه لا يحل النفي محصنة وأن جوازه مشروط بثلاثة : عدم تحصينها ووجود العزل منه وغلبة الظن بأنه ليس منه. وقد يقال : إن ما في المعراج بيان لمحل غلبة الظن بأنه ليس منه فإذا كان قد عزل ولم يعد غلب على ظنه أنه ليس منه بشرط أن لا تكون محصنة وبه يحصل التوفيق. وينبغي أن يكون سد المرأة فم رحمها كما تفعله النساء لمنع الولد حراماً بغير إذن الزوج قياساً على عزله بغير إذنها. وفي فتح القدير : وهل يباح الإسقاط بعد الحبل ؟ يباح ما لم يتخلق شيء منه . ثم في غير موضع : ولا يكون ذلك إلا بعد مائة وعشرين يوماً. وهذا يقتضي أنهم أرادوا بالتخليق نفخ الروح وإلا فهو غلط لأن التخليق يتحقق بالمشاهدة قبل هذه المدة اهـ. وفي الخانية من كتاب الكراهية ولا أقول بأنه يباح الإسقاط مطلقاً فإن المحرم إذا كسر بيض الصيد يكون ضامناً لأنه أصل الصيد فلما كان يؤاخذ بالجزاء ثم فلا أقل من أن يلحقها إثم ههنا إذا أسقطت بغير عذر ا هـ . وينبغي الاعتماد عليه لأن له أصلاً صحيحاً يقاس عليه، والظاهر أن هذه المسألة لم تنقل عن أبي حنيفة صريحة ولذا يعبرون عنها بصيغة «قالوا». والظاهر أن المراد من الأمة في المختصر القنة والمدبرة وأم الولد وأما المكاتبة فينبغي أن يكون الإذن إليها لأن الولد لم يكن للمولى ولم أره صريحاً.
Book of Nikah, Chapter on Marriage of Slaves. From where it was, when semen came out, repeating ghusl became obligatory. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: A man has a non-muhsanah slave girl who goes out and comes in and the master practices withdrawal (azl) from her, and she gives birth to a child and his predominant assumption is that it is not from him, he has latitude to deny him. But if she is muhsanah, he does not have latitude to deny him, because he might have withdrawn and water fell into the outer vagina then entered, so he cannot rely on withdrawal absolutely. This indicates the weakness of the preceding detail, and that denying is not permissible for a muhsanah, and that its permissibility is conditioned by three: that she is not muhsanah, existence of withdrawal from him, and predominant assumption that it is not from him. And it may be said: That which is in al-Mi'raj is an explanation of the place of predominant assumption that it is not from him. So if he withdrew and did not repeat, his predominant assumption is that it is not from him, on condition that she is not muhsanah, and thus reconciliation is achieved. And it ought to be that a woman closing the mouth of her womb, as women do to prevent pregnancy, is prohibited without the husband's permission, analogously to his withdrawal without her permission. And in Fath al-Qadir: Is abortion permissible after conception? It is permissible as long as nothing has been created from it. Then in another place: That is only after one hundred and twenty days. This requires that by 'creation' they meant the breathing of the soul; otherwise it is a mistake, because creation is observed before this period. In al-Khaniyah, from Kitab al-Karahiyah: I do not say that abortion is absolutely permissible. For the muhrim, if he breaks the egg of game, he is liable because it is the origin of the game. Since he is taken for the ransom, then at the very least she incurs sin here if she aborts without excuse. So it is appropriate to rely on this, because it has a sound basis for analogy. Apparently this issue is not explicitly transmitted from Abu Hanifa, so they express it with the formula 'they said'. And apparently the meaning of 'ummah' in al-Mukhtasar includes the full slave, the mudabbara, and the umm walad. As for the mukataba, it seems that the permission is to her because the child is not for the master, and I have not seen it explicitly.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from various sources on issues related to reproductive rights in the context of slave ownership. The first ruling, from Fatawa Qadikhan, concerns a master denying paternity of a child born to his female slave. The permissibility is conditional: the slave must not be muhsanah (i.e., not previously married or in a state that establishes lineage firmly), the master must have practiced 'azl (coitus interruptus), and his predominant assumption must be that the child is not from him. If the slave is muhsanah, denial is not allowed because there is a strong possibility that despite withdrawal, some semen entered. The author notes a reconciliation between the earlier and later texts: the prevailing view is that withdrawal without subsequent bath (ghusl) after urination ensures presumption. Next, the author extends the ruling by analogy: a woman blocking her womb to prevent pregnancy is prohibited without her husband's permission, just as withdrawal without her permission is disliked. Then, the permissibility of abortion (isqat) is discussed. According to Fath al-Qadir, abortion is permissible before any creation (takhalluq), which they interpret as the soul being breathed at 120 days. However, al-Khaniyah cautions that it is not absolutely permissible, drawing an analogy to breaking the egg of a game animal in ihram, which incurs liability. The author concludes that the relied-upon view is that abortion without excuse incurs sin, as it is analogous to destroying the origin of life. This issue, not explicitly from Abu Hanifa, is expressed with 'they said'. Finally, the term 'ummah' in the matn is clarified to include full slave, mudabbara, and umm walad, but for a mukataba, permission regarding her child rests with her.

Key Terms
تخليق (takhalluq): Creation of a human form in the womb; interpreted in this text as the breathing of the soul at 120 days.
إسقاط (isqat): Abortion: expulsion of the fetus from the womb before viability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, what is the ruling on abortion before 120 days without a valid excuse?
A) It is absolutely permissible (mubah).
B) It is permissible but disliked and sinful (makruh tanzih).
C) It is prohibited (haram) because it is before 120 days?
D) It is permissible only for the muhrim.

Key Takeaway
Abortion is not absolutely permissible even before ensoulment; there is moral responsibility and sin if done without a valid excuse.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
The Option of Freedom for Slave Women (Khiyar al-'Itq)
Explains the ruling and scope of khiyar al-'itq for slave women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-'itq, explain the proof from the hadith of Barirah, and distinguish the positions of the majority Hanafi imams and Zufar regarding the mukataba.

Core Idea
A freed slave woman has the option to annul her marriage—the increase of ownership is the cause, and the hadith of Barirah is clear proof, even for the mukataba.

This lesson explores the right of a slave woman to annul her marriage upon being freed. We will examine the central proof from the incident of Barirah, understand why the Hanafi school includes the mukataba (slave under contract) in this ruling, and see how the disagreement with Zufar is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act of khiyar al-'itq? Who receives it? What is the ruling?
The ruling is that a slave woman has the right to choose to annul or remain in her marriage upon freedom.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Barirah: what does it narrate? Where is it recorded?
Barirah was a mukataba of 'A'isha (ra) and was given the option. Narrated in Muwatta' and Sahihayn.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? What is the effective cause (illa)?
The majority say the cause is increase of ownership (izdiyad al-milk), which applies to all slave women including the mukataba.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar's objection: the mukataba already owns her private part. What is the Hanafi response?
The response: before freedom she did not have full control (e.g., could not marry without permission). After freedom she does.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Why is the majority view relied upon?
Especially due to the clear text in Muwatta' that Barirah was a mukataba and was given the option.
