في الأصل ثم صارت أمة ثم أعتقت لما في المبسوط : لو كانت حرة في أصل العقد ثم صارت أمة ثم أعتقت بأن ارتدت امرأة مع زوجها ولحقا بدار الحرب معاً ـ والعياذ بالله تعالى - ثم سبيا معاً فأعتقت الأمة كان لها الخيار عند أبي يوسف لأنها بالعتق ملكت أمر نفسها وازداد ملك الزوج عليها ولا خيار لها عند محمد لأن بأصل العقد يثبت عليها ملك كامل برضاها ثم انتقص الملك فإذا أعتقت عاد إلى أصله كما كان .اهـ. ولا يخفى ترجيح قول أبي يوسف لدخولها تحت النص. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان أن خيار البلوغ يفارق خيار العتق من وجوه أحدها أن خيار العتق يبطل بالقيام من المجلس. والثاني أن الجهل بخيار العتق عذر. والثالث أنه يثبت للأمة دون الغلام والرابع أنه لا يبطل بالسكوت وإن كانت بكراً. والخامس أن الفرقة لا تتوقف فيه على القضاء بخلاف خيار البلوغ في الكل. وفيها أيضاً أن خيار العتق بمنزلة خيار المخيرة وإنما يفارقه من وجه واحد وهو أن الفرقة في خيار العتق عذر. والثالث أنه يثبت للأمة دون الغلام والرابع أنه لا يبطل بالسكوت وإن كانت بكراً. والخامس أن الفرقة لا تتوقف فيه على القضاء بخلاف خيار البلوغ في الكل. وفيها أيضاً أن خيار العتق بمنزلة خيار المخيرة وإنما يفارقه من وجه واحد وهو أن الفرقة في خيار العتق لا تكون طلاقاً، وفي خيار المخيرة يكون طلاقاً .اهـ. ويزاد على هذا ما في جامع الفصولين أن الجهل بأن لها الخيار في خيار المخيرة ليس بعذر بخلافه في الإعتاق، وفرقوا بينهما بأن الأمة لا تتفرغ للعلم بخلاف المخيرة ومقتضاه أن المخيرة لو كانت أمة فإنها تعذر بالجهل اهـ. وفيه أيضاً أن الأمة إذا أعتقت في عدة الرجعي لها الخيار.
ثم اعلم أن الظاهر الإطلاق من أن الجهل في المخيرة ليس بعذر لأنهم عللوا كونه عذراً في خيار العتق بعلتين إحداهما أن الأمة مشغولة بخدمة المولى فلا تتفرغ لمعرفة أن لها الخيار بخلاف الجهل بخيار البلوغ فإن الحرة الصغيرة لم تكن مشغولة بخدمة أحد . ثانيهما أن سبب الخيار في العتق لا يعلمه إلا الخواص من الناس لخفائه بخلاف خيار البلوغ لأنه ظاهر
لتزويجها منفي لأنه صرح في باب المكاتب بأنها بعقد الكتابة خرجت من يد المولى فصار كالأجنبي وصارت أحق بنفسها ويغرم المولى العقر إن وطئها .اهـ. وقوله وصارت أحق بنفسها» ليس على إطلاقه لبقاء ملك المولى في رقبتها فلا ينفذ تزويجها بدون إذن مولاها كما لا ينفذ تزويجه بدون رضاها لموجب الكتابة. وعبارة كافي النسفي المكاتبة إذا تزوجت بإذن مولاها ثم عتقت خيرت اهـ . فليتنبه لذلك اهـ . قلت : ويؤيده قول المؤلف في الرد على الكمال وإنما لم يجز وطؤها للمولى وجبرها على النكاح لا لأجل أنها مكلت بضعها بعقد الكتابة». وكذا ما صرح به عند قوله «وله إجبارهما على النكاح حيث قال : وخرج المكاتب والمكاتبة والصغيرة فليس له إجبارهما عليه لأنهما التحقا بالأحرار تصرفاً فيشترط رضاهما .اهـ. وفي المعراج ولا يجوز تزويج المكاتب والمكاتبة جبراً بالإجماع قوله (ثم اعلم أن الظاهر الإطلاق من أن الجهل كذا في هذه النسخة فقوله «من أن الجهل» متعلق
And it is preferred. The Book of Marriage, Chapter on Marriage of Slaves. I mean the increase of ownership over her is found in the mukataba because her 'iddah is [like that of] a free woman and her divorce is two [pronouncements]. The narration in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim about Barirah's husband differed: it was narrated that he was free and narrated that he was a slave. Our imams [preferred] the first because in the principles it is confirmed, and the narration that he was a slave is negative of knowledge that his original state was slavery, and the negator is the one who preserves it and negates the accidental matter, while the confirmer is the one who removes it. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir preferred the view of Zufar that the mukataba when freed has no option because his statement (peace be upon him) 'you have owned your private part' only means the benefits of your private part since it is impossible for her to own its essence, and her ownership of her earning is subsidiary to her ownership of the benefits of herself, so it follows that she owned her private part in the intended sense before freedom, so the text does not apply to her. This is based on the cause being her ownership of her private part through freedom, while the majority [hold] that the cause is the increase of ownership over her, which exists in the mukataba. And if the cause is ownership of the private part, there is no doubt that she did not own the benefits of her private part completely before freedom, as evidenced by the fact that she cannot marry herself without the master's permission, and she owns that after freedom, so it is correct to say that she owned her private part by freedom and thus falls under the text. Rather, the master's intercourse with her and compulsion to marriage are not permitted not because she owned her private part, but because of the kitaba contract, for it necessitates non-interference in her earning. So the view of our imams is preferred, especially since Malik narrated in al-Muwatta' that Barirah was a mukataba of 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) and that she was given the option when she was freed, making it a clear text on the matter, so Zufar is refuted by it. The absolute term 'slave woman' includes the qinna, the mudabbara, and the umm walad, and includes the adult and the minor. So if a minor is freed, her option is deferred until she reaches puberty, because dissolution of marriage is among acts that are between benefit and harm, so a minor cannot exercise it nor can her guardian exercise it on her behalf since he stands in her place. Thus in Jami' al-Fusulain; when she reaches puberty, she has the option of freedom, not the option of puberty, according to the most correct view. Thus in al-Dhakhira and we have mentioned it earlier. And it includes whether the marriage was [contracted] or not, and whether it occurred with her consent or by compulsion. And it includes whether she was free. Al-Dhakhira: If she wants to abort before the time when the soul is breathed in, is that permissible for her or not? They differed on it, and al-Faqih 'Ali ibn Musa said that it is disliked, because the water after it falls into the womb has the potential for life, so it has the ruling of life, like the egg of the game of the Haram and similar. Thus in al-Zahiriyya. Ibn Wahban said: Permissibility of abortion is based on an excuse or that she is not sinful with the sin of killing. By what is in al-Dhakhira it becomes clear that they intended by 'formation' (takhliq) only the breathing of the soul, and that Qadikhan is preceded by what has passed of comprehension. His statement (because the child was not for the master): The annotator Miskin said: This reasoning also requires that withdrawal does not depend on the master's permission if the husband stipulates freedom of his children because there is no ownership of the master in the children then, and I have not seen it. His statement (it integrates the two parts, i.e. whether her husband is free or not). His statement (for knowledge that it was...) the lam is for causation, not attached to the negative. His statement (and it includes whether the marriage was or not, occurring with her consent or by compulsion): al-Zayla'i said: If a slave woman or mukataba is freed, she is given the option even if her husband is free, and there is no difference in this whether the marriage was with her consent or without her consent. Similarly in al-Durar. He said in al-Sharnabaliyya: Negation of the mukataba's consent... between... that in the original then she became a slave then was freed, as in al-Mabsut: If [a woman] was free at the time of the contract, then became a slave, then was freed, such as a woman who apostatized with her husband and both joined the enemy territory simultaneously - Allah forbid - then were both taken captive together, and the slave woman was freed, she has the option according to Abu Yusuf because by freedom she owns her own affair and the husband's ownership over her has increased, and she has no option according to Muhammad because by the original contract full ownership is established over her with her consent, then ownership decreased, and when she was freed it returned to its original state. It is not hidden that Abu Yusuf's view is preponderant because she falls under the text. In Fatawa Qadikhan: The option of puberty differs from the option of freedom in several aspects: First, the option of freedom is invalidated by standing up from the session. Second, ignorance concerning the option of freedom is an excuse. Third, it is established for the slave woman but not for the slave boy. Fourth, it is not invalidated by silence even if she is a virgin. Fifth, separation in it does not depend on judiciary, contrary to the option of puberty in all. Also: The option of freedom is like the option of the mukhayyara, differing only in one aspect: separation in the option of freedom is not a divorce, while in the option of the mukhayyara it is a divorce. What is added to this from Jami' al-Fusulain is that ignorance of having the option in the mukhayyara is not an excuse, contrary to in manumission, and they distinguished between them because the slave woman is not free to learn, unlike the mukhayyara, and its implication is that if the mukhayyara were a slave, she would be excused by ignorance. Also: If a slave woman is freed during the 'idda of a revocable divorce, she has the option. Then know that the absolute appears that ignorance in the mukhayyara is not an excuse, because they justified it being an excuse in the option of freedom by two causes: one, that the slave woman is occupied with serving the master so she is not free to know that she has the option, unlike ignorance of the option of puberty because the free minor is not occupied with serving anyone. Second, that the cause of the option in freedom is known only to the elite due to its subtlety, unlike the option of puberty for it is apparent. For her marriage is negated, because it is stated in the chapter on the mukatab that by the kitaba contract she leaves the master's hand, so she becomes like a foreigner and becomes more entitled to herself, and the master pays the dower if he has intercourse with her. His statement 'and she becomes more entitled to herself' is not absolute, as the master's ownership of her person remains, so her marriage is not valid without her master's permission, just as his marriage is not valid without her consent due to the kitaba obligation. The expression of Kafi al-Nasafi: The mukataba, if she marries with her master's permission then is freed, is given the option. So one should be careful about that. I say: This is supported by the author's statement in refutation of al-Kamal: 'Rather, the master's intercourse with her and compulsion to marriage are not permitted because she owned her private part by the kitaba contract.' Also what he stated in his statement 'And he may compel both of them to marriage' where he said: 'The mukatab, mukataba, and minor are excluded, so he cannot compel them to marriage because they are joined to free persons in action, so their consent is required.' In al-Mi'raj: It is not permissible to marry the mukatab and mukataba by compulsion by consensus. His statement (then know that the absolute appears that ignorance...). In this copy, his statement 'min anna al-jahl' is attached.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion and commentary on the ruling of khiyar al-'itq (option of freedom) for slave women. The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, is explaining the scope and details of this option, addressing various issues and disputes within the Hanafi school.

First, the passage establishes that the option upon freedom applies to both an ordinary slave woman (ama) and a mukataba (a slave woman under a manumission contract). This is contested by Zufar, who argued that a mukataba does not get the option because she already holds ownership over her private parts before freedom. The commentator explains that the majority of Hanafi imams disagree, holding that the effective cause (illa) is the increase of ownership over her (izdiyad al-milk), which occurs when she is freed. The passage uses the famous hadith of Barirah as proof: her husband was reported to be free, and she was given the option when freed. The commentator cites Imam Malik's narration in al-Muwatta' that Barirah was a mukataba of 'A'isha, making it a clear textual proof against Zufar.

The ruling is then extended to all types of slave women: qinna (full slave), mudabbara (one whose manumission is promised upon the master's death), and umm walad (a slave woman who bears her master's child). It also applies whether the slave woman is an adult or a minor. However, if a minor slave girl is freed, her option is deferred until she reaches puberty because she cannot make such a significant decision. At puberty, she has the option of freedom, not the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh).

The option applies regardless of whether the slave woman was married with her consent or by compulsion before freedom. The passage also addresses a tangential issue: if a freed woman wishes to abort before the soul is breathed in, it is disliked (makruh) according to some, as the water in the womb has potential life. However, the commentator notes that permission may be given in cases of excuse.

Next, the passage discusses a special scenario: a woman who was free at the time of marriage, then became a slave (e.g., through apostasy and captivity), then is freed again. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf says she has the option because she gains ownership of herself, while Muhammad says she does not because the original marriage had full ownership. The commentator favors Abu Yusuf's view.

Then, the passage compares khiyar al-'itq with khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and khiyar al-mukhayyara (option given to a free woman who was married before puberty but reached puberty after marriage). Five or six differences are listed: (1) khiyar al-'itq is lost by leaving the session, (2) ignorance of the option is an excuse, (3) it applies only to slave women, not male slaves, (4) it is not lost by silence even if the woman is a virgin, (5) separation does not require judicial intervention, (6) when separation occurs in khiyar al-'itq it is not a divorce, but in khiyar al-mukhayyara it is a divorce. Additionally, ignorance is not an excuse in khiyar al-mukhayyara for a free woman, but it is for a slave woman due to her occupation.

The passage concludes with remarks that the mukataba's marriage with her master's permission is valid and she has the option when freed, and that the master cannot compel her into marriage.

Key Terms
خيار العتق (khiyar al-‘itq): The right of a slave woman to annul her marriage when she is freed.
مكاتبة (mukataba): A female slave who has a written contract with her master to earn her freedom by paying installments.
ازدياد الملك (izdiyad al-milk): The increase of ownership over herself that occurs when a slave woman is freed; this is the effective cause (illa) for the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi ruling regarding a mukataba who is freed?
A) She does not have the option (khiyar) because she already owned her private part before freedom.
B) She has the option, based on the hadith of Barirah.
C) She only has the option if her husband is also a slave.
D) The option is deferred until she reaches the age of puberty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school firmly establishes the option of freedom for all slave women, including the mukataba, based on the hadith of Barirah and the cause of increased ownership.

When a Free Woman Becomes a Slave and Is Freed: The Dispute over Khiyar al-'Itq
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-'itq for a woman with fluctuating status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on khiyar al-'itq in this scenario, and explain why the preferred view is Abu Yusuf's.

Core Idea
Free will at contract matters: Muhammad sees return to original; Abu Yusuf sees new option upon freedom.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case: a woman was free at marriage, then became a slave due to apostasy and capture, then was freed. Does she have khiyar al-'itq (option to annul the marriage)? This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, each with a distinct reasoning. Understanding their arguments clarifies how the Hanafi school treats the khiyar al-'itq in non-standard situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact scenario
The woman was free at the time of the marriage contract, then she apostatized with her husband and joined dar al-harb, then both were captured and she became a slave, then she was freed.
This is not the standard case of a slave married while a slave.
2
Compare the two positions
Abu Yusuf says she has khiyar; Muhammad says she does not.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each imam
Abu Yusuf: freedom gives her control (milk) and increases the husband's ownership. Muhammad: original contract established full ownership over her with her consent, so when freed it returns to the original state as if nothing changed.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The author prefers Abu Yusuf because this case falls under the general text (nass) for khiyar al-'itq.
The nass likely refers to the hadith that a slave woman freed after marriage has the option.
5
Connect to the general rule
The standard khiyar al-'itq for a slave freed after marriage is established; this case extends it to a woman who was free then enslaved then freed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأصل ثم صارت أمة ثم أعتقت لما في المبسوط : لو كانت حرة في أصل العقد ثم صارت أمة ثم أعتقت بأن ارتدت امرأة مع زوجها ولحقا بدار الحرب معاً ـ والعياذ بالله تعالى - ثم سبيا معاً فأعتقت الأمة كان لها الخيار عند أبي يوسف لأنها بالعتق ملكت أمر نفسها وازداد ملك الزوج عليها ولا خيار لها عند محمد لأن بأصل العقد يثبت عليها ملك كامل برضاها ثم انتقص الملك فإذا أعتقت عاد إلى أصله كما كان .اهـ. ولا يخفى ترجيح قول أبي يوسف لدخولها تحت النص.
In the original [contract] then she became a slave then she was freed, as in al-Mabsut: If a woman was free in the original contract, then became a slave, then was freed — such as if a woman apostatized with her husband and both joined the enemy territory (may Allah protect us), then both were captured together, and the slave woman was freed — she has the option according to Abu Yusuf because by freedom she gains control over herself and the husband's ownership over her increases; and she has no option according to Muhammad because by the original contract complete ownership was established over her with her consent, then the ownership decreased, so when she is freed it returns to its original state as it was. End. And the preferability of Abu Yusuf's opinion is not hidden because she falls under the text.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the khiyar al-'itq for a woman who was free at the time of the marriage contract, then became a slave due to apostasy and capture, and then was freed. Abu Yusuf says she has the option because freedom gives her control and increases the husband's ownership (implying a change in status). Muhammad says she has no option because the original contract established full marital authority over her with her consent, and the later reduction and restoration to original state does not create a new option. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq says the preferred view is Abu Yusuf's because it aligns with the general text (nass) regarding khiyar al-'itq.

Key Terms
خيار العتق (khiyar al-'itq): The option granted to a slave woman who is freed after being married while a slave, allowing her to annul or continue the marriage.
المبسوط (al-Mabsut): A major Hanafi fiqh work by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.
النص (al-nass): The textual evidence (usually a hadith) that establishes khiyar al-'itq for a woman freed after marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, why does the freed slave woman have khiyar al-'itq?
A) Because she was free at the time of contract.
B) Because freedom gives her control over herself.
C) Because the husband's ownership increased.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when a woman who was free at marriage becomes enslaved and then freed, the preferred view is that she retains khiyar al-'itq, allowing her to annul or continue the marriage based on her new status.

Five Differences between Khiyar al-Bulugh and Khiyar al-'Itq
Enumeration of five differences between two options in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five differences between khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itq and apply each difference to identify which option governs a given scenario.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-'itq is a session-based option; ignorance excuses, but silence does not nullify it.

This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan contrasts two important options in Hanafi marriage law: the option of puberty and the option of freedom. While both allow annulment of a marriage under specific circumstances, they differ in five key rulings—ranging from nullification conditions to who may exercise them. Understanding these differences is crucial for correctly advising in nikah cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options compared
Name both khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itq, and recall their basic definitions from earlier study.
Khiyar al-bulugh: for minors married before puberty. Khiyar al-'itq: for slave women freed after marriage.
2
List the five differences as enumerated in the passage
Read each of the five points carefully: (1) nullification by standing, (2) ignorance as excuse, (3) who can exercise it, (4) effect of silence, (5) need for qadhi.
Note that the passage states 'unlike khiyar al-bulugh in all these points'—meaning khiyar al-bulugh has the opposite ruling.
3
Understand the legal implication of each difference
For each difference, consider what it means in practice. For example, the first difference means khiyar al-'itq is a 'session option' while khiyar al-bulugh is not.
Session option: must be exercised before leaving the meeting.
4
Relate differences to underlying rationale
Think about why each rule exists. For instance, the third difference (only for female slaves) reflects that a male slave's marriage is not affected by his freedom in the same way.
Rationales help memory and application.
5
Apply the differences to a scenario
Given a hypothetical situation (e.g., a freed slave woman remains silent after the contract), determine which option applies and whether it is nullified.
Use the differences as a checklist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان أن خيار البلوغ يفارق خيار العتق من وجوه أحدها أن خيار العتق يبطل بالقيام من المجلس. والثاني أن الجهل بخيار العتق عذر. والثالث أنه يثبت للأمة دون الغلام والرابع أنه لا يبطل بالسكوت وإن كانت بكراً. والخامس أن الفرقة لا تتوقف فيه على القضاء بخلاف خيار البلوغ في الكل.
And in Fatawa Qadikhan: The option of puberty differs from the option of freedom in several ways: First, the option of freedom is nullified by standing from the session. Second, ignorance of the option of freedom is an excuse. Third, it is established for the slave woman but not for the slave boy. Fourth, it is not nullified by silence even if she is a virgin. Fifth, separation in it does not require judicial decree, unlike the option of puberty in all these points.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan lists five distinguishing features between khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty for a minor given in marriage) and khiyar al-'itq (option of freedom for a slave woman freed after marriage). The five differences are: (1) Khiyar al-'itq is lost if the woman stands from the session of contract, implying it is a session option; khiyar al-bulugh is not. (2) Ignorance of the option is an excuse in khiyar al-'itq, so a woman who did not know can exercise it later, unlike khiyar al-bulugh. (3) Khiyar al-'itq is only for female slaves, not male slaves; khiyar al-bulugh applies to both males and females. (4) Silence does not nullify khiyar al-'itq even if she is a virgin (who is expected to be shy); in khiyar al-bulugh, silence of a virgin is considered consent. (5) Separation in khiyar al-'itq does not require a judge's ruling; in khiyar al-bulugh, it requires a qadhi to annul the marriage.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option of puberty: a minor given in marriage by a guardian can annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
خيار العتق (khiyar al-'itq): The option of freedom: a slave woman married while a slave can annul the marriage upon being freed.
القيام من المجلس (al-qiyam min al-majlis): Standing from the session; a physical act that nullifies session-based options.
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence; for khiyar al-bulugh, silence of a virgin indicates consent and nullifies the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a difference between khiyar al-'itq and khiyar al-bulugh?
A) Khiyar al-'itq requires a judicial decree.
B) Ignorance is an excuse in khiyar al-bulugh.
C) Khiyar al-'itq is nullified by standing from the session.
D) Khiyar al-bulugh applies only to female slaves.

Key Takeaway
Remember the five differences: session nullification, ignorance excuse, gender applicability, effect of silence, and need for judicial decree.

Emancipation Following Marriage Without Permission: No Option for the Freed Slave
Ruling with rational proof on slave marriage and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave's marriage contracted without permission becomes effective upon emancipation without an option to annul, and explain the sub-rulings for mudabbara, umm walad, and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Emancipation removes the master's impediment, making the prior marriage effective without giving the slave any option to annul.

Imagine a female slave contracts a marriage without her master's permission. The contract is valid in itself but its enforcement is blocked by the master's right. If she is later emancipated—whether by the master's act, his death, or otherwise—does the marriage automatically become valid? And if so, does she have the right to annul it? This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq resolves these questions, establishing the main rule and then navigating the special cases of the mudabbara (promised freedom) and the umm walad (mother of her master's child), including a well-known difference between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its rationale
Locate the sentence 'ولو نكحت بلا إذن فعتقت نفذ بلا خيار'. Note that the marriage was concluded while she was a slave, so the contract was valid but obstructed by the master's right. Emancipation removes that obstruction, causing the contract to take effect. She has no option (khiyar) because enforcement occurs after freedom, not at the moment of freedom.
The rationale distinguishes this from khiyar al-‘itq, which applies when a slave marries with permission and then is freed.
2
Distinguish subcategories: qinn, mudabbara, umm walad
The text notes that the absolute ruling includes the qinn (full slave), mudabbara (promised freedom upon master's death), and umm walad (mother of master's child). For a mudabbara freed during the master's lifetime, the same rule applies. For a mudabbara freed by the master's death, an ikhtilaf arises. For an umm walad, the marriage does not take effect due to the obligatory 'idda from the master at the moment of emancipation.
The 'idda of the umm walad prevents the marriage from being enforced immediately, and it is compared to marriage during another's 'idda, which is invalid.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf for mudabbara freed by death
According to Abu Hanifa, if the mudabbara does not come out of one third of the estate (i.e., the master's estate is insufficient to cover her full value), she must pay sā‘ya (compensation to the heirs) for the marriage to be valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the marriage is valid without payment. Identify the basis: Abu Hanifa sees partial ownership by heirs as an impediment; the Sahibayn see emancipation as removing all impediment.
The text does not explicitly choose a tarjih, but later Hanafi fatwa often follows the Sahibayn.
4
Apply the principle to other cases
The passage extends the reasoning: the same rule applies to a male slave (no difference). It also contrasts with cases where the original contract was not valid (e.g., minor's marriage, distant guardian, purchase by slave before emancipation). Understand that the rule of 'removal of impediment' only works when the contract was valid in itself but merely blocked by a superior right.
The author uses these comparisons to reinforce the logic and scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو نكحت بلا إذن فعتقت نفذ بلا خيار) أي نكحت الأمة بغير إذن المولى ثم أعتقت فإنه ينفذ ذلك النكاح من جهتها لأنها من أهل العبارة وامتناع النفوذ لحق المولى وقد زال، ولا خيار لها لأن النفوذ بعد العتق فلا تتحقق زيادة الملك كما إذا زوجت نفسها بعد العتق. ولذا قال الأسبيجابي الأصل أن عقد النكاح متى تم على المرأة وهي مملوكة يثبت لها خيار العتق، ومتى تم عليها وهي حرة لا يثبت لها خيار العتق .اهـ. ولو اقترنا لا خيار لها كما لو زوجها فضولي وأعتقها فضولي فأجاز المولى الكل فإنه لا خيار لها. كذا في تلخيص الجامع. أطلق . في الأمة فشمل القنة والمدبرة وأم الولد والمكاتبة لكن في المدبرة وأم الولد تفصيل؛ ففي المديرة إن أعتقها المولى في حياته فالحكم كالقنة إذا أعتقت. وإن عتقت بموت المولى فقال في الظهيرية : لو تزوجت مدبرة بغير إذن مولاها ثم مات المولى وقد خرجت من الثلث جاز النكاح وإن لم تخرج لم يجز حتى تؤدي السعاية عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يجوز اهـ. وأما أم الولد إذا أعتقها أو مات عنها المولى فإن النكاح لا ينفذ لأن العدة وجبت عليها من المولى كما عتقت والعدة تمنع نفاذ النكاح. كذا في المحيط والخانية. وينبغي أن يقال في جواب المسألة فإن النكاح يبطل لأنه لا يمكن توقفه مع وجود العدة إذ النكاح في عدة الغير فاسد، ويدل عليه ما زاد في المحيط في هذه المسألة : فإن دخل بها الزوج قبل العتق نفذ النكاح. وهذا إنما يصح على رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد لأنه وجبت العدة من الزوج فلا تجب العدة من المولى، ولا يصح على ظاهر الرواية لأنه لا تجب العدة من الزوج فوجبت العدة من المولى، ووجوب العدة من المولى قبل الإجازة يوجب انفساح النكاح اهـ. فقوله «يوجب الإنفساخ ظاهر فيه، وإنما قيد المصنف بالأمة مع أن الحكم في العبد أنه إذا تزوج بلا إذن ثم أعتق فإن النكاح ينفذ لزوال المائع فيهما لأجل أن يبين نفي الخيار ولذا قال في فتح القدير : ولا فرق بين الأمة والعبد في هذا الحكم وإنما فرضها في الأمة ليرتب عليها المسألة التي تليها تفريعاً اهـ. وفي تلخيص الجامع : ولو زوج فضولي عبداً امرأتين ثم امرأتين ثم عتق يخير في اثنتين كيف شاء بخلاف ما لو باشر العبد حيث يخير في الآخريين لأنه رد في الأوليين كما أن الحر لو تزوج أربعاً ثم أربعاً ثم ثنتين بغير أمرهن توقف في الأخريين وارتد الباقي، ولو أجاز العبد النكاح في ثلاث بطل عقدهن لأن الجمع إجازة كالجمع حالة العقد ويخير في الرابعة، وكذا لو زوج فضولي حراً له امرأة أربعاً في عقود فماتت امرأته لا يخير إلا في الثلاث، وإن كان في عقد يلغو كما لو زوجه أختها أو تزوج مكاتبته ثم عتقت وإنما يوقف ماله مجيز حالة العقد ا هـ . وقيد بالنكاح لأنها لو اشترت شيئاً فأعتقها المولى لا ينفذ الشراء بل يبطل لأنه لو نفذ عليها لتغير المالك. وقيد بالرقيق لأن الصبي إذا تزوج بغير إذن وليه ثم بلغ فإنه لا ينفذ بل يتوقف على إجازته لأنه لم يكن أهلاً له أصلاً فلم يكن نافذاً من جهته، ولأن الولي الأبعد إذا زوج مع وجود الأقرب ثم غاب الأقرب أومات فتحولت الولاية إلى المزوج فإنه يتوقف على إجازة مستأنفة منه وإن زال المانع لأن الأبعد حين باشر لم يكن ولياً، ومن لم يكن ولياً في شيء لا يبالي بعواقبه اتكالاً على رأي الأقرب فيتوقف على إجازته ليتمكن من الأصلح، فليس هو من باب زوال المانع لأن له ولاية جديدة، ولأن المولى إذا زوج مكاتبته الصغيرة حتى توقف على إجازتها ثم أدت المال قبل الإجازة فعتقت فإنه لا ينفذ ذلك العقد، بل لا بد من إجازة المولى وإن كان هو العاقد لأنه لم يكن ولياً حين العقد فلا يبالي بعواقبه وفيه ما قدمناه من البحث . وقيد بالعتق لأنه لو تزوج العبد بلا إذن ثم أذن له فإنه لا ينفذ إلا بإجازة المولى
His saying: (And if she marries without permission then is emancipated, it takes effect without option) i.e., the slave-girl marries without the master's permission then is emancipated, so that marriage takes effect from her side because she is qualified to contract and the impediment to enforcement was the master's right which has ceased, and she has no option because the enforcement is after emancipation so no increase in ownership occurs, just as if she married herself after emancipation. Hence al-Asbījābī said: The principle is that a marriage contract whenever it is concluded upon a woman while she is a slave, she has the option of emancipation, and whenever it is concluded upon her while she is free, she does not have the option of emancipation. End quote. And if they coincide, she has no option, as if a third party married her and another third party emancipated her and the master approved everything, then she has no option. Thus in Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi‘. He made an absolute statement regarding the slave-girl, so it includes the qinn, the mudabbara, the umm walad, and the mukātaba, but there is detail concerning the mudabbara and the umm walad. As for the mudabbara, if the master emancipates her during his lifetime, the ruling is like that of a qinn if emancipated. But if she is emancipated by the master's death, then in al-Ẓahīriyya: If a mudabbara married without her master's permission then the master died and she comes out of one third, the marriage is valid; if she does not come out, it is not valid until she pays the sā‘ya according to Abū Ḥanīfa, while according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) it is valid. End quote. As for the umm walad, if she is emancipated or the master dies, the marriage does not take effect because the waiting period (idda) becomes obligatory upon her from the master at the time of emancipation, and the waiting period prevents the enforcement of the marriage. Thus in al-Muḥīṭ and al-Khāniyya. And it should be said in answering the issue that the marriage is invalid because it cannot be suspended with the existence of the waiting period, since marriage during another's waiting period is invalid. This is supported by what was added in al-Muḥīṭ in this issue: If the husband had intercourse with her before emancipation, the marriage takes effect. This is valid only according to the narration of Ibn Samā‘a from Muḥammad because the waiting period becomes obligatory from the husband, so it does not become obligatory from the master, but it is not valid according to the apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāya) because the waiting period does not become obligatory from the husband, so it becomes obligatory from the master, and the obligation of the waiting period from the master before permission causes the annulment of the marriage. End quote. So his statement “causes annulment” is clear regarding it. The author restricted (the ruling) to the slave-girl, although the ruling for a male slave is that if he marries without permission then is emancipated, the marriage takes effect because the impediment is removed in both, in order to clarify the negation of the option. Hence it was said in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: There is no difference between the slave-girl and the male slave in this ruling; he only presented it with the slave-girl to base upon it the following issue as a branch. End quote. In Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi‘: If a third party married a male slave to two women (in one contract) then to two women (in another contract) then he is emancipated, he may choose which two he wishes (to confirm), contrary to if the slave himself had married, where he chooses the last two because the first two are rejected, just as if a free man married four then another four then two without their permission, he suspends the last two and the rest are rejected. If the slave confirms the marriage to three, their contracts become void because the collective confirmation is like a collective contract, and he chooses the fourth. Similarly, if a third party married a free man who has one wife to four women in separate contracts, then his wife dies, he does not choose except among the three. And if there is a void contract, like marrying her sister, or he marries his mukātaba then she is emancipated, then his wealth is suspended as if he were a confirming party at the time of the contract. End quote. He restricted (the ruling) to marriage because if she bought something then her master emancipated her, the purchase does not take effect but is void because it would change the owner. He restricted it to the slave because if a minor marries without the permission of his guardian then reaches puberty, it does not take effect but is suspended on his permission because he was not originally qualified, so it was not effective from his side. And because if a distant guardian marries (the minor) in the presence of a nearer guardian, then the nearer guardian disappears or dies, and the guardianship transfers to the one who married, then it is suspended on a fresh permission from him even though the impediment has ceased, because the distant one at the time of contract was not a guardian, and whoever was not a guardian in a matter does not care about consequences relying on the nearer guardian's opinion, so it is suspended on his permission to achieve the best interest. So this is not from the category of removal of impediment because he has a new guardianship. And because if the master marries his minor mukātaba so that it is suspended on her permission, then she pays the money before permission and becomes free, that contract does not take effect; rather the master's permission is necessary even though he is the contractor, because he was not a guardian at the time of the contract, so he does not care about consequences. And in it is the investigation we mentioned earlier. And he restricted (the ruling) to emancipation because if a male slave marries without permission then the master gives permission, it does not take effect except with the master's permission.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq is an expansion (sharh) of the matn of Kanz al-Daqā’iq. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains the ruling: if a female slave marries without her master's permission and then is emancipated, the marriage becomes effective (nāfidh) from her side, and she does not have the option of annulment (khiyār al-‘itq). The reason is that at the time of contract, the slave was qualified (ahl al-‘ibāra) but the contract's enforcement was blocked by the master's right. Once emancipation removes that right, the contract takes effect. There is no khiyār because the contract was already completed; emancipation merely removes the impediment, so no new increase in ownership occurs. This is contrasted with the case where the slave was free at the time of marriage—then she would have the option. The author then details sub-categories: the ruling applies to a qinn (full slave), but for a mudabbara and umm walad there are details. For a mudabbara emancipated by death, if she comes out of one third (i.e., the master has enough estate), the marriage is valid; if not, according to Abū Ḥanīfa she must pay sā‘ya (compensation to heirs) for it to be valid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is valid without payment. For an umm walad, the marriage does not take effect because she must observe an ‘idda (waiting period) from her master upon his death, and marriage during another's ‘idda is invalid. The author then discusses a variant narration from Ibn Samā‘a and notes that according to the ẓāhir al-riwāya, the marriage is annulled. Next, the author explains why the matn specified the slave-girl: the same ruling applies to a male slave, but the author wanted to set up the next issue. The passage also includes comparisons with minor's marriage and third-party guardianship, emphasizing that the removal of impediment only applies when the initial contract was valid in itself but merely obstructed by a right. The lesson ultimately affirms that emancipation after a marriage without permission makes the marriage effective without option, subject to specific categories and narrations.

Key Terms
خيار العتق (khiyār al-‘itq): The option of a slave who is emancipated while married to annul the marriage. The text states that if the marriage was contracted while she was a slave and then she is freed, she has this option; but if the marriage was contracted already after emancipation (i.e., as a free woman), she does not.
مدبرة (mudabbara): A female slave who has been promised emancipation upon her master's death (tadbīr). The text discusses her case separately: if emancipated by death, the validity of her marriage without permission depends on whether she comes out of one third of the estate.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master. Upon master's death, she becomes free and must observe an 'idda from him. The text rules that her marriage contracted without permission does not take effect due to this 'idda.
سعاية (sā‘ya): Payment made by a slave to the master or heirs to compensate for freedom or partially secure freedom. In this context, the mudabbara who does not come out of one third must pay sā‘ya for the marriage to be valid according to Abū Ḥanīfa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the marriage of a female slave who marries without her master's permission and is then emancipated?
A) The marriage is void ab initio.
B) The marriage becomes effective upon emancipation without any option to annul.
C) The marriage is suspended and she has the option to annul upon emancipation.
D) The marriage is valid only if the master approved before emancipation.

Key Takeaway
A slave's marriage contracted without permission becomes effective upon emancipation without an option to annul, except where an 'idda prevents it (umm walad) or where sā‘ya is required (mudabbara per Abū Ḥanīfa).

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
10 min
Ruling on Marriage of a Slave Without Permission Followed by Manumission
Explains effect of manumission and ownership transfer on suspended marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's unauthorized marriage upon manumission and explain when the contract becomes valid or invalid based on the status of the slave and the new owner.

Core Idea
An unauthorized slave marriage is suspended; manumission alone doesn't validate it – explicit authorization is needed, and new ownership can void it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the effect of manumission and ownership transfer on the unauthorized marriage of a slave. It distinguishes between male and female slaves, and between consummated and unconsummated marriages. The Hanafi school's relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that if a female slave is transferred to a new master who has the right to intercourse, the marriage becomes invalid unless consummated prior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling: the marriage is suspended (mawquf)
When a slave marries without the master's permission, the contract is valid in essence but its effects are suspended pending authorization (ijazah) by the master or, after manumission, by the former slave.
This principle is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools may consider it invalid (batil) outright.
2
Distinguish between male and female slaves
For a male slave, manumission alone makes the marriage effective without new authorization. For a female slave, ownership transfer to a new master may invalidate the contract if the new master has the right to sexual intercourse.
The text states 'there is no difference between a female slave and a male slave' in some contexts, but the issue of the new master's right creates a distinction.
3
Understand the role of consummation
If the female slave's marriage was consummated before manumission, it may affect whether 'iddah is established, which in turn affects the new master's right. According to Ibn Sama'a, the 'iddah from the husband prevents the new master from claiming the slave, so authorization is valid. According to Zahir al-Riwayah, 'iddah only arises after separation, so the new master's right invalidates the contract.
The phrase 'فإن دخل بها الزوج قبل العتق نفذ النكاح' from al-Muhit is the evidence discussed.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The conflict is between the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad and the Zahir al-Riwayah (from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad). Zahir al-Riwayah is the mufta bihi position, as strengthened by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi.
Intra-Hanafi disputes like this require knowledge of the hierarchy of narrations; Zahir al-Riwayah takes precedence over nawadir.
5
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position is that if the new master has the right to intercourse and the marriage was not consummated, the authorization is invalid and the contract becomes void, even if consummated later.
This is the position in al-Muhit and Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid.

. وقيد بالنكاح لأنها لو اشترت شيئاً فأعتقها المولى لا ينفذ الشراء بل يبطل لأنه لو نفذ عليها لتغير المالك. وقيد بالرقيق لأن الصبي إذا تزوج بغير إذن وليه ثم بلغ فإنه لا ينفذ بل يتوقف على إجازته لأنه لم يكن أهلاً له أصلاً فلم يكن نافذاً من جهته، ولأن الولي الأبعد إذا زوج مع وجود الأقرب ثم غاب الأقرب أومات فتحولت الولاية إلى المزوج فإنه يتوقف على إجازة مستأنفة منه وإن زال المانع لأن الأبعد حين باشر لم يكن ولياً، ومن لم يكن ولياً في شيء لا يبالي بعواقبه اتكالاً على رأي الأقرب فيتوقف على إجازته ليتمكن من الأصلح، فليس هو من باب زوال المانع لأن له ولاية جديدة، ولأن المولى إذا زوج مكاتبته الصغيرة حتى بات على موقوف فأبطله، وإن كان قد دخل بها الزوج ففي رواية ابن سماعة عن تصح الإجازة لوجوب العدة عليها بهذا الدخول فلا يحل فرجها للمشتري فتصح إجازة المشتري . وجزم به قاضیخان في فتاواه، وظاهر الرواية أنه لا تصح الإجازة كما في المحيط وهو المذكور في كافي الحاكم الشهيد وقواه شمس الأئمة السرخسي بأن وجوب العدة إنما يكون بعد التفريق بينهما، فأما قبل التفريق فهي ليست بمعتدة فاعتراض الملك الثاني يبطل الملك والجد كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وقيد بكون التوقف لأجل المولى لأن المولى لو زوج أمته الكبيرة رجلاً برضاها وقبل عن الزوج فضولي ثم أعتقت قبل إجازة الزوج فإن لها النقض، ولو نقض المولى قالوا لا يصح، فإن أجاز الرجل قبل النقض فلا خيار لها والمهر لها ولو كان زوجها بغير رضاها فلها الرد وإن أجاز الزوج، وتمامه في المحيط.
The answer to the question: the marriage becomes invalid because it cannot be suspended with the existence of 'iddah, since marriage during the 'iddah of another is invalid. And it is indicated by what was added in al-Muhit on this issue: if the husband had intercourse with her before manumission, the marriage is effective. This is only valid according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, because 'iddah became obligatory from the husband, so 'iddah from the master is not obligatory. But it is not valid according to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), because 'iddah is not obligatory from the husband, so 'iddah becomes obligatory from the master, and its obligation from the master before authorization necessitates the dissolution of the marriage. So his statement 'necessitates dissolution' is clear in it. The author only specified a female slave even though the ruling for a male slave is that if he marries without permission and then is freed, the marriage becomes effective due to the removal of the impediment, in order to clarify the negation of option. Therefore, it was said in Fath al-Qadir: there is no difference between a female slave and a male slave in this ruling; he only presented it for the female slave to arrange the subsequent issue as a branch. And in Talkhis al-Jami': if a guardian marries a slave to two women, then to two women, then the slave is freed, he may choose two whichever he wishes, unlike if the slave himself had acted, where he chooses the latter two because he rejected the first two, just as a free man who married four, then four, then two without their permission, the latter two are suspended and the rest are rejected. And if the slave authorizes marriage to three, his contracts become invalid because collective authorization is like collective contracting, and he chooses among the fourth. Similarly, if a guardian marries a free man who has a wife to four women in separate contracts, then his wife dies, he only chooses among the three; and if it is a single contract, it is invalid, like marrying her sister, or marrying his mukatibah then she is freed. Only the one who authorized at the time of the contract has a suspended right. He restricted it to marriage because if she bought something and then the master freed her, the purchase is not effective but becomes invalid, because if it were effective for her, ownership would change. He restricted it to a slave because if a minor marries without his guardian's permission and then reaches puberty, it is not effective but is suspended on his permission, because he was not qualified at all, so it was not effective from him. And because if a distant guardian marries in the presence of a nearer one, then the nearer one disappears or dies, and guardianship transfers to the one who performed the marriage, it is suspended on a new permission from him even though the impediment is removed, because the distant one at the time of acting was not a guardian, and one who is not a guardian in something does not consider its consequences, relying on the opinion of the nearer, so it is suspended on his permission to enable the best choice. This is not a case of removal of impediment because he has a new guardianship. And because if a master marries his young mukatibah, it is suspended on her permission, then she pays the money before permission and is freed, that contract does not become effective; rather the master's permission is necessary even though he was the contractor, because he was not a guardian at the time of the contract, so he does not consider consequences. And in it is the research we have presented. He restricted it to manumission because if a slave marries without permission and then the master gives permission, it is not effective except with the permission of the master or the slave, and we have presented it. And because if ownership transfers to someone other than the master, like a buyer, donee, or heir, the authorization transfers to the second owner. The contract does not become invalid if the one who married without permission is a male slave. But if it is a female slave, then if the second owner is not allowed to have intercourse with her, it becomes effective with his authorization. If he is allowed to have intercourse with her, then if the husband had not had intercourse with her, the authorization is not valid and the suspended contract becomes invalid, because a lawful right suddenly arises upon a suspended contract and invalidates it. If the husband had had intercourse with her, then according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, the authorization is valid because 'iddah becomes obligatory upon her due to this intercourse, so her private part becomes unlawful for the buyer, so the buyer's authorization is valid. Qadikhan affirmed this in his Fatawa. But the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that the authorization is not valid, as in al-Muhit, and it is mentioned in Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi strengthened it by stating that the obligation of 'iddah only occurs after separation; before separation she is not in 'iddah, so the second owner's objection invalidates the suspended ownership even though he is prohibited from having intercourse with her, and we have already mentioned this. And what is apparent in al-Muhit is that there is no 'iddah in a suspended marriage after intercourse at all, as we have mentioned. The author intended by 'female slave' the adult female slave, because if she were a minor and married without the master's permission and then he freed her, that contract is not effective and is invalid according to Zufar; with us, it is suspended on the master's permission if she has no other guardian besides him. If she has a guardian other than the master, then if he authorizes, it is valid. When she attains puberty, she has the option of puberty with respect to anyone other than the father and grandfather, as in Sharh al-Tahawi. He restricted the suspension to the master because if the master marries his adult slave woman to a man with her consent, and the acceptance from the husband is by a guardian, then she is freed before the husband's authorization, she has the right to rescind. If the master rescinds, they said it is not valid. If the man authorizes before rescission, she has no option and the mahr is for her. If he married her without her consent, she has the right to reject even if the husband authorizes. The rest is in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the effect of manumission on a slave's unauthorized marriage. The author addresses various scenarios: a male slave vs. female slave, whether the marriage was consummated, and whether ownership transfers to a new master. The default ruling is that the marriage is suspended (mawquf) and becomes valid upon the slave's authorization after manumission. However, if the slave is female and the new master has the right to sexual intercourse, the marriage is invalidated if not consummated. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding consummation: according to Ibn Sama'a, the 'iddah from the husband prevents the new master from claiming the wife, so authorization is valid; but according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the 'iddah is not yet established, so the new master's right invalidates the marriage. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the apparent narration, that authorization is not valid, as affirmed by Al-Sarakhsi and others. The passage also distinguishes between cases involving a minor slave, a guardian, and a mukatibah, all following the principle that lack of authority at the time of contract requires fresh authorization.

Key Terms
النكاح الموقوف (al-nikah al-mawquf): A marriage contract that is valid in its essence but its legal effects are suspended pending a condition, such as the master's permission or the slave's manumission.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The authoritative and transmitted rulings from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani as compiled in the six core books of Imam Muhammad.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): The authorization or ratification of a suspended contract, either by the slave after manumission or by the new master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah position, what is the ruling if a female slave marries without her master's permission, is then sold to a new master who has the right to intercourse, and the marriage was not consummated?
A) The marriage is valid and the new master's authorization is effective.
B) The marriage becomes invalid (batil) because the new master's right invalidates the suspended contract.
C) The marriage remains suspended until the husband gives permission.
D) The marriage is automatically valid because the impediment is removed.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a slave's unauthorized marriage after manumission depends on the type of slave, whether the marriage was consummated, and the new owner's rights; the Hanafi mufta bihi position is that the contract becomes void if the new owner can have intercourse with the female slave and the marriage was not consummated.

Nikah — Marriage

One Mahr, Not Two: Istihsan in Slave Woman's Marriage
Determines mahr recipient via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr recipient when a slave woman is manumitted after consummation and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
When a slave woman's marriage becomes effective after manumission, only one mahr is due—to the master if intercourse preceded manumission.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a slave woman marries without her master's permission, the husband consummates, then she is manumitted and the master ratifies the marriage. The question: who gets the mahr? Qiyas might suggest two mahr payments, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan to preserve the unity of the marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Note the timing of intercourse relative to manumission: before (the first case) or after (the second). The ruling depends on this timing.
The husband's act of intercourse is the same, but the legal status of the woman changes.
2
State the Ruling for Each Scenario
If intercourse occurs before manumission, the mahr belongs to the master. If after, it belongs to the woman. Memorize this distinction.
The master is entitled because he owned the benefits when they were used.
3
Understand the Qiyas That Would Demand Two Mahr
Analogy (qiyas) would consider each act of intercourse as generating a separate obligation: one for the semblance of marriage before manumission, one for the valid marriage after. That would require two mahr.
This is the logical conclusion if we treat each act independently.
4
Grasp the Istihsan Reasoning
Juristic preference (istihsan) rejects two mahr because the obligation is tied to the contract, not to individual acts. One contract cannot produce two mahr. The authorization (ijazah) relates back to the original contract.
Istihsan overrides qiyas when strict analogy leads to inconsistency with the general principle that mahr is countervalue for the entire marriage.
5
Note the Additional Clarification on Non-Division
The mahr is not divided between master and woman because the recipient differs for the same set of acts; division is impossible when the entitled party changes during the marriage.
This reinforces the unity of the contract and the mahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فلو وطئ قبله فالمهر له وإلا فلها) أي لو وطئ زوج الأمة التي نكحت بغير إذن قبل العتق ثم نفذ بالعتق فالمهر للمولى وإن وطئها بعد العتق فالمهر لها لأنه في الأول استوفى منافع مملوكة للمولى وفي الثاني لها وفي القياس يجب عليه مهران : مهر بالدخول لشبهة النكاح قبل العتق ومهر لها لنفوذ العقد عليها بعد العتق، ولكنا استحسنا وقلنا لا يجب إلا مهر واحد للمولى لأن وجوبه إنما يكون باعتبار العقد، والعقد الواحد لا يوجب إلا مهراً واحداً، وإذا وجب به المهر للمولى لا يجب لها به مهر آخر، يوضحه أن الإجازة وإن كانت بعد العتق فحكمها يستند إلى أصل العقد كذا في المبسوط. وإنما لم يقسم المهر ههنا بين المولى وبينها كما قال الإمام في مسألة حبس المرأة نفسها بعد الدخول برضاها حتى يوفيها مهرها معللاً بأن المهر مقابل بالكل أي بجميع وطات توجد في النكاح لا يخلو الوطء عن المهر لأن قسمته على جميع الوطات إذا لم يختلف المستحق لأن الجهالة لا تضر فيه وأما إذا اختلف المستحق كما في هذه المسألة فلا يمكن قسمته
His statement: (If he has intercourse before it, then the mahr is for him; otherwise, it is for her.) That is, if the husband of the slave woman who married without permission has intercourse with her before manumission, then the marriage becomes effective by manumission, the mahr is for the master. If he has intercourse with her after manumission, the mahr is for her, because in the first case he utilized benefits belonging to the master, and in the second case, for her. In analogy, two mahr would be obligatory: one mahr for the entry due to the semblance of marriage before manumission, and one mahr for her due to the validity of the contract upon her after manumission. But we prefer by istihsan and say: only one mahr is obligatory for the master, because its obligation is based on the contract, and one contract does not oblige more than one mahr. When the mahr is thus obliged for the master, no other mahr is obliged for her. This is clarified by the fact that authorization, even if after manumission, its ruling is attributed to the original contract, as in al-Mabsut. And the mahr is not divided here between the master and her, as the Imam said in the case of a woman detaining herself after intercourse with her consent until he pays her mahr, reasoning that the mahr is a countervalue for the whole, i.e., for all acts of intercourse that occur in the marriage; no act of intercourse is devoid of mahr. Because its division over all acts of intercourse is possible if the recipient is the same, since ignorance does not harm; but if the recipients differ, as in this issue, division is not possible.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding the mahr in a case where a slave woman marries without permission, is later manumitted, and the marriage becomes effective. The matn states: if the husband has intercourse before manumission, the mahr is for the master; if after, it is for her. The commentary explains that analogy (qiyas) would require two mahr payments—one for the intercourse before manumission (considered shubhah, resembling marriage) and one for the valid marriage after. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), only one mahr is due, and it goes to the master if intercourse occurred before manumission, because the contract is one and cannot generate two mahr obligations. The master is entitled to it because the husband enjoyed the slave woman's services while she was still his property. If intercourse occurred after manumission, the mahr goes to the woman because she is now free and the master has no claim. The passage further clarifies why the mahr is not apportioned between the master and the woman: since the recipient differs for the same set of acts (intercourse), allocation is not possible. This is based on the general principle that mahr is countervalue for the entire marital relationship, not for individual instances of intercourse.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The bridal gift (dower) obligatory upon marriage; in this context, it is due to the master if consummation occurred before the slave's manumission, otherwise to the woman.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here it would require two mahr payments, but istihsan overrides.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; the method by which the Hanafi school avoids the strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is only one mahr due even though intercourse occurred both before and after manumission?
A) Because the slave woman is not entitled to mahr.
B) Because the mahr is a countervalue for the contract, not for individual acts of intercourse.
C) Because the master waived the second mahr.
D) Because the marriage was invalid before manumission.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a slave woman's marriage is ratified after manumission, only one mahr is due—to the master if consummation occurred before manumission, or to the woman if after—because the mahr is tied to the single marriage contract.

Mahr for the Emancipated Slave Woman: Istihsan Overriding Qiyas
Explains mahr distribution with istihsan overriding qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr distribution when a slave woman marries without permission and is later emancipated, and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides the qiyas argument for two mahr payments.

Core Idea
One contract yields one mahr; the recipient is the owner of the benefits at the time of first intercourse.

What happens to the mahr when a slave woman marries without her master's permission, the husband has intercourse, and then she is emancipated? Should the master receive the mahr, or the woman, or both? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq resolves the puzzle using the Hanafi principle of istihsan (juristic preference), setting aside the apparent qiyas that would require two mahr payments. Understanding this ruling clarifies how the Hanafi school balances textual analogy with equitable reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties
Recognize the key elements: a slave woman marries without permission, the husband has intercourse either before or after emancipation, and the marriage is ratified by emancipation.
The ratification (ijazah) relates back to the original contract.
2
Apply the qiyas argument
By strict analogy, two mahr payments would be due: one for the doubtful intercourse before emancipation (since the marriage was initially invalid) and one for the valid marriage after emancipation.
This is the default analogical reasoning that the Hanafis reject here.
3
Apply the istihsan and its reasoning
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to rule that only one mahr is obligatory. The mahr is obligated by the contract, not by individual acts of intercourse. A single contract cannot yield two mahr payments. Since the ratification relates back, the contract is valid from its inception.
The principle: 'العقد الواحد لا يوجب إلا مهراً واحداً' (a single contract does not obligate more than one mahr).
4
Determine the recipient based on timing of intercourse
If the husband had intercourse before emancipation, the entire mahr goes to the master because the benefits of the slave woman belonged to the master. If intercourse occurs after emancipation, the mahr goes to the woman because she now owns her benefits.
The mahr cannot be split because the entitled party differs; it goes entirely to the owner of the first instance of intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فلو وطئ قبله فالمهر له وإلا فلها أي لو وطئ زوج الأمة التي نكحت بغير إذن قبل العتق ثم نفذ بالعتق فالمهر للمولى وإن وطئها بعد العتق فالمهر لها لأنه في الأول استوفى منافع مملوكة للمولى وفي الثاني لها وفي القياس يجب عليه مهران : مهر بالدخول لشبهة النكاح قبل العتق ومهر لها لنفوذ العقد عليها بعد العتق، ولكنا استحسنا وقلنا لا يجب إلا مهر واحد للمولى لأن وجوبه إنما يكون باعتبار العقد، والعقد الواحد لا يوجب إلا مهراً واحداً، وإذا وجب به المهر للمولى لا يجب لها به مهر آخر، يوضحه أن الإجازة وإن كانت بعد العتق فحكمها يستند إلى أصل العقد كذا في المبسوط. وإنما لم يقسم المهر ههنا بين المولى وبينها كما قال الإمام في مسألة حبس المرأة نفسها بعد الدخول برضاها حتى يوفيها مهرها معللاً بأن المهر مقابل بالكل أي بجميع وطات توجد في النكاح لا يخلو الوطء عن المهر لأن قسمته على جميع الوطات إذا لم يختلف المستحق لأن الجهالة لا تضر فيه وأما إذا اختلف المستحق كما في هذه المسألة فلا يمكن قسمته حتى استحقه بتمامه من حصل الوطء الأول على ملكه، وبهذا اندفع ما ذكره في التبيين. وأراد المصنف بالمهر المهر المسمى لا مهر المثل. قال في الهداية والمراد بالمهر الألف المسمى لأن نفاذ العقد بالعتق استند إلى وقت وجود العتق فصحت التسمية ووجب المسمى. وفي فتح القدير : وقد يورد فيقال لو استند إلى أصل العقد يجب كون المهر للمولى كما لو تزوجت بإذن المولى ولم يدخل بها حتى أعتقها وهو بمعزل عن صورة المسألة فإنما النفاذ بالعتق وبه تملك منافعها بخلاف النفاذ بالإذن والرق قائم.
His saying: (If he had intercourse before it, then the mahr is for him, otherwise it is for her) meaning: if the husband of the slave woman who married without permission had intercourse before emancipation, then the marriage is executed by emancipation, so the mahr is for the master. If he had intercourse after emancipation, the mahr is for her, because in the first case he utilized the benefits of property belonging to the master, and in the second case it belongs to her. By analogy, two mahr payments would be obligatory: a mahr due to doubtful intercourse before emancipation, and a mahr for her due to the contract being executed after emancipation. But we applied istihsan and said only one mahr is obligatory, for the master, because its obligation is only on account of the contract, and a single contract does not obligate more than one mahr. Since the mahr is obligated for the master by it, another mahr is not obligated for her. This is clarified by the fact that the ratification, even if after emancipation, relates back to the original contract, as in al-Mabsut. The mahr is not divided here between the master and her, as the Imam stated in the issue of a woman detaining herself after intercourse with her consent until he pays her mahr, explaining that mahr is in exchange for the entire set — that is, for all instances of intercourse occurring in the marriage — so that no instance of intercourse is free from mahr. Because its division over all instances of intercourse is possible if the entitled party does not differ, since ignorance does not harm it. But if the entitled party differs, as in this issue, it cannot be divided, so it is entirely taken by the one to whom the first instance of intercourse occurred while it was his property. By this, what was mentioned in al-Tabyin is refuted. The author intended by 'mahr' the named dower, not the dower of the like. Al-Hidayah said: 'By mahr is meant the named thousand, because the execution of the contract by emancipation relates back to the time when emancipation existed, so the naming is valid and the named is obligatory.' In Fath al-Qadir: 'It may be objected: if it relates back to the original contract, the mahr should be for the master, as if she married with the master's permission and he did not have intercourse until she was emancipated, and he is far from the form of the issue. For execution is by emancipation, and by it she owns her benefits, unlike execution by permission while slavery remains.'
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with the situation of a slave woman who marries without her master's permission and is subsequently emancipated. The key ruling is that if the husband had intercourse before emancipation, the entire mahr (the named dower) goes to the master. If intercourse occurs after emancipation, the mahr goes to the woman herself. The commentator explains the reasoning: before emancipation, the woman's benefits belong to the master, so he is entitled to the mahr for the benefit (intercourse) that he owned. After emancipation, she owns her benefits, so the mahr is hers. Analogically (qiyas), one might think two mahr are due—one for the doubtful intercourse before emancipation and one for the valid marriage after—but the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to rule that only one mahr is due, because the mahr is obligated by the contract, not by individual instances of intercourse. The contract is one, so one mahr. The ratification (ijazah) of the marriage by emancipation relates back to the time of the contract. The commentator also distinguishes between the view of Imam Abu Hanifa and an objection from al-Tabyin, using the principle that mahr is an exchange for the entire marital relationship, not for each act of intercourse. Since the entitled party differs (master vs. woman), the mahr cannot be split; it goes entirely to the one who owned the first intercourse (the master). The author specifies that the mahr in question is the named dower (al-mahr al-musamma), not the dower of the like (mahr al-mithl), because the contract's execution by emancipation establishes the validity of the named amount.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife; here specifically the named dower (المسمى).
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference departing from strict analogy to achieve a better outcome.
العتق (al-‘itq): Emancipation of a slave, which here causes the suspended marriage contract to be executed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave woman marries without her master's permission. Her husband has intercourse before emancipation, then she is emancipated. According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, who receives the mahr?
A) The woman receives the entire mahr
B) The master receives the entire mahr
C) The mahr is split equally between master and woman
D) No mahr is due because the marriage was initially invalid

Key Takeaway
One contract yields one mahr; the recipient is determined by ownership of the woman's benefits at the time of first intercourse.

The Eight Options (Khiyarat) in Hanafi Marriage Law
Enumerates the five (or eight) marriage options in Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the five main and three additional marriage options (khiyarat) in Hanafi fiqh and state for each whether the right belongs to the wife, the husband, or the guardian.

Core Idea
Hanafi marriage law recognizes five core options and three related to physical defects; the right to annul belongs to specific parties.

Marriage is a binding contract, but Hanafi fiqh provides limited grounds for annulment through options (khiyarat). This lesson summarises the eight types listed by al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, distinguishing between options that belong to the woman, the slave, the wife, and the guardians. Understanding these options is essential for determining when a marriage can be dissolved without talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first five options
The text lists five main options: (1) khiyar al-mukhayyirah (option for a woman given choice by guardian), (2) khiyar al-‘itq (option upon emancipation), (3) khiyar al-bulugh (option upon reaching puberty for minors married by non-father/guardian), (4) khiyar al-nuqsan ‘an mahr al-mithl (option for deficient dower), (5) khiyar al-tazawwuj bi-ghayri kuf' (option for marrying a non-peer).
For options (4) and (5), the right belongs to the guardians, not the women.
2
Note the additional three options
The text adds three more: (6) khiyar al-‘innah (impotence of husband), (7) khiyar al-khisi (castration), (8) khiyar al-jabb (amputation of male organ). These are grounds for the wife to seek annulment.
These are defects that give the wife a right to dissolve the marriage.
3
Distinguish who holds each option
The first three (mukhayyirah, ‘itq, bulugh) belong to the woman (or slave). Options (4) and (5) belong to the guardians. The last three give the wife a right against the husband.
Khiyar al-bulugh does not apply if the minor was married by the father or grandfather.
4
Memorize the sequence
The text is a memorisation anchor. Learn the list: five primary (mukhayyirah, ‘itq, bulugh, nuqsan mahr, ghayr kuf') and three added (‘innah, khisi, jabb).
The order helps recall who holds each right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن حاصل الخيارات في النكاح خسمة : خيار المخيرة والعتق والبلوغ والنقصان عن مهر المثل والتزوج بغير كفء والخيار في الأخيرين للأولياء. ويزاد خيار العنة والخصي والجب.
Then know that the summary of options in marriage is five: the option of the woman given choice, emancipation, puberty, deficiency from dower of the like, and marrying without a peer (kuf'). The option in the last two is for the guardians. And added is the option of impotence, castration, and amputation of the penis.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a concise summary of the khiyarat (options) recognized in Hanafi marriage law. The author states that there are five main options: (1) khiyar al-mukhayyirah: the option for a woman who has been given a choice by her guardian at the time of marriage; (2) khiyar al-‘itq: the option upon emancipation (e.g., a slave woman who is emancipated after marriage may choose to continue or annul); (3) khiyar al-bulugh: the option upon reaching puberty for minors married by guardians other than father or grandfather; (4) khiyar al-nuqsan ‘an mahr al-mithl: the option for the wife if her dower is less than her proper dower; (5) khiyar al-tazawwuj bi-ghayri kuf': the option for the guardian if the woman marries someone not her peer. For the last two, the right belongs to the guardians, not the woman. Additionally, three more options are added: khiyar al-‘innah (impotence of husband), khiyar al-khisi (castration), and khiyar al-jabb (amputation of the penis), which allow the wife to seek annulment. This is a summary meant for memorization.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option or right to annul or continue a marriage contract under specific grounds.
المخيرة (al-mukhayyirah): A woman given the choice at marriage to continue or annul.
الكفء (al-kuf'): Equality or compatibility in marriage, especially in lineage, religion, and profession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Khiyar al-‘itq
B) Khiyar al-bulugh
C) Khiyar al-nuqsan ‘an mahr al-mithl
D) Khiyar al-‘innah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes eight marriage options: five primary (mukhayyirah, ‘itq, bulugh, nuqsan mahr, ghayr kuf') and three added for physical defects; the holder differs by option.
